The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Why the Minor Must Be Affirmative
Why negative minor fails with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a negative minor premise invalidates a first-figure syllogism and how logicians responded to an objection about dependency.

Core Idea
A negative minor separates the minor and middle terms, making inference to the major impossible.

The second condition for first figure productivity is that the minor premise must be affirmative. Why? Because a negative minor indicates that the minor and middle terms are entirely or partially separate, so no judgment about the major term can be transferred. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the theoretical objection it raised.

Guided Path
1
Understand the consequence of a negative minor
The author says: 'if its minor premise were negative, then from affirming the major of the middle and from denying it of it, neither affirming it of the minor nor denying it of it would follow.' This is because the negative minor indicates separation (tabayun).
The minor and middle are separate; judging one says nothing about the other.
2
Examine the example given
The example: 'No human is a stone; every stone is a body.' This syllogism has a negative minor. If one tries to conclude 'No human is a body', that is false. If one concludes 'Every human is a body', that is not entailed. So the form alone is insufficient.
The form is identical to a valid one, but the material leads to instability.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objection is: if the minor is negative, why can't we know the conclusion? The response distinguishes between knowing the judgment in terms of the middle term vs. the minor term. The dependency on the middle is allowed.
The passage says: 'the prohibited dependency is the affirmation of the major of the essence of the minor inasmuch as it is an individual of the minor, while the dependency we hold is inasmuch as it is an individual of the middle.'
4
Formulate the logical rule
For a valid first-figure syllogism, the minor must be affirmative. This is a necessary condition.
This condition eliminates 8 out of 16 possible moods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَمَّا كَانَ المُتَصَوَّرُ فِي كُلِّ شَكْلٍ سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ ضَرْباً كَمَا سَبَقَ، وَمِنْهَا مَا تَلْزَمُ عَنْهُ النَّتِيجَةُ وَيُسَمَّى «مُنْتِجاً»، وَمَا لَا تَلْزَمُ عَنْهُ وَيُسَمَّى «عَقِيماً»، اُحْتِيجَ إِلَى شُرُوطِ الْإِنْتَاجِ لِيُعْلَمَ أَنَّ مَا تَوَفَّرَتْ فِيهِ مِنَ الضُّرُوبِ مُنْتِجٌ، وَمَا تَخَلَّفَتْ فِيهِ أَوْ بَعْضُهَا عَقِيمٌ. فَأَمَّا الشَّكْلُ الْأَوَّلُ فَلِإِنْتَاجِهِ شَرْطَانِ: (1) أَحَدُهُمَا بِاعْتِبَارِ الْكَيْفِ، وَهُوَ إِيجَابُ صُغْرَاهُ. (2) وَالثَّانِي بِاعْتِبَارِ الْكَمِّ، وَهُوَ كُلِّيَّةُ كُبْرَاهُ. إِذْ بِمَجْمُوعِ الشَّرْطَيْنِ يَتَعَدَّى حُكْمُ الْأَكْبَرِ إِلَى الْأَصْغَرِ، فَيَتَحَقَّقُ الْإِنْتَاجُ. فَإِنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَتْ صُغْرَاهُ سَالِبَةً لَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ إِثْبَاتِ الْأَكْبَرِ لِلْأَوْسَطِ، وَمِنْ سَلْبِهِ عَنْهُ إِثْبَاتُهُ لِلْأَصْغَرِ وَلَا سَلْبُهُ عَنْهُ؛ لِأَنَّ الصُّغْرَى السَّالِبَةَ تَدُلُّ عَلَى تَبَايُنِ الْأَصْغَرِ وَالْأَوْسَطِ كُلِّيًّا أَوْ جُزْئِيًّا، وَالْحُكْمُ عَلَى أَحَدِ الْمُتَبَايَنَيْنِ بِإِيجَابٍ أَوْ سَلْبٍ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ الْحُكْمُ عَلَى الْآخَرِ. كَقَوْلِنَا: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ، وَكُلُّ حَجَرٍ جِسْمٌ» فَلَا يُنْتِجُ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِجِسْمٍ»؛ لِأَنَّ الْحَقَّ الْإِيْجَابُ. وَلَوْ قُلْتَ بَدَلَ الْكُبْرَى: «وَكُلُّ حَجَرٍ جَمَادٌ» لَكَانَ الْحَقُّ السَّلْبَ. فَصُورَةُ الْقِيَاسِ وَاحِدَةٌ، وَالنَّتِيجَةُ مُضْطَرِبَةٌ؛ تَارَةً يَكُونُ الْحَقُّ الْإِيجَابَ، وَتَارَةً السَّلْبَ، وَمَا ذَاكَ إِلَّا لِخُصُوصِ الْمَوَادِّ. وَأَجَابُوا: بِأَنَّ الْحُكْمَ يَخْتَلِفُ عِلْماً وَجَهْلاً بِحَسَبِ اخْتِلَافِ الْعِبَارَةِ عَنِ الْمَوْضُوعِ، فَيَكُونُ مَجْهُولاً مِنْ حَيْثُ التَّعْبِيرُ عَنْهُ بِلَفْظِ الْأَصْغَرِ، وَمَعْلُوماً مِنْ حَيْثُ التَّعْبِيرُ عَنْهُ بِلَفْظِ الْأَوْسَطِ، وَلَا امْتِنَاعَ فِي تَوَقُّفِ الْأَوَّلِ عَلَى الثَّانِي. فَالتَّوَقُّفُ الْمَمْنُوعُ ثُبُوتُ الْأَكْبَرِ لِذَاتِ الْأَصْغَرِ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا مِنْ أَفْرَادِ الْأَصْغَرِ، وَالْمُتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ عِنْدَنَا ثُبُوتُ الْأَكْبَرِ لِذَاتِ الْأَصْغَرِ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا مِنْ أَفْرَادِ الْأَوْسَطِ. قَوْلُهُ: (سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ ضَرْباً) أَيْ مِنْ ضَرْبِ الْمَحْصُورَاتِ الْأَرْبَعِ صُغْرِيَّاتٍ فِي الْمَحْصُورَاتِ الْأَرْبَعِ كُبْرِيَّاتٍ. وَلَمْ يَعْتَبِرُوا فِي عَدَدِ الضُّرُوبِ الْمُهْمَلَةَ؛ لِأَنَّهَا فِي قُوَّةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ، وَلَا الشَّخْصِيَّةَ؛ لِأَنَّهَا فِي حُكْمِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ؛ لِانْحِصَارِ الْمَحْكُومِ عَلَيْهِ فِيهِمَا. الْمُنْتَجُ مِنْ هَذِهِ الضُّرُوبِ لِلشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ أَرْبَعَةٌ، أَشَارَ لَهَا صَاحِبُ «الْقَادِرِيَّةِ» بِقَوْلِهِ: كُلٌّ فَكُلٌّ، أَوْ فَلَا شَيْءَ، كَذَا بَعْضٌ فَكُلٌّ، أَوْ فَلَا شَيْءَ خُذَا. وَأَمَّا صُورَةُ الْقِيَاسِ فَلَمْ تَسْتَلْزِمْ شَيْئاً؛ لِأَنَّ اللَّازِمَ لَا يَتَخَلَّفُ. وَلَوْ كَانَتْ كُبْرَاهُ جُزْئِيَّةً، لَجَازَ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْبَعْضُ الْمَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا غَيْرَ الْأَصْغَرِ، فَلَا يَتَعَدَّى حُكْمُهَا مِنْ إِيجَابٍ أَوْ سَلْبٍ إِلَيْهِ. فَلَوْ قُلْتَ: «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَبَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ فَرَسٌ» لَكَانَ الْحَقُّ السَّلْبَ، وَلَوْ جَعَلْتَ بَدَلَ الْكُبْرَى: «وَبَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ» لَكَانَ الْحَقُّ الْإِيجَابَ. فَضُرُوبُهُ الْمُنْتِجَةُ بِمُقْتَضَى الشَّرْطَيْنِ أَرْبَعَةٌ؛ لِأَنَّ إِيجَابَ الصُّغْرَى يَثْبُتُ لَهَا كُلِّيَّةٌ وَجُزْئِيَّةٌ، وَكُلِّيَّةَ الْكُبْرَى تَثْبُتُ لَهَا مُوجَبَةٌ وَسَالِبَةٌ، فَتُضْرَبُ حَالَتَيِ الصُّغْرَى فِي حَالَتَيِ الْكُبْرَى بِأَرْبَعَةٍ. وَبَقِيَتْ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ اثْنَا عَشَرَ كُلُّهَا عَقِيمَةٌ. هَذَا طَرِيقُ التَّحْصِيلِ، وَهُوَ أَنْ يَحْصُلَ الْمُنْتِجُ، ثُمَّ يُقَالُ: مَا سِوَاهُ عَقِيمٌ. وَأَمَّا طَرِيقُ الْإِسْقَاطِ، وَهُوَ أَنْ يُبَيَّنَ أَوَّلاً الْعَقِيمُ الَّذِي أَسْقَطَتْهُ الشُّرُوطُ، ثُمَّ يُقَالُ: وَالْبَاقِي مُنْتِجٌ. فَإِنْ تَقُلْ: شَرْطُ إِيجَابِ الصُّغْرَى يُسْقِطْ ثَمَانَيَةَ أَضْرُبٍ، مِنْ ضَرْبِ حَالَتَيِ السَّالِبَةِ الصُّغْرَى كُلِّيَّةٌ وَجُزْئِيَّةٌ فِي الْمَحْصُورَاتِ الْأَرْبَعِ كُبْرِيَّاتٍ، وَشَرْطُ كُلِّيَّةِ الْكُبْرَى يُسْقِطْ أَرْبَعَةً أُخْرَى، مِنْ ضَرْبِ حَالَتَيِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ الْكُبْرَى مُوجَبَةٌ وَسَالِبَةٌ فِي الْمُوجَبَتَيْنِ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ بِأَرْبَعَةٍ، فَهَذِهِ اثْنَا عَشَرَ ضَرْباً كُلُّهَا عَقِيمَةٌ، وَتَبْقَى أَرْبَعَةٌ مُنْتِجَةٌ كَمَا سَبَقَ، وَإِلَيْهَا أَشَارَ النَّاظِمُ بَعْدَ هَذَا بِقَوْلِهِ: «فَمُنْتِجٌ لِأَوَّلٍ أَرْبَعَةُ». لِنُبَيِّنَهَا مَعَ نَتَائِجِهَا فَنَقُولُ: (1) الضَّرْبُ الْأَوَّلُ: مِنْ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ، يُنْتِجُ كُلِّيَّةً مُوجَبَةً. مِثَالُهُ مِنَ الْعَقْلِيَّاتِ: «كُلُّ جِرْمٍ مُلَازِمٌ لِلْعَرَضِ، وَكُلُّ مُلَازِمٍ لِلْعَرَضِ حَادِثٌ» يُنْتِجُ: «كُلُّ جِرْمٍ حَادِثٌ». وَمِنَ الْفِقْهِيَّاتِ: «كُلُّ بُرٍّ مُقْتَاتٌ مُدَّخَرٌ، وَكُلُّ مُقْتَاتٍ مُدَّخَرٍ رِبَوِيٌّ» يُنْتِجُ: «كُلُّ بُرٍّ رِبَوِيٌّ». وَمِنَ النَّحَوِيَّاتِ: «كُلُّ فَاعِلٍ مَرْفُوعٌ، وَكُلُّ مَرْفُوعٍ عُمْدَةٌ» يُنْتِجُ: «كُلُّ فَاعِلٍ عُمْدَةٌ». (2) الضَّرْبُ الثَّانِي: مِنْ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ وَالْكُبْرَى سَالِبَةٌ، يُنْتِجُ كُلِّيَّةً سَالِبَةً. مِثَالُهُ مِنَ الْعَقْلِيَّاتِ: «كُلُّ جِرْمٍ حَادِثٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْحَادِثِ يُغْنِي عَنِ الْفَاعِلِ» يُنْتِجُ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْجِرْمِ يُغْنِي عَنِ الْفَاعِلِ».
Since what is conceived in every figure is sixteen moods as previously mentioned, and among them are those from which the conclusion follows, called 'productive', and those from which it does not, called 'sterile', there was a need for conditions of productivity so that it may be known which of the moods fulfill them are productive, and which lack them or some of them are sterile. As for the first figure, it has two conditions for its productivity: (1) One regarding quality, which is the affirmation of its minor premise. (2) The second regarding quantity, which is the universality of its major premise. For by the combination of the two conditions, the judgment of the major term extends to the minor term, thus productivity is realized. For if its minor premise were negative, then from affirming the major of the middle and from denying it of it, neither affirming it of the minor nor denying it of it would follow; because the negative minor indicates the separation of the minor and the middle, either wholly or partially, and judging one of the two separate things by affirmation or negation does not entail the judgment on the other. As in our saying: 'No human is a stone, and every stone is a body'—it does not yield: 'No human is a body'; because the truth is affirmation. And if you replaced the major with 'and every stone is inanimate', then the truth would be negation. So the form of the syllogism is one, yet the conclusion is unstable; sometimes the truth is affirmation, sometimes negation, and that is only due to the specific subject matter. And they answered: That judgment differs in knowledge and ignorance according to the difference of expression about the subject; so it is unknown in terms of being expressed by the term 'minor', and known in terms of being expressed by the term 'middle', and there is no impossibility in the first depending on the second. For the prohibited dependency is the affirmation of the major of the essence of the minor inasmuch as it is an individual of the minor, while the dependency we hold is the affirmation of the major of the essence of the minor inasmuch as it is an individual of the middle. His saying: (sixteen moods) i.e., from multiplying the four categorical propositions as minors by the four categorical propositions as majors. And they did not count among the number of moods the indefinite, because it is equivalent to the particular, nor the singular, because it is in the ruling of the universal, due to the restriction of the subject in both. The productive among these moods for the first figure are four, indicated by the author of 'al-Qadiriyya' in his saying: 'Universal, so universal, or none; likewise particular, so universal, or none, take.' As for the form of the syllogism, it does not necessitate anything; because the necessary does not fail. And if its major were particular, it would be possible that the part judged in it is other than the minor, so its judgment from affirmation or negation does not extend to it. So if you said: 'Every human is an animal, and some animal is a horse'—the truth would be negation; and if you made the major: 'and some animal is rational'—the truth would be affirmation. So its productive moods according to the two conditions are four; because affirmation of the minor admits of universal and particular, and universality of the major admits of affirmative and negative, so the two states of the minor are multiplied by the two states of the major, giving four. And twelve of its moods remain, all sterile. This is the method of verification, which is to obtain the productive, then say: what is other than it is sterile. As for the method of elimination, which is to first show the sterile that the conditions eliminate, then say: and the remainder is productive. For if you say: the condition of affirmation of the minor eliminates eight moods, from multiplying the two states of the negative minor, universal and particular, by the four categorical majors; and the condition of universality of the major eliminates four others, from multiplying the two states of the particular major, affirmative and negative, by the two affirmative minors, giving four; so these are twelve moods, all sterile, and four remain productive as before, and the versifier referred to them after this with his saying: 'Productive for the first are four.' We will explain them with their conclusions, and we say: (1) The first mood: from two universal affirmatives, yields a universal affirmative. Its example from rational sciences: 'Every body is inseparable from accidents, and everything inseparable from accidents is originated' yields 'Every body is originated.' And from fiqh: 'Every wheat is a stored staple food, and every stored staple food is subject to riba' yields 'Every wheat is subject to riba.' And from grammar: 'Every subject (fa'il) is nominative, and every nominative is a core constituent (umda)' yields 'Every subject is a core constituent.' (2) The second mood: from two universals with the major negative, yields a universal negative. Its example from rational sciences: 'Every body is originated, and nothing originated is independent of an agent' yields 'No body is independent of an agent.'
Source pages p. 681

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary (sharh) on a matn, likely al-Sullam al-Munawraq, explaining the conditions for productivity in the first figure of the categorical syllogism and enumerating its productive moods. The author begins by reminding the reader that each figure has 16 possible moods (four minor premises × four major premises). Some moods produce a necessary conclusion (productive), others do not (sterile). To distinguish them, conditions for productivity are needed. For the first figure, there are exactly two conditions: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative (quality), and (2) the major premise must be universal (quantity). The author then justifies why these conditions are necessary: together they allow the judgment of the major term to 'transfer' from the middle term to the minor term. If the minor were negative, it would indicate a separation between minor and middle, so no inference about the minor from the major can be made. An example is given: 'No human is a stone; every stone is a body' does not validly yield 'No human is a body' because the truth is that humans are bodies. Similarly, if the major is 'every stone is inanimate', the truth is negation (humans are not inanimate). Thus the same syllogistic form with different subject matter can give opposite conclusions, showing that form alone does not guarantee truth; the material truth of premises matters. An objection might arise: why does the minor being negative fail to produce a conclusion? The answer (from the commentator) explains that the judgment (major predicate) is known in relation to the middle term but unknown in relation to the minor term, which is essentially the same set of individuals but expressed differently. The dependency (tawaqquf) that is prohibited is the knowledge of the major for the minor as such, but the dependency in the syllogism is on the middle term, which is permissible. Next, the author explains the phrase 'sixteen moods' in the matn: it comes from multiplying the four categorical propositions (A, E, I, O) as minors by the same four as majors. Indefinite and singular propositions are not counted as separate because indefinite is equivalent to particular, singular is treated as universal. From these 16, only four are productive for the first figure. The author of al-Qadiriyya (a commentary) summarized them in a mnemonic: 'Universal, so universal; or none; likewise particular, so universal; or none, take.' That is: AAA, EAE, AII, EIO. The author then explains why the major cannot be particular: if the major is particular, the part it describes might not include the minor, so no transfer of judgment. Examples show that with a particular major, the truth of the conclusion depends on subject matter, not form. The four productive moods are derived by combining the two conditions: the minor must be affirmative (so only two possibilities: universal affirmative A or particular affirmative I), and the major must be universal (so two possibilities: universal affirmative A or universal negative E). Multiplying gives 2×2=4. The remaining 12 are sterile. The author presents two methods to determine the productive moods: (a) the method of verification (tahsil): first identify the productive moods (here, using the conditions), then everything else is sterile; (b) the method of elimination (isqat): first identify the sterile moods eliminated by each condition, then the remainder are productive. The conditions eliminate 12 moods: the negative minor condition eliminates 8 (since there are two types of negative minor (universal and particular) × four majors = 8), and the particular major condition eliminates 4 (two affirmative minors (universal and particular) × two types of particular major (affirmative and negative) = 4). Total 12 sterile, leaving 4. The versifier (nazim) is quoted: 'Productive for the first are four.' Finally, the author lists the first two moods with examples from three domains: rational sciences (kalam), fiqh, and grammar. The first mood (AAA) yields a universal affirmative conclusion; the second mood (EAE) yields a universal negative. The examples are appropriate for the Islamic intellectual tradition: 'Every body is inseparable from accidents, everything inseparable from accidents is originated' is a standard kalam argument for the createdness of bodies. 'Every wheat is a stored staple food, every stored staple food is subject to riba' is a fiqh analogy regarding usury. 'Every subject (fa'il) is nominative, every nominative is a core constituent (umda)' is a grammar rule. The third and fourth moods (AII and EIO) are not given in the available text, but the pattern is clear.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason a negative minor premise prevents a valid conclusion in the first figure?
A) The major premise becomes irrelevant.
B) The negative minor indicates separation between minor and middle terms, so no inference about the minor is possible.
C) The negative minor makes the major premise false.
D) The negative minor is always false in Islamic sciences.

Key Takeaway
A negative minor premise breaks the connection between minor and middle, making the syllogism sterile.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'No wine is permissible; every intoxicant is impermissible; therefore no wine is intoxicant?' Identify why it fails (minor negative). Then correct the minor to make it a valid first-figure syllogism on the same topic.

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
The Four Productive Moods of the First Figure
Listing and derivation of the four productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four productive moods of the first figure and derive them using either the method of verification or elimination.

Core Idea
Four moods survive the conditions: AAA, EAE, AII, EIO.

After establishing the two necessary conditions, the logicians determined that exactly four of the sixteen possible moods in the first figure are productive. This lesson shows how to derive them and introduces two complementary methods: verification (tahsil) and elimination (isqat), both used in Islamic logical pedagogy.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two conditions
Minor must be affirmative, major must be universal. This gives the constraints.
Affirmative minor can be universal (A) or particular (I). Universal major can be affirmative (A) or negative (E).
2
Multiply the possibilities
Combine the two possibilities for minor (A, I) with the two possibilities for major (A, E). This yields 2×2 = 4 moods.
The passage says: 'affirmation of the minor admits of universal and particular, and universality of the major admits of affirmative and negative, so the two states of the minor are multiplied by the two states of the major, giving four.'
3
List the four moods with their acronyms
AAA, EAE, AII, EIO. The passage gives a mnemonic: 'Universal, so universal; or none; likewise particular, so universal; or none, take.'
The versifier al-Qadiriyya is quoted.
4
Understand the two methods
Verification (tahsil): first identify the productive moods, then the rest are sterile. Elimination (isqat): first identify the sterile moods eliminated by each condition, then the remainder are productive.
The passage explains that the condition of affirmative minor eliminates 8 moods, and condition of universal major eliminates 4 more, totaling 12 sterile.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
For instance, the fiqh syllogism 'Every wheat is a stored staple food; every stored staple food is subject to riba' is AAA (productive). If we change the major to 'some stored staple food is subject to riba' (I), it becomes sterile because major is not universal.
The example is from the passage itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَمَّا كَانَ المُتَصَوَّرُ فِي كُلِّ شَكْلٍ سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ ضَرْباً كَمَا سَبَقَ، وَمِنْهَا مَا تَلْزَمُ عَنْهُ النَّتِيجَةُ وَيُسَمَّى «مُنْتِجاً»، وَمَا لَا تَلْزَمُ عَنْهُ وَيُسَمَّى «عَقِيماً»، اُحْتِيجَ إِلَى شُرُوطِ الْإِنْتَاجِ لِيُعْلَمَ أَنَّ مَا تَوَفَّرَتْ فِيهِ مِنَ الضُّرُوبِ مُنْتِجٌ، وَمَا تَخَلَّفَتْ فِيهِ أَوْ بَعْضُهَا عَقِيمٌ. فَأَمَّا الشَّكْلُ الْأَوَّلُ فَلِإِنْتَاجِهِ شَرْطَانِ: (1) أَحَدُهُمَا بِاعْتِبَارِ الْكَيْفِ، وَهُوَ إِيجَابُ صُغْرَاهُ. (2) وَالثَّانِي بِاعْتِبَارِ الْكَمِّ، وَهُوَ كُلِّيَّةُ كُبْرَاهُ. إِذْ بِمَجْمُوعِ الشَّرْطَيْنِ يَتَعَدَّى حُكْمُ الْأَكْبَرِ إِلَى الْأَصْغَرِ، فَيَتَحَقَّقُ الْإِنْتَاجُ. فَإِنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَتْ صُغْرَاهُ سَالِبَةً لَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ إِثْبَاتِ الْأَكْبَرِ لِلْأَوْسَطِ، وَمِنْ سَلْبِهِ عَنْهُ إِثْبَاتُهُ لِلْأَصْغَرِ وَلَا سَلْبُهُ عَنْهُ؛ لِأَنَّ الصُّغْرَى السَّالِبَةَ تَدُلُّ عَلَى تَبَايُنِ الْأَصْغَرِ وَالْأَوْسَطِ كُلِّيًّا أَوْ جُزْئِيًّا، وَالْحُكْمُ عَلَى أَحَدِ الْمُتَبَايَنَيْنِ بِإِيجَابٍ أَوْ سَلْبٍ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ الْحُكْمُ عَلَى الْآخَرِ. كَقَوْلِنَا: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ، وَكُلُّ حَجَرٍ جِسْمٌ» فَلَا يُنْتِجُ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِجِسْمٍ»؛ لِأَنَّ الْحَقَّ الْإِيْجَابُ. وَلَوْ قُلْتَ بَدَلَ الْكُبْرَى: «وَكُلُّ حَجَرٍ جَمَادٌ» لَكَانَ الْحَقُّ السَّلْبَ. فَصُورَةُ الْقِيَاسِ وَاحِدَةٌ، وَالنَّتِيجَةُ مُضْطَرِبَةٌ؛ تَارَةً يَكُونُ الْحَقُّ الْإِيجَابَ، وَتَارَةً السَّلْبَ، وَمَا ذَاكَ إِلَّا لِخُصُوصِ الْمَوَادِّ. وَأَجَابُوا: بِأَنَّ الْحُكْمَ يَخْتَلِفُ عِلْماً وَجَهْلاً بِحَسَبِ اخْتِلَافِ الْعِبَارَةِ عَنِ الْمَوْضُوعِ، فَيَكُونُ مَجْهُولاً مِنْ حَيْثُ التَّعْبِيرُ عَنْهُ بِلَفْظِ الْأَصْغَرِ، وَمَعْلُوماً مِنْ حَيْثُ التَّعْبِيرُ عَنْهُ بِلَفْظِ الْأَوْسَطِ، وَلَا امْتِنَاعَ فِي تَوَقُّفِ الْأَوَّلِ عَلَى الثَّانِي. فَالتَّوَقُّفُ الْمَمْنُوعُ ثُبُوتُ الْأَكْبَرِ لِذَاتِ الْأَصْغَرِ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا مِنْ أَفْرَادِ الْأَصْغَرِ، وَالْمُتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ عِنْدَنَا ثُبُوتُ الْأَكْبَرِ لِذَاتِ الْأَصْغَرِ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا مِنْ أَفْرَادِ الْأَوْسَطِ. قَوْلُهُ: (سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ ضَرْباً) أَيْ مِنْ ضَرْبِ الْمَحْصُورَاتِ الْأَرْبَعِ صُغْرِيَّاتٍ فِي الْمَحْصُورَاتِ الْأَرْبَعِ كُبْرِيَّاتٍ. وَلَمْ يَعْتَبِرُوا فِي عَدَدِ الضُّرُوبِ الْمُهْمَلَةَ؛ لِأَنَّهَا فِي قُوَّةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ، وَلَا الشَّخْصِيَّةَ؛ لِأَنَّهَا فِي حُكْمِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ؛ لِانْحِصَارِ الْمَحْكُومِ عَلَيْهِ فِيهِمَا. الْمُنْتَجُ مِنْ هَذِهِ الضُّرُوبِ لِلشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ أَرْبَعَةٌ، أَشَارَ لَهَا صَاحِبُ «الْقَادِرِيَّةِ» بِقَوْلِهِ: كُلٌّ فَكُلٌّ، أَوْ فَلَا شَيْءَ، كَذَا بَعْضٌ فَكُلٌّ، أَوْ فَلَا شَيْءَ خُذَا. وَأَمَّا صُورَةُ الْقِيَاسِ فَلَمْ تَسْتَلْزِمْ شَيْئاً؛ لِأَنَّ اللَّازِمَ لَا يَتَخَلَّفُ. وَلَوْ كَانَتْ كُبْرَاهُ جُزْئِيَّةً، لَجَازَ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْبَعْضُ الْمَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا غَيْرَ الْأَصْغَرِ، فَلَا يَتَعَدَّى حُكْمُهَا مِنْ إِيجَابٍ أَوْ سَلْبٍ إِلَيْهِ. فَلَوْ قُلْتَ: «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَبَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ فَرَسٌ» لَكَانَ الْحَقُّ السَّلْبَ، وَلَوْ جَعَلْتَ بَدَلَ الْكُبْرَى: «وَبَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ» لَكَانَ الْحَقُّ الْإِيجَابَ. فَضُرُوبُهُ الْمُنْتِجَةُ بِمُقْتَضَى الشَّرْطَيْنِ أَرْبَعَةٌ؛ لِأَنَّ إِيجَابَ الصُّغْرَى يَثْبُتُ لَهَا كُلِّيَّةٌ وَجُزْئِيَّةٌ، وَكُلِّيَّةَ الْكُبْرَى تَثْبُتُ لَهَا مُوجَبَةٌ وَسَالِبَةٌ، فَتُضْرَبُ حَالَتَيِ الصُّغْرَى فِي حَالَتَيِ الْكُبْرَى بِأَرْبَعَةٍ. وَبَقِيَتْ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ اثْنَا عَشَرَ كُلُّهَا عَقِيمَةٌ. هَذَا طَرِيقُ التَّحْصِيلِ، وَهُوَ أَنْ يَحْصُلَ الْمُنْتِجُ، ثُمَّ يُقَالُ: مَا سِوَاهُ عَقِيمٌ. وَأَمَّا طَرِيقُ الْإِسْقَاطِ، وَهُوَ أَنْ يُبَيَّنَ أَوَّلاً الْعَقِيمُ الَّذِي أَسْقَطَتْهُ الشُّرُوطُ، ثُمَّ يُقَالُ: وَالْبَاقِي مُنْتِجٌ. فَإِنْ تَقُلْ: شَرْطُ إِيجَابِ الصُّغْرَى يُسْقِطْ ثَمَانَيَةَ أَضْرُبٍ، مِنْ ضَرْبِ حَالَتَيِ السَّالِبَةِ الصُّغْرَى كُلِّيَّةٌ وَجُزْئِيَّةٌ فِي الْمَحْصُورَاتِ الْأَرْبَعِ كُبْرِيَّاتٍ، وَشَرْطُ كُلِّيَّةِ الْكُبْرَى يُسْقِطْ أَرْبَعَةً أُخْرَى، مِنْ ضَرْبِ حَالَتَيِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ الْكُبْرَى مُوجَبَةٌ وَسَالِبَةٌ فِي الْمُوجَبَتَيْنِ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ بِأَرْبَعَةٍ، فَهَذِهِ اثْنَا عَشَرَ ضَرْباً كُلُّهَا عَقِيمَةٌ، وَتَبْقَى أَرْبَعَةٌ مُنْتِجَةٌ كَمَا سَبَقَ، وَإِلَيْهَا أَشَارَ النَّاظِمُ بَعْدَ هَذَا بِقَوْلِهِ: «فَمُنْتِجٌ لِأَوَّلٍ أَرْبَعَةُ». لِنُبَيِّنَهَا مَعَ نَتَائِجِهَا فَنَقُولُ: (1) الضَّرْبُ الْأَوَّلُ: مِنْ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ، يُنْتِجُ كُلِّيَّةً مُوجَبَةً. مِثَالُهُ مِنَ الْعَقْلِيَّاتِ: «كُلُّ جِرْمٍ مُلَازِمٌ لِلْعَرَضِ، وَكُلُّ مُلَازِمٍ لِلْعَرَضِ حَادِثٌ» يُنْتِجُ: «كُلُّ جِرْمٍ حَادِثٌ». وَمِنَ الْفِقْهِيَّاتِ: «كُلُّ بُرٍّ مُقْتَاتٌ مُدَّخَرٌ، وَكُلُّ مُقْتَاتٍ مُدَّخَرٍ رِبَوِيٌّ» يُنْتِجُ: «كُلُّ بُرٍّ رِبَوِيٌّ». وَمِنَ النَّحَوِيَّاتِ: «كُلُّ فَاعِلٍ مَرْفُوعٌ، وَكُلُّ مَرْفُوعٍ عُمْدَةٌ» يُنْتِجُ: «كُلُّ فَاعِلٍ عُمْدَةٌ». (2) الضَّرْبُ الثَّانِي: مِنْ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ وَالْكُبْرَى سَالِبَةٌ، يُنْتِجُ كُلِّيَّةً سَالِبَةً. مِثَالُهُ مِنَ الْعَقْلِيَّاتِ: «كُلُّ جِرْمٍ حَادِثٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْحَادِثِ يُغْنِي عَنِ الْفَاعِلِ» يُنْتِجُ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْجِرْمِ يُغْنِي عَنِ الْفَاعِلِ».
Since what is conceived in every figure is sixteen moods as previously mentioned, and among them are those from which the conclusion follows, called 'productive', and those from which it does not, called 'sterile', there was a need for conditions of productivity so that it may be known which of the moods fulfill them are productive, and which lack them or some of them are sterile. As for the first figure, it has two conditions for its productivity: (1) One regarding quality, which is the affirmation of its minor premise. (2) The second regarding quantity, which is the universality of its major premise. For by the combination of the two conditions, the judgment of the major term extends to the minor term, thus productivity is realized. For if its minor premise were negative, then from affirming the major of the middle and from denying it of it, neither affirming it of the minor nor denying it of it would follow; because the negative minor indicates the separation of the minor and the middle, either wholly or partially, and judging one of the two separate things by affirmation or negation does not entail the judgment on the other. As in our saying: 'No human is a stone, and every stone is a body'—it does not yield: 'No human is a body'; because the truth is affirmation. And if you replaced the major with 'and every stone is inanimate', then the truth would be negation. So the form of the syllogism is one, yet the conclusion is unstable; sometimes the truth is affirmation, sometimes negation, and that is only due to the specific subject matter. And they answered: That judgment differs in knowledge and ignorance according to the difference of expression about the subject; so it is unknown in terms of being expressed by the term 'minor', and known in terms of being expressed by the term 'middle', and there is no impossibility in the first depending on the second. For the prohibited dependency is the affirmation of the major of the essence of the minor inasmuch as it is an individual of the minor, while the dependency we hold is the affirmation of the major of the essence of the minor inasmuch as it is an individual of the middle. His saying: (sixteen moods) i.e., from multiplying the four categorical propositions as minors by the four categorical propositions as majors. And they did not count among the number of moods the indefinite, because it is equivalent to the particular, nor the singular, because it is in the ruling of the universal, due to the restriction of the subject in both. The productive among these moods for the first figure are four, indicated by the author of 'al-Qadiriyya' in his saying: 'Universal, so universal, or none; likewise particular, so universal, or none, take.' As for the form of the syllogism, it does not necessitate anything; because the necessary does not fail. And if its major were particular, it would be possible that the part judged in it is other than the minor, so its judgment from affirmation or negation does not extend to it. So if you said: 'Every human is an animal, and some animal is a horse'—the truth would be negation; and if you made the major: 'and some animal is rational'—the truth would be affirmation. So its productive moods according to the two conditions are four; because affirmation of the minor admits of universal and particular, and universality of the major admits of affirmative and negative, so the two states of the minor are multiplied by the two states of the major, giving four. And twelve of its moods remain, all sterile. This is the method of verification, which is to obtain the productive, then say: what is other than it is sterile. As for the method of elimination, which is to first show the sterile that the conditions eliminate, then say: and the remainder is productive. For if you say: the condition of affirmation of the minor eliminates eight moods, from multiplying the two states of the negative minor, universal and particular, by the four categorical majors; and the condition of universality of the major eliminates four others, from multiplying the two states of the particular major, affirmative and negative, by the two affirmative minors, giving four; so these are twelve moods, all sterile, and four remain productive as before, and the versifier referred to them after this with his saying: 'Productive for the first are four.' We will explain them with their conclusions, and we say: (1) The first mood: from two universal affirmatives, yields a universal affirmative. Its example from rational sciences: 'Every body is inseparable from accidents, and everything inseparable from accidents is originated' yields 'Every body is originated.' And from fiqh: 'Every wheat is a stored staple food, and every stored staple food is subject to riba' yields 'Every wheat is subject to riba.' And from grammar: 'Every subject (fa'il) is nominative, and every nominative is a core constituent (umda)' yields 'Every subject is a core constituent.' (2) The second mood: from two universals with the major negative, yields a universal negative. Its example from rational sciences: 'Every body is originated, and nothing originated is independent of an agent' yields 'No body is independent of an agent.'
Source pages p. 681

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary (sharh) on a matn, likely al-Sullam al-Munawraq, explaining the conditions for productivity in the first figure of the categorical syllogism and enumerating its productive moods. The author begins by reminding the reader that each figure has 16 possible moods (four minor premises × four major premises). Some moods produce a necessary conclusion (productive), others do not (sterile). To distinguish them, conditions for productivity are needed. For the first figure, there are exactly two conditions: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative (quality), and (2) the major premise must be universal (quantity). The author then justifies why these conditions are necessary: together they allow the judgment of the major term to 'transfer' from the middle term to the minor term. If the minor were negative, it would indicate a separation between minor and middle, so no inference about the minor from the major can be made. An example is given: 'No human is a stone; every stone is a body' does not validly yield 'No human is a body' because the truth is that humans are bodies. Similarly, if the major is 'every stone is inanimate', the truth is negation (humans are not inanimate). Thus the same syllogistic form with different subject matter can give opposite conclusions, showing that form alone does not guarantee truth; the material truth of premises matters. An objection might arise: why does the minor being negative fail to produce a conclusion? The answer (from the commentator) explains that the judgment (major predicate) is known in relation to the middle term but unknown in relation to the minor term, which is essentially the same set of individuals but expressed differently. The dependency (tawaqquf) that is prohibited is the knowledge of the major for the minor as such, but the dependency in the syllogism is on the middle term, which is permissible. Next, the author explains the phrase 'sixteen moods' in the matn: it comes from multiplying the four categorical propositions (A, E, I, O) as minors by the same four as majors. Indefinite and singular propositions are not counted as separate because indefinite is equivalent to particular, singular is treated as universal. From these 16, only four are productive for the first figure. The author of al-Qadiriyya (a commentary) summarized them in a mnemonic: 'Universal, so universal; or none; likewise particular, so universal; or none, take.' That is: AAA, EAE, AII, EIO. The author then explains why the major cannot be particular: if the major is particular, the part it describes might not include the minor, so no transfer of judgment. Examples show that with a particular major, the truth of the conclusion depends on subject matter, not form. The four productive moods are derived by combining the two conditions: the minor must be affirmative (so only two possibilities: universal affirmative A or particular affirmative I), and the major must be universal (so two possibilities: universal affirmative A or universal negative E). Multiplying gives 2×2=4. The remaining 12 are sterile. The author presents two methods to determine the productive moods: (a) the method of verification (tahsil): first identify the productive moods (here, using the conditions), then everything else is sterile; (b) the method of elimination (isqat): first identify the sterile moods eliminated by each condition, then the remainder are productive. The conditions eliminate 12 moods: the negative minor condition eliminates 8 (since there are two types of negative minor (universal and particular) × four majors = 8), and the particular major condition eliminates 4 (two affirmative minors (universal and particular) × two types of particular major (affirmative and negative) = 4). Total 12 sterile, leaving 4. The versifier (nazim) is quoted: 'Productive for the first are four.' Finally, the author lists the first two moods with examples from three domains: rational sciences (kalam), fiqh, and grammar. The first mood (AAA) yields a universal affirmative conclusion; the second mood (EAE) yields a universal negative. The examples are appropriate for the Islamic intellectual tradition: 'Every body is inseparable from accidents, everything inseparable from accidents is originated' is a standard kalam argument for the createdness of bodies. 'Every wheat is a stored staple food, every stored staple food is subject to riba' is a fiqh analogy regarding usury. 'Every subject (fa'il) is nominative, every nominative is a core constituent (umda)' is a grammar rule. The third and fourth moods (AII and EIO) are not given in the available text, but the pattern is clear.

How many productive moods does the first figure have?
A) 4
B) 8
C) 12
D) 16

Key Takeaway
Only four moods (AAA, EAE, AII, EIO) are productive in the first figure; the conditions eliminate the remaining twelve.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the method of elimination, show that a first-figure syllogism with minor 'Some wheat is a staple food' (I) and major 'Every staple food is subject to riba' (A) is productive (AII mood). Then change the major to 'No staple food is subject to riba' and determine if it remains productive. Explain why or why not.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Deriving the Productive Moods of the First Figure
Author explains why form alone does not necessitate and derives four productive moods via two methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the four productive moods of the first figure using both enumeration and elimination methods.

Core Idea
Only four out of sixteen first‑figure moods are productive, due to two simple conditions.

The mere form of a syllogism does not guarantee a valid conclusion, as the author shows with clear examples. We now learn exactly which moods in the first figure are productive and why. The passage presents two complementary methods—one that counts up the valid moods, and one that eliminates the invalid ones. Mastering both will give you a firm grasp of syllogistic validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions for productivity
The minor premise must be affirmative (either universal or particular), and the major premise must be universal (either affirmative or negative). These are the only two restrictions.
These conditions are derived from the requirement that the middle term must distribute the major term over the whole minor term.
2
Use the enumeration method (ṭarīq al‑taḥṣīl)
Multiply the two states of the minor (universal affirmative, particular affirmative) by the two states of the major (universal affirmative, universal negative). The four resulting combinations are the productive moods.
This method directly lists the valid moods.
3
Use the elimination method (ṭarīq al‑isqāṭ)
Start with all 16 possible moods (2×2 for the minor times 4 for the major). First, eliminate eight moods with a negative minor. Then, eliminate four more with a particular major. The remaining four are productive.
This method confirms that exactly 12 are sterile and 4 are productive.
4
Check the examples given in the passage
The author gives two examples: 'Every human is an animal, some animals are horses' (sterile, because major is particular) and 'Every human is an animal, some animals are rational' (also sterile, by the same condition). Verify that neither satisfies the universal major condition.
These examples illustrate why the form alone is insufficient.

وَأَمَّا صُورَةُ الْقِيَاسِ فَلَمْ تَسْتَلْزِمْ شَيْئاً؛ لِأَنَّ اللَّازِمَ لَا يَتَخَلَّفْ وَلَوْ كَانَتْ كُبْرَاهُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ، لَجَازَ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْبَعْضُ الْمَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا غَيْرَ الْأَصْغَرِ، فَلَا يَتَعَدَّى حُكْمُهَا مِنْ إِيْجَابٍ أَوْ سَلْبٍ إِلَيْهِ، فَلَوْ قُلْتَ: (كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَبَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ فَرَسُ) لَكَانَ الْحَقُّ السَّلْبَ، وَلَوْ جَعَلْتَ بَدَلَ الْكُبْرَى: وَبَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ لَكَانَ الْحَقُّ الْإِيْجَابَ. فَضُرُوبُهُ الْمُنْتِجَةُ بِمُقْتَضَى الشَّرْطَيْنِ أَرْبَعَةٌ لِأَنَّ إِيْجَابَ الصُّغْرَى يَثْبُتُ لَهَا كُلِّيَّةٌ وَجُزْئِيَّةٌ، وَكُلِّيَّةُ الْكُبْرَى يَثْبُتُ لَهَا مُوجَبَةٌ وَسَالِبَةٌ، فَتُضْرَبُ حَالَتَيِ الصُّغْرَى فِي حَالَتَيِ الْكُبْرَى، بِأَرْبَعَةٍ، وَبَقِيَتْ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ اثْنَا عَشَرَ كُلُّهَا عَقِيمَةٌ. هَذَا طَرِيقُ التَّحْصِيلِ، وَهُوَ أَنْ يَحْصُلَ الْمُنْتِجُ، ثُمَّ يُقَالُ: مَا سِوَاهُ عَقِيمٌ؛ وَأَمَّا طَرِيقُ الْإِسْقَاطِ، وَهُوَ أَنْ يُبَيَّنَ أَوَّلاً الْعَقِيمُ الَّذِي أَسْقَطَتْهُ الشُّرُوطُ، ثُمَّ يُقَالُ: وَالْبَاقِي مُنْتِجٌ، فَإِنْ تَقُلْ: شَرْطُ إِيْجَابِ الصُّغْرَى يُسْقِطْ ثَمَانِيَةَ أَضْرُبٍ، مِنْ ضَرْبِ حَالَتَيِ السَّالِبَةِ الصُّغْرَى كُلِّيَّةٌ وَجُزْئِيَّةٌ فِي الْمَحْصُورَاتِ الْأَرْبَعِ كُبْرِيَّاتٍ، وَشَرْطُ كُلِّيَّةِ الْكُبْرَى يُسْقِطْ أَرْبَعَةً أُخْرَى مِنْ ضَرْبِ حَالَتَيِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ الْكُبْرَى مُوجَبَةٌ وَسَالِبَةٌ فِي الْمُوجَبَتَيْنِ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ بِأَرْبَعَةٍ، فَهَذِهِ اثْنَا عَشَرَ ضَرْباً كُلُّهَا عَقِيمَةٌ، وَتَبْقَى أَرْبَعَةٌ مُنْتِجَةٌ كَمَا سَبَقَ، وَإِلَيْهَا أَشَارَ النَّاظِمُ بَعْدَ هَذَا بِقَوْلِهِ: فَمُنْتِجٌ لِلأَوَّلِ أَرْبَعَةُ.
As for the form of the syllogism, it does not necessitate anything by itself, because the consequent is never absent; but if its major premise were particular, it is possible that the part predicated in it is other than the minor, so its ruling, whether affirmative or negative, does not extend to it. For if you said: 'Every human is an animal, and some animals are horses,' the truth would be negative; and if you made the major instead: 'and some animals are rational,' the truth would be affirmative. Thus its productive moods according to the two conditions are four, because the affirmation of the minor premise holds for it both universal and particular, and the universality of the major premise holds for it both affirmative and negative, so the two states of the minor are multiplied by the two states of the major, giving four, and twelve of its moods remain, all sterile. This is the method of enumeration, which is to enumerate the productive and then say: what besides them is sterile. As for the method of elimination, which is to first show the sterile that the conditions have eliminated, then say: the remainder is productive. For you say: The condition of the affirmation of the minor eliminates eight moods, from multiplying the two states of the negative minor (universal and particular) by the four restricted major premises; and the condition of the universality of the major eliminates another four, from multiplying the two states of the particular major (affirmative and negative) by the two affirmative minors, giving four. So these are twelve moods, all sterile, and four remain productive as before. And to these the versifier referred after this by his saying: 'So productive for the first are four.'
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for productivity in the first figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins by explaining why the mere form of the syllogism does not guarantee a valid conclusion: if the major premise is particular, the middle term may not distribute the major term to the entire minor term, so the conclusion may not follow. Two examples illustrate this: with 'Every human is an animal, some animals are horses,' the correct conclusion is negative (no human is a horse), not affirmative; with 'some animals are rational,' the correct conclusion is affirmative (some humans are rational). Thus, the form alone is insufficient. The author then states that the first figure has exactly four productive moods, based on two conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative (either universal or particular), and the major premise must be universal (either affirmative or negative). Multiplying the two possibilities of the minor (universal affirmative, particular affirmative) by the two possibilities of the major (universal affirmative, universal negative) gives four productive moods; the remaining twelve combinations (where either the minor is negative or the major is particular) are sterile. Two methods for deriving these four moods are presented. The first method (tariq al-tahsil) enumerates the productive moods directly based on the conditions. The second method (tariq al-isqat) eliminates the sterile moods systematically: the condition of an affirmative minor eliminates eight moods (all those with a negative minor, whether universal or particular, multiplied by the four types of major); the condition of a universal major eliminates another four moods (all those with a particular major, whether affirmative or negative, multiplied by the two affirmative minors). This leaves exactly four productive moods. The author concludes by noting that the versifier (of al-Sullam) indicated these four moods in his poem. The commentary uses intellectual (aqli), jurisprudential (fiqhi), and grammatical (nahwi) examples, but here it only states the method, leaving examples for the next section.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The Four Productive Moods of the First Figure with Examples
Author enumerates the four productive moods of the first figure with examples from theology, fiqh, and grammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive moods of the first figure, determine their conclusion types, and verify them using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The first figure alone yields all four types of categorical conclusions.

In this lesson, Al-Bannani presents the four moods of the first figure that produce valid conclusions. Each mood is defined by its premise combination—universal/particular, affirmative/negative—and yields a unique conclusion. Examples from theology, fiqh, and grammar show how the same logical form applies across different sciences. Pay attention to how the middle term appears in the premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise combination of each mood
For each mood, note the quantity and quality of the minor and major premises. The first mood uses two universal affirmatives; the second uses a universal affirmative minor and universal negative major; the third uses a particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major; the fourth uses a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major.
These are the only valid premise pairs in the first figure.
2
Determine the conclusion type
Based on the premises, the conclusion's quantity and quality follow. The first mood yields universal affirmative; second yields universal negative; third yields particular affirmative; fourth yields particular negative.
Memorize: AAA, EAE, AII, EIO (Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio in Latin terms).
3
Trace the middle term in the examples
In each example, find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. This is the middle term. Notice how it connects the minor and major terms.
For the first theology example: middle term is 'subject to accidents' (مُلَازِمٌ لِلْعَرَضِ).
4
Understand the ordering rationale
The moods are ordered by the nobility of the conclusion: universal affirmative is most noble, then universal negative, then particular affirmative, then particular negative.
The first mood is the only one that combines universality and affirmation, making it the most perfect.

لِنُبَيِّنَهَا مَعَ نَتَائِجِهَا فَنَقُولُ: (١) - الضَّرْبُ الْأَوَّلُ: مِنْ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ، يُنْتِجُ: كُلِّيَّةً مُوجَبَةً. مِثَالُهُ مِنَ الْعَقْلِيَّاتِ: كُلُّ جِرْمٍ مُلَازِمٌ لِلْعَرَضِ، وَكُلُّ مُلَازِمٍ لِلْعَرَضِ حَادِثٌ، يُنْتِجُ: كُلُّ جِرْمٍ حَادِثٌ. وَمِنَ الْفِقْهِيَّاتِ: كُلُّ بُرٍّ مُقْتَاتٌ مُدَّخَرٌ، وَكُلُّ مُقْتَاتٍ مُدَّخَرٍ رِبَوِيٌّ، يُنْتِجُ: كُلُّ بُرٍّ رِبَوِيٌّ. وَمِنَ النَّحَوِيَّاتِ: كُلُّ فَاعِلٍ مَرْفُوعٌ، وَكُلُّ مَرْفُوعٍ عُمْدَةٌ، يُنْتِجُ: كُلُّ فَاعِلٍ عُمْدَةٌ. (٢) - الضَّرْبُ الثَّانِي: مِنْ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ وَالْكُبْرَى سَالِبَةٌ، يُنْتِجُ: كُلِّيَّةً سَالِبَةً. مِثَالُهُ مِنَ الْعَقْلِيَّاتِ: كُلُّ جِرْمٍ حَادِثٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْحَادِثِ يُغْنِي عَنِ الْفَاعِلِ، يُنْتِجُ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْجِرْمِ يُغْنِي عَنِ الْفَاعِلِ. وَمِنَ الْفِقْهِيَّاتِ: كُلُّ صَوْمٍ عِبَادَةٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْعِبَادَةِ يَصِحُّ بِلَا نِيَّةٍ، يُنْتِجُ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الصَّوْمِ يَصِحُّ بِلَا نِيَّةٍ. وَمِنَ النَّحَوِيَّاتِ: كُلُّ فَاعِلٍ مَرْفُوعٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْمَرْفُوعِ بِفَضْلَةٍ، يُنْتِجُ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْفَاعِلِ بِفَضْلَةٍ. (٣) - الضَّرْبُ الثَّالِثُ: مِنْ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ وَالصُّغْرَى جُزْئِيَّةٌ، يُنْتِجُ: جُزْئِيَّةً مُوجَبَةً. مِثَالُهُ مِنَ الْعَقْلِيَّاتِ: بَعْضُ الصِّفَاتِ عَرَضٌ، وَكُلُّ عَرَضٍ حَادِثٌ، يُنْتِجُ: بَعْضُ الصِّفَاتِ حَادِثٌ. وَمِنَ الْفِقْهِيَّاتِ: بَعْضُ الطَّعَامِ مُقْتَاتٌ مُدَّخَرٌ، وَكُلُّ مُقْتَاتٍ مُدَّخَرٍ رِبَوِيٌّ، يُنْتِجُ: بَعْضُ الطَّعَامِ رِبَوِيٌّ. وَمِنَ النَّحَوِيَّاتِ: بَعْضُ الْمَرْفُوعِ فَاعِلٌ، وَكُلُّ فَاعِلٍ يُمْنَعُ حَذْفُهُ، يُنْتِجُ: بَعْضُ الْمَرْفُوعِ يُمْنَعُ حَذْفُهُ. (٤) - الضَّرْبُ الرَّابِعُ: مِنْ جُزْئِيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَكُلِّيَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ، يُنْتِجُ: جُزْئِيَّةً سَالِبَةً. مِثَالُهُ مِنَ الْعَقْلِيَّاتِ: بَعْضُ الصِّفَاتِ قَدِيمٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْقَدِيمِ بِعَرَضٍ، يُنْتِجُ: بَعْضُ الصِّفَاتِ لَيْسَ بِعَرَضٍ. وَمِنَ الْفِقْهِيَّاتِ: بَعْضُ الْعِبَادَةِ صَوْمٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الصَّوْمِ يُقَابِلُ النِّيَابَةَ، يُنْتِجُ: لَيْسَ بَعْضُ الْعِبَادَةِ يُقَابِلُ النِّيَابَةَ. وَمِنَ النَّحَوِيَّاتِ: بَعْضُ الْمَرْفُوعِ فَاعِلٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْفَاعِلِ بِجَائِزِ التَّقْدِيمِ، يُنْتِجُ: بَعْضُ الْمَرْفُوعِ لَيْسَ بِجَائِزِ التَّقْدِيمِ. وَقَدْ تَبَيَّنَ أَنَّ الشَّكْلَ الْأَوَّلَ مُنْتِجٌ لِلْمَطَالِبِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ، وَقُدِّمَ الْأَوَّلُ مِنْهُ لِإِنْتَاجِهِ الشَّرَفَيْنِ الْإِيْجَابِ وَالْكُلِّيَّةِ، وَلَا يُنْتِجُهُمَا ضَرْبٌ غَيْرُهُ فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَشْكَالِ، ثُمَّ الثَّانِي لِإِنْتَاجِهِ شَرَفَ الْكُلِّيَّةِ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةً؛ لِأَنَّهَا أَشْرَفُ مِنَ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ الْمُوجَبَةِ الَّتِي يُنْتِجُهَا الثَّالِثُ، وَأُخِّرَ الرَّابِعُ لِاسْتِمَالِ نَتِيجَتِهِ عَلَى الْخِسَّتَيْنِ.
Let us clarify them with their conclusions, and we say: (1) The first mood: from two universal affirmatives, it produces a universal affirmative conclusion. An example from intellectual matters: 'Every body is subject to accidents, and everything subject to accidents is originated' produces 'Every body is originated.' From jurisprudential matters: 'Every wheat is a staple food stored, and every staple food stored is subject to riba' produces 'Every wheat is subject to riba.' From grammatical matters: 'Every agent is nominative, and every nominative is core element' produces 'Every agent is core element.' (2) The second mood: from two universals with the major negative, it produces a universal negative conclusion. An example from intellectual matters: 'Every body is originated, and nothing originated is independent of an agent' produces 'No body is independent of an agent.' From jurisprudential matters: 'Every fast is worship, and nothing of worship is valid without intention' produces 'No fast is valid without intention.' From grammatical matters: 'Every agent is nominative, and nothing nominative is extra' produces 'No agent is extra.' (3) The third mood: from two affirmatives with the minor particular, it produces a particular affirmative conclusion. An example from intellectual matters: 'Some attributes are accident, and every accident is originated' produces 'Some attributes are originated.' From jurisprudential matters: 'Some food is staple stored, and every staple stored is subject to riba' produces 'Some food is subject to riba.' From grammatical matters: 'Some nominatives are agent, and every agent prevents its deletion' produces 'Some nominatives prevent deletion.' (4) The fourth mood: from a particular affirmative and a universal negative, it produces a particular negative conclusion. An example from intellectual matters: 'Some attributes are eternal, and nothing eternal is accident' produces 'Some attributes are not accidents.' From jurisprudential matters: 'Some worship is fast, and no fast accepts deputyship' produces 'Some worship does not accept deputyship.' From grammatical matters: 'Some nominatives are agent, and no agent permits fronting' produces 'Some nominatives do not permit fronting.' And it has become clear that the first figure produces the four types of desired conclusions. The first mood is presented first because it produces the two superior qualities: affirmation and universality, and no other mood in all the figures produces them. Then the second because it produces the superior quality of universality even though it is negative, for it is superior to the particular affirmative which the third produces. And the fourth is delayed because its conclusion comprises the two inferior qualities.
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage presents the four productive moods of the first figure with examples from three disciplines: theology (al-ʿaqliyyāt), jurisprudence (al-fiqhiyyāt), and grammar (al-nahwiyyāt). The author systematically lists each mood, describes its premise combination, states the type of conclusion, and provides an example from each field. The first mood (mood 1) has two universal affirmative premises and yields a universal affirmative conclusion. The example from theology: 'Every body is subject to accidents; everything subject to accidents is originated; therefore every body is originated.' From fiqh: 'Every wheat is a staple food stored; every staple food stored is subject to riba; therefore every wheat is subject to riba.' From grammar: 'Every agent is nominative; every nominative is a core element; therefore every agent is a core element.' The second mood (mood 2) has a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, yielding a universal negative conclusion. Examples: theology: 'Every body is originated; nothing originated is independent of an agent; therefore no body is independent of an agent.' Fiqh: 'Every fast is worship; nothing worship is valid without intention; therefore no fast is valid without intention.' Grammar: 'Every agent is nominative; nothing nominative is extra; therefore no agent is extra.' The third mood (mood 3) has a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. Examples: theology: 'Some attributes are accident; every accident is originated; therefore some attributes are originated.' Fiqh: 'Some food is staple stored; every staple stored is subject to riba; therefore some food is subject to riba.' Grammar: 'Some nominatives are agent; every agent prevents its deletion; therefore some nominatives prevent deletion.' The fourth mood (mood 4) has a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion. Examples: theology: 'Some attributes are eternal; nothing eternal is accident; therefore some attributes are not accidents.' Fiqh: 'Some worship is fast; no fast accepts deputyship; therefore some worship does not accept deputyship.' Grammar: 'Some nominatives are agent; no agent permits fronting; therefore some nominatives do not permit fronting.' After presenting the moods, the author explains the order: the first mood is presented first because it produces the most noble conclusion (universal affirmative), combining both superiority of quantity (universal) and quality (affirmative). The second mood comes next because it produces a universal conclusion, which is superior to the particular affirmative of the third mood. The third mood follows because its conclusion is affirmative (superior to negative). The fourth mood is last because its conclusion is both particular and negative, the least noble. The point is that the first figure alone can produce all four types of categorical propositions as conclusions, which is a key strength.

Key Takeaway

intermediate

Second, Third, and Fourth Moods of the First Figure
Author presents three moods of the first figure with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second, third, and fourth moods of the first figure and determine the conclusion for each mood.

Core Idea
The first figure alone produces all four conclusion types, but not all moods are equal in nobility.

In the previous lesson, you learned about the first mood (AAA) of the first figure. Now the author presents the remaining three moods that complete the first figure. Each mood is defined by the quality and quantity of its premises, and yields a specific conclusion. The author also ranks the moods by the 'honor' of their conclusions – universality and affirmation are superior to particularity and negation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises for each mood
For each mood, note the combination of premises. The second mood has a universal negative major and a universal affirmative minor. The third mood has two affirmatives with a particular minor. The fourth mood has a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major.
These combinations are fixed for validity.
2
Note the conclusion pattern
The conclusion is determined by the premises. The second mood yields a universal negative (E), the third yields a particular affirmative (I), and the fourth yields a particular negative (O).
3
Verify with the examples
Check each example from the passage. For the second mood: 'Every fast is worship' (universal affirmative minor) and 'No worship is valid without intention' (universal negative major) give 'No fast is valid without intention' (universal negative). For the third: 'Some attributes are accidents' (particular affirmative minor) and 'Every accident is originated' (universal affirmative major) give 'Some attributes are originated' (particular affirmative). For the fourth: 'Some attributes are eternal' (particular affirmative minor) and 'No eternal is an accident' (universal negative major) give 'Some attributes are not accidents' (particular negative).
Examples come from rational theology, jurisprudence, and grammar.
4
Understand the ranking
The author ranks the moods: first mood (AAA) is best because it gives affirmation and universality; second (EAE) gives universality but negation; third (AII) gives particular affirmation; fourth (EIO) gives particular negation. Hence the order of presentation.
This ranking reflects the epistemic strength of the conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأشكال
الضرب الثاني: من كلية سالبة وكلية موجبة ينتج كلية سالبة. مثال من الفقهيات: كل صوم عبادة، ولا شيء من العبادة يصح بلا نية، ينتج: لا شيء من الصوم يصح بلا نية. ومن النحويات: كل فاعل مرفوع، ولا شيء من المرفوع بفضلة، ينتج: لا شيء من الفاعل بفضلة.
الضرب الثالث: من موجبتين والصغرى جزئية، ينتج جزئية موجبة. مثال من العقليات: بعض الصفات عرض، وكل عرض حادث، ينتج: بعض الصفات حادث. ومن الفقهيات: بعض الطعام مقتات مدخر، وكل مقتات مدخرب ربوي، ينتج: بعض الطعام ربوي. ومن النحويات: بعض المرفوع فاعل، وكل فاعل يمنع حذفه، ينتج: بعض المرفوع يمنع حذفه.
الضرب الرابع: من جزئية موجبة وكلية سالبة، ينتج جزئية سالبة. مثال من العقليات: بعض الصفات قديم، ولا شيء من القديم بعرض، ينتج: بعض الصفات ليس بعرض. ومن الفقهيات: بعض العبادة صوم، ولا شيء من الصوم يقابل النيابة، ينتج: ليس بعض العبادة يقابل النيابة. ومن النحويات: بعض المرفوع فاعل، ولا شيء من الفاعل بجائز التقديم، ينتج: بعض المرفوع ليس بجائز التقديم.
وقد تبين أن الشكل الأول منتج للمطالب الأربعة، وقدم الأول منه لإنتاجه الشرفين الإيجاب والكلية، ولا ينتجهما ضرب غيره في جميع الأشكال، ثم الثاني لإنتاجه شرف الكلية وإن كانت سالبة؛ لأنها أشرف من الجزئية الموجبة التي ينتجها الثالث، وأخر الرابع لاستمال نتيجته على الخستين.
Chapter on the Figures. The second mood: from a universal negative and a universal affirmative, it produces a universal negative. Example from jurisprudential matters: Every fast is an act of worship, and nothing from the acts of worship is valid without intention, therefore nothing from fasting is valid without intention. And from grammatical matters: Every subject (fa'il) is in the nominative case (marfu'), and nothing from the nominative is with an extra (bi-fadla), therefore nothing from the subject is with an extra. The third mood: from two affirmatives with the minor particular, it produces a particular affirmative. Example from rational matters: Some attributes are an accident, and every accident is originated, therefore some attributes are originated. And from jurisprudential matters: Some food is a stored staple (muqtat mudakhar), and every stored staple is subject to riba, therefore some food is subject to riba. And from grammatical matters: Some nominatives are a subject, and every subject is prevented from deletion, therefore some nominatives are prevented from deletion. The fourth mood: from a particular affirmative and a universal negative, it produces a particular negative. Example from rational matters: Some attributes are eternal, and nothing from the eternal is an accident, therefore some attributes are not an accident. And from jurisprudential matters: Some acts of worship are fasting, and nothing from fasting accepts substitution (niyaba), therefore not every act of worship accepts substitution. And from grammatical matters: Some nominatives are a subject, and nothing from the subject is permissible to advance (ja'iz al-taqdim), therefore some nominatives are not permissible to advance. And it has become clear that the first figure produces the four types of conclusions. The first (mood) is given precedence because it produces the two honorable features: affirmation and universality, and no other mood in all the figures produces them. Then the second (is given precedence) because it produces the honor of universality even though it is negative, since it is more honorable than the particular affirmative produced by the third. And the fourth is delayed because its conclusion has the two lowly features.
Source pages p. 683

Commentary
This passage presents the second, third, and fourth moods of the first syllogistic figure (shakl awwal) from the text al-Sullam al-Munawraq. It begins by defining each mood in terms of the combination of premises (two premises with specific quality and quantity) and the resulting conclusion. For each mood, the author provides examples from three sciences: rational theology ('aqliyyat), jurisprudence (fiqhiyyat), and grammar (nahwiyyat). These examples illustrate how the same logical form applies across different disciplines, all rooted in the Islamic sciences. The second mood (universal negative major and universal affirmative minor) yields a universal negative conclusion. The third mood (both premises affirmative with a particular minor) yields a particular affirmative. The fourth mood (particular affirmative minor and universal negative major) yields a particular negative. After presenting the moods, the text concludes with a ranking: the first mood (universal affirmative premises) is most noble because it produces both universality and affirmation, which the other moods cannot claim. The second mood is second-best because it produces universality, even if negative, which is more honorable than the particularity produced by the third mood. The fourth mood is least noble because its conclusion is both particular and negative. This ranking is important for understanding which arguments are most certain in demonstrative reasoning.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Conditions for a Valid Second Figure Syllogism
Commentary explains the two necessary conditions for a valid syllogism in the second figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a second figure categorical syllogism meets the two necessary conditions (differing quality and universal major) and identify violations using Islamic science examples.

Core Idea
Second figure requires differing quality and a universal major premise.

In this passage, Al-Bannani expands on the two conditions for the second figure: premises must differ in quality, and the major must be universal. Without both, the syllogism fails to produce a consistent conclusion. Let's unpack why these conditions matter and see them applied in an fiqh example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Read the passage and locate the two conditions: (1) the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and (2) the major premise must be universal. Note the Arabic terms: الكيف (quality) and الكم (quantity).
The major premise is the one containing the major term (predicate of the conclusion).
2
Test the condition on a valid example
Take the example from the commentary: 'Every human is an animal' (affirmative universal) and 'No stone is an animal' (negative universal). Check: the premises differ in quality, and the major ('No stone is an animal') is universal. Conclusion: 'No human is a stone.' This is valid.
This is a second figure syllogism (Camestres mood).
3
Recognize the logical principle
The passage explains: disparity in the concomitants (lawaazim) indicates disparity in the entailed subjects (malzumaat). In the example, the concomitants 'animality' and 'non-animality' are contradictory, so the subjects 'human' and 'stone' must be distinct.
This principle underlies why the second figure works: it shows that two things are different because they have opposite necessary properties.
4
Analyze a violation of the conditions
Consider what happens if the major premise is particular: 'Every speaker is a human' (affirmative universal) and 'Some animal is not a human' (negative particular). The premises differ in quality, but the major is particular. The attempted conclusion 'Some speaker is not an animal' is false because the contradiction is only with part of the major.
The condition of universal major ensures that the minor term contradicts the entire major term, not just some individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والثان: أن يختلفا في الكيف مع كلية الكبرى له شرط وقع ذكر لإنتاج الشكل الثاني أيضا شرطين: (1) أحدهما: باعتبار الكيف، وهو: اختلاف كيف مقدمتيه؛ بأن تكون صغراه موجبة وكبراه سالبة، أو العكس. (2) وثانيهما: باعتبار الكم، وهو: كلية كبراه؛ لأن بمجموع الشرطين يتحقق وجه إنتاجه، وهو أن التباين في اللوازم يؤذن بالتباين في الملزومات، ولأنه متى انتفى أحد شرطيه...
ثم شرح: قول الناظم: (والثان: أن يختلفا في الكيف ... إلخ): قوله: (وهو: اختلاف كيف مقدمتيه) أي: لأنهما لو اتفقتا في الكيف، فهما إما موجبتان أو سالبتان وأيا ما كان لزم الاختلاف الموجب للعقم باضطراب النتيجة بصدقها تارة وكذبها أخرى؛ لأن حاصل إنتاج هذا الشكل أن الأصغر والأكبر متناقضان في الأوسط، ولا يتحقق ذلك مع الاتفاق في الكيف؛ مثلاً إذا قلت في المتساويين: «كل إنسان حيوان وكل ناطق حيوان»، وفي المتباينين في الكبرى: «وكل فرس حيوان»، كان الحق في نتيجة الأول الإيجاب، وفي الثاني السلب، فقد اضطربت النتيجة؛ لعدم تناقض الأصغر والأكبر في الأوسط.
قوله: (وهو: كلية كبراه) أي: لأنها لو كانت جزئية؛ لجاز أن يكون المباين للأصغر بعض أفراد الأكبر، وذلك غير مستلزم لمباينة حقيقة الأكبر للأصغر؛ كقولنا: «كل ناطق إنسان، وبعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان»، فحاصل المقدمتين: أن الناطق وهو الأصغر تنافى مع بعض أفراد الأكبر، فكذبت النتيجة، فإنه ينتج: بعض الناطق ليس بحيوان» وهو باطل. والحاصل: أن النتيجة لا تصدق إلا إذا تنافى الأصغر مع سائر أفراد الأكبر؛ كقولنا: «كل ناطق إنسان، ولا شيء من الفرس بإنسان ينتج: لا شيء من الناطق بفرس»، وهو حق. قوله: (وهو أن التباين في اللوازم يؤذن بالتباين في الملزومات) مثلاً إذا قلنا: كل إنسان حيوان، ولا شيء من الحجر بحيوان، فقد بينا أن الإنسان والحجر تنافى لازماهما؛ إذ لازم الإنسان هو الحيوانية، ولازم الحجر نقيضها، وهذان اللازمان لا يجتمعان أبداً؛ إذ لو اجتمع الإنسان والحجر لاجتمع «حيوان» و: «لا حيوان»؛ لوجود حيوان مع وجود إنسان، ووجود لا حيوان مع وجود الحجر، وهو معنى قولهم: اجتماع الملزومين يستلزم اجتماع لازميهما»، لكن اجتماع...
His statement: 'And the second: that they differ in quality with the universality of the major – for it a condition mentioned.' For the production of the second figure also two conditions: (1) One of them: with regard to quality, which is: the difference in quality of its two premises; such that its minor is affirmative and its major is negative, or the opposite. (2) And the second of them: with regard to quantity, which is: the universality of its major; because by the totality of the two conditions the aspect of its production is realized, which is that the disparity in the concomitants indicates disparity in the entailed subjects, and because whenever one of its conditions is absent... Then a commentary: The words of the author: 'And the second: that they differ in quality ... etc.': His statement: 'Which is: difference in quality of its two premises' – that is, because if they agreed in quality, they would be either both affirmative or both negative, and whichever it was, the difference would necessarily lead to sterility due to the inconsistency of the conclusion, being true sometimes and false other times; because the essence of the production of this figure is that the minor and major are contradictory in the middle, and that is not realized with agreement in quality; for example, if you say in the case of equal terms: 'Every human is an animal and every speaker is an animal', and in the case of differing terms in the major: 'And every horse is an animal', the truth in the conclusion of the first is affirmative, and in the second negative, so the conclusion is inconsistent due to lack of contradiction of the minor and major in the middle. His statement: 'Which is: universality of its major' – that is, because if it were particular, then it would be possible that the thing opposite to the minor is some individuals of the major, and that does not necessitate the opposition of the reality of the major to the minor; as if we say: 'Every speaker is a human, and some animal is not a human', so the outcome of the two premises is that 'the speaker' which is the minor contradicts some individuals of the major, so the conclusion is false, for it produces: 'Some speaker is not an animal' which is false. And the upshot: that the conclusion is not true unless the minor contradicts all individuals of the major; as if we say: 'Every speaker is a human, and nothing from the horse is a human, produces: Nothing from the speaker is a horse', which is true. His statement: 'Which is that disparity in the concomitants indicates disparity in the entailed subjects' – for example, if we say: 'Every human is an animal, and nothing from the stone is an animal', we have shown that the concomitants of human and stone are disparate; since the concomitant of human is animality, and the concomitant of stone is its negation, and these two concomitants never combine; because if human and stone combined, 'animal' and 'not animal' would combine due to the existence of animal with the existence of human, and the existence of not animal with the existence of stone, and that is the meaning of their saying: 'The combination of the two entailed subjects necessitates the combination of their two concomitants', but the combination...
Source pages p. 684

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the conditions for the validity of the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The author, al-Bannani, explains two conditions extracted from the original text (al-Sullam). The first condition concerns the quality (kayf) of the premises: they must differ—one must be affirmative and the other negative. If both are affirmative or both negative, the syllogism will not produce a valid conclusion because the minor and major terms would not be contradictory in the middle term, leading to inconsistent results. The second condition concerns the quantity (kamm) of the major premise: it must be universal. If the major is particular, the minor may contradict only some individuals of the major, not all, so the conclusion might be false. Only when the major is universal does the contradiction hold for all individuals, ensuring a true negative conclusion. The commentary illustrates these conditions with concrete examples using Islamic disciplines: the example of 'human', 'animal', 'horse', and 'stone' are from rational theology and natural philosophy. It also explains the underlying logical principle: the disparity in the concomitants (lawaazim) indicates a disparity in the entailed subjects (malzumaat). If two things have conflicting necessary properties, then the things themselves cannot coexist. This principle is foundational for understanding why the second figure works.

Key Terms
كيف
كم
كبرى
لازم
ملزوم

Key Takeaway
For a valid second figure syllogism, the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal, ensuring that the minor term contradicts the entire major term.

The Syllogism
intermediate

First Condition for Second Figure: Difference in Quality
Author states the two conditions for productivity of the second figure and explains the first condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for the productivity of the second figure and explain why the first condition (difference in quality) is necessary.

Core Idea
To produce a valid conclusion in the second figure, premises must differ in quality and the major must be universal.

The second figure of categorical syllogism has its own requirements for productivity. In this lesson, we examine the first condition: the two premises must differ in quality—one affirmative, one negative. The commentator explains why this is essential to avoid sterility and provides an example illustrating the instability of the conclusion when this condition is violated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Read the passage to find that the author lists two conditions: (1) the premises must differ in quality, and (2) the major premise must be universal.
2
Understand 'difference in quality'
Note that this means one premise is affirmative and the other is negative, in either order.
3
Grasp the rationale
The passage states that productivity depends on the subject and predicate being contradictory in the middle term. If both premises are affirmative, this contradiction is lost.
4
Analyse the example
In the example with 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every rational is an animal', the conclusion varies between affirmative and negative depending on the subject, showing sterility when both premises are affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَالثَّانِ: أَنْ يَخْتَلِفًا فِي الكَيْفِ مَعَ كُلِّيَّةِ الكُبْرَى لَهُ شَرْطُ وَقَعْ ذَكَرَ لِإِنْتاجِ الشَّكْلِ الثَّانِي أَيْضاً شَرْطَيْنِ : (١) - أَحَدُهُمَا: بِاعْتِبَارِ الكَيْفِ، وَهُوَ : اخْتِلَافُ كَيْفِ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ ؛ بِأَنْ تَكُونَ صُغْرَاهُ مُوجَبَةٌ وَكُبْرَاهُ سَالِبَةٌ، أَوِ العَكْسَ [٢/ ٥٥] . - (۲) - وَثَانِيهُمَا : بِاعْتِبَارِ الكَمْ، وَهُوَ : كُلِّيَّةٌ كُبْرَاهُ ؛ لأنَّ بِمَجْمُوع الشَّرْطَيْنِ يَتَحَقَّقُ وَجْهُ إِنْتاجِهِ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ التَّبَايُنَ فِي اللَّوَازِمِ يُؤذِنُ بِالتَّبَايُنِ فِي المَلْزُومَاتِ، وَلِأَنَّهُ مَتَى انْتَفَى أَحَدُهُمَا لَزِمَ الِاخْتِلَافُ المُوجِبُ لِلْعُقْم بِاضْطِرَابِ النَّتِيجَةِ بِصِدْقِهَا تَارَةً وَكَذِبِهَا أُخْرَى؛ لأنَّ حَاصِلَ إِنْتَاجِ هَذَا الشَّكْلِ أَنَّ الْأَصْغَرَ وَالْأَكْبَرَ مُتَنَاقِضَانِ فِي الْأَوْسَطِ، وَلَا يَتَحَقَّقُ ذَلِكَ مَعَ الِاتِّفَاقِ فِي الْكَيْفِ؛ مَثَلًا إِذَا قُلْتَ فِي الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ وَكُلُّ نَاطِقٍ حَيَوَانٌ»، وَفِي الْمُتَبَايِنَيْنِ فِي الْكُبْرَى: «وَكُلُّ فَرَسٍ حَيَوَانٌ»، كَانَ الْحَقُّ فِي نَتِيجَةِ الْأَوَّلِ الْإِيْجَابَ، وَفِي الثَّانِي السَّلْبَ، فَقَدِ اضْطَرَبَتِ النَّتِيجَةُ؛ لِعَدَمِ تَنَاقُضِ الْأَصْغَرِ وَالْأَكْبَرِ فِي الْأَوْسَطِ
His statement: 'And the second: that they differ in quality together with the universality of the major; it has a condition mentioned for the productivity of the second figure also two conditions: (1) One of them: with respect to quality, and it is: the difference of the quality of its two premises; such that its minor is affirmative and its major is negative, or the opposite. (2) And the second of them: with respect to quantity, and it is: the universality of its major; because by the sum of the two conditions the aspect of its productivity is realized, and it is that the difference in the concomitants indicates the difference in the things concomitated. And because when one of them is absent, the difference necessitating sterility occurs, due to the instability of the conclusion, being true at one time and false at another; because the import of the productivity of this figure is that the subject and predicate are contradictory in the middle term, and that is not realized when they agree in quality; for example, when you say about the two equal things: 'Every human is an animal and every rational is an animal', and about the two different things in the major: 'And every horse is an animal', the truth in the conclusion of the first is affirmative, and in the second is negative, so the conclusion is unstable due to the lack of contradiction of the subject and predicate in the middle term.
Source pages p. 684

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn line discussing the two conditions for productivity of the second figure. The commentator first states that the second figure also has two conditions: first, the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative); second, the major premise must be universal. He then explains the first condition in detail. The reason is that the second figure's productivity relies on the subject and predicate being contradictory in relation to the middle term. If both premises agree in quality, this contradiction is lost, leading to a sterile syllogism where the conclusion is sometimes true and sometimes false. He gives an example: if both premises are affirmative, as 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every rational is an animal', the conclusion 'Every human is rational' would be true, but if we use 'Every horse is an animal' as the major, the conclusion 'No human is a horse' would be true? Actually he shows that in the first case (equal things) the conclusion is affirmative, in the second (different things) the conclusion is negative, so the conclusion varies. Thus, the syllogism is not productive without the difference in quality. All examples are from common philosophical discourse: human, animal, rational, horse.

Key Terms
اختلاف الكيف (ikhtilāf al-kayf)
العقم (al-ʿuqum)
الموجبة (al-mūjaba)
السالب (al-sāliba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must the two premises differ in quality in the second figure?
A) Because the subject and predicate must be contradictory in the middle term.
B) To ensure the major premise is universal.
C) To allow conversion of the minor premise.
D) To produce a true conclusion every time.

Key Takeaway
For a second figure syllogism to be productive, the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a second figure categorical syllogism in Islamic theology (e.g., about the necessity of a creator) that satisfies the two conditions (difference in quality, universal major). Then modify it so that both premises are affirmative, and explain why the conclusion becomes unstable.
?
Identify the quality of each premise in the following argument and check if the second figure condition is met: 'Every act of injustice is prohibited; no act of charity is an act of injustice; therefore no act of charity is prohibited.' (Note: this is actually first figure, but adapt as an exercise for checking conditions?)
