The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Why the Major Premise Must Be Universal in the Second Figure
Explains why the major must be universal in the second figure with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given second-figure categorical syllogism has a universal major and determine if the conclusion necessarily follows.

Core Idea
A particular major makes the second figure sterile; only a universal major guarantees a valid conclusion.

In the second figure of the categorical syllogism, the major premise must be universal. If it is particular, the minor term might be opposed only to some individuals of the major, not all, leading to a false conclusion. This lesson unpacks that condition with concrete examples from the commentary.

Guided Path
1
Locate the major premise
In a second-figure syllogism, the major premise is the one whose predicate is the predicate of the conclusion. Check its quantity: is it universal or particular?
2
Test with a particular major
Take the example: 'Every rational is human, some animal is not human.' The major 'animal' is particular. The minor 'rational' opposes only 'some animal', not all, so the conclusion 'Some rational is not animal' is false.
This shows that a particular major allows the possibility of the minor not opposing all individuals of the major.
3
Compare with a universal major
Take 'Every rational is human, no horse is human.' The major 'horse' is universal negative. The minor 'rational' opposes all horses, so the conclusion 'No rational is a horse' is true.
The universal major ensures the minor's opposition covers all individuals of the major.
4
Generalize the condition
For the conclusion to be necessarily true, the minor must be opposed to every individual of the major. Hence, the major must be universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَهُوَ : كُلِّيَّةُ كُبْرَاهُ أي : لأنَّها لو كانت جزئيَّةٌ ؛ لجاز أن يكون المباين للأصغر بعض أفراد الأكبر، وذلك غير مستلزم لمباينة حقيقة الأكبر للأصغر؛ كقولنا : «كل ناطق إنسان، وبعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان»، فحاصلُ المقدِّمتين : أنَّ النَّاطق» وهو الأصغر تنافى مع بعض أفراد الأكبر، فكذبت النتيجة ، فإنَّه ينتج : بعض الناطق ليس بحيوان» وهو باطل. والحاصلُ : أنَّ النتيجة لا تصدق إلَّا إذا تنافى الأصغر مع سائر أفراد الأكبر ؛ كقولنا : «كل ناطق إنسان، ولا شيء مِنَ الفرس بإنسان ينتج : لا شيء مِنَ النَّاطق بفرس»، وهو حق. قوله : (وَهُوَ أَنَّ التَّبَايُنَ فِي اللَّوَازِمِ يُؤذِنُ بِالثَّبَايُنِ فِي المَلْزُومَاتِ) مثلاً إذا قلنا : كلُّ إنسان حيوان، ولا شيء مِنَ الحجر بحيوان، فقد بيّنا أنَّ الإنسان والحجر تنافي لازماهما ؛ إذ لازم الإنسان هو الحيوانية، ولازم الحجر نقيضها، وهذان اللازمان لا يجتمعان أبداً؛ إذ لو اجتمع الإنسان والحجر لاجتمع «حيوان» و : «لا حيوان»؛ لوجود حيوان مع وجود إنسان، ووجود لا حيوان مع وجود الحجر، وهو معنى قولهم : اجتماع الملزومين يستلزم اجتماع لازميهما»، لكن اجتماع حيوان ولا حيوان باطل، فاجتماع الإنسان والحجر المؤدِّي إلى هذا الباطل باطل، وهو معنى قولهم : تنافي اللوازم دليل على تنافي الملزومات»، وهي قاعدة مطردة. فإن قيل : يلزمُ على قولهم : تباين اللوازم يؤذن بالتَّباين في الملزومات، أن يكون إنتاج الثاني مبنيا على صدق هذه المقدّمة، ويلزم على ذلك أنَّه لم يبق بينه وبين قياس المساواة الذي يحترزون عن دخوله في حد القياس فرقٌ، فلا يدخل هذا ويخرج ذلك. أجيب عنه : بأنَّ بينهما فرقاً، وهو أنَّ قياس المساواة يتوقف إنتاجه على الإتيان بالمقدمة الأجنبيَّة، والشَّكلُ الثَّاني لا يتوقف إنتاجُهُ على الإتيان بها ؛ لفهمهم مقتضاها من مقدمتيه، ووجود معناها فيهما، وذلك مثل ما في الشكل الأوَّل الذي هو أبينها، فإنَّه مبني على أنَّ لازم اللازم لازم ؛ لفهمه من مقدِّمتيه ضرورةً . قوله : (لَزِمَ الِاخْتِلَافُ المُوجِبُ لِلْعُقْم بيانُهُ أنَّ قولنا مثلاً : كلُّ إنسان حيوان، وكلُّ فرس حیوان» نتيجته كاذبة، ولو قلت في الكبرى : وكلُّ ناطق حيوان، صدقت، وكذا إن قلت في السَّالبتين : لا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بحجر ولا شيء مِنَ النَّاطق بحجر، فالنتيجة كاذبة، ولو قلت في الكبرى: «لا شيء مِنَ الفرس بحجر لصدقت، ولو كانت الكبرى جزئية، لكان حاصل المقدمتين تنافي الأصغر وبعض ما صدق عليه الأكبر كما تقدم.
His statement: (And it is: the universality of its major) meaning: because if it were particular, it would be possible that the thing differing from the minor is some individuals of the major, and that does not necessitate the difference of the reality of the major from the minor; as our saying: 'Every rational is human, and some animal is not human', so the import of the two premises is that 'the rational' which is the minor is opposed to some individuals of the major, so the conclusion is falsified, for it produces 'Some rational is not an animal' which is false. And the conclusion: the conclusion is not true unless the minor is opposed to all individuals of the major; as our saying: 'Every rational is human, and no horse is human' produces 'No rational is a horse', which is true. His statement: (And it is that the difference in the concomitants indicates the difference in the things concomitated) for example if we say: 'Every human is an animal, and no stone is an animal', we have shown that the concomitants of human and stone are opposed; for the concomitant of human is animality, and the concomitant of stone is its negation, and these two concomitants never combine; because if human and stone combined, 'animal' and 'not animal' would combine due to the existence of animal with the existence of human, and the existence of not animal with the existence of stone, and that is the meaning of their saying: 'The conjunction of the two concomitated things necessitates the conjunction of their two concomitants', but the conjunction of animal and not animal is absurd, so the conjunction of human and stone leading to this absurdity is absurd, and that is the meaning of their saying: 'The opposition of the concomitants is evidence for the opposition of the things concomitated', and this is a universal rule. If it is said: It follows from their saying 'difference of concomitants indicates difference of the things concomitated' that the productivity of the second figure is based on the truth of this premise, and it follows from that that there remains no difference between it and the 'syllogism of equality' which they guard against entering into the definition of the syllogism, so this one would not enter and that one would exit. The answer: There is a difference between them, namely that the syllogism of equality its productivity depends on bringing an extraneous premise, while the second figure does not depend on bringing it, because they understand its import from its two premises and the existence of its meaning in them, like the first figure which is the clearest, for it is based on 'the concomitant of a concomitant is a concomitant' because it is understood from its two premises necessarily. His statement: (The difference necessitating sterility occurred) its explanation: that our saying for example: 'Every human is animal and every horse is animal' its conclusion is false, and if you said in the major: 'Every rational is animal', it would be true, and likewise if you said in the two negatives: 'No human is stone and no rational is stone', the conclusion is false, and if you said in the major: 'No horse is stone', it would be true. And if the major were particular, the import of the two premises would be the opposition of the minor to some of what the major applies to, as previously.
Source pages p. 684

Commentary
This passage explains the second condition (universality of the major) and the underlying principle (difference in concomitants indicates difference in the things concomitated). The commentator first argues that if the major is particular, the minor might be opposed only to some individuals of the major, not all, leading to a false conclusion. He gives an example: 'Every rational is human, some animal is not human' would produce 'Some rational is not animal' which is false. The correct conclusion requires the major to be universal. Then he explains the principle: the syllogism works because if the two extremes (minor and major) had different concomitants (e.g., animality and non-animality), then the extremes themselves must differ. He illustrates with 'Every human is animal, no stone is animal' showing that the concomitants (animal, not-animal) cannot coexist, so human and stone cannot coexist. He then addresses an objection: this principle seems to make the second figure dependent on an external premise (like the syllogism of equality), which would violate the definition of syllogism. He answers that the second figure does not require an extraneous premise because the principle is implicitly understood from the premises themselves, just as in the first figure the principle 'the concomitant of a concomitant is a concomitant' is understood. Finally, he gives further examples showing that without difference in quality, the conclusion is unstable. All examples are from common philosophical examples (human, animal, rational, horse, stone).

Key Terms
كلية الكبرى
شكل ثاني

Checkpoint
Why does a particular major lead to a false conclusion in the second figure?
A) Because the minor might oppose only some individuals of the major.
B) Because the middle term is not distributed.
C) Because the conclusion must be universal.
D) Because the major must be affirmative.

Key Takeaway
In the second figure, the major premise must be universal to ensure the minor term is opposed to all individuals of the major, yielding a necessary conclusion.

The Syllogism

The Principle of Difference in Concomitants and Its Defense
Explains the principle of difference in concomitants and defends it against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the principle of difference in concomitants and explain how it distinguishes the second figure from the syllogism of equality.

Core Idea
The second figure does not need an extra premise because the principle of difference in concomitants is implicit in its premises.

The commentator presents a powerful principle: if two things have different concomitants (necessary properties), then the things themselves must differ. This principle underlies the second figure's validity. An objector claims it makes the second figure dependent on an external premise, blurring the boundary between syllogism and non-syllogistic argument. The commentator responds by showing that the principle is understood from the premises themselves.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
The principle: 'Difference in concomitants indicates difference in the things concomitated.' For instance, human has the concomitant 'animality', stone has 'non-animality'. Therefore, human and stone must differ.
2
See how it works in a syllogism
Take 'Every human is animal, no stone is animal.' The middle term 'animal' shows that the concomitant of human (animality) is present, and of stone (non-animality) is absent. Hence, human and stone differ.
3
Understand the objection
The critic says this principle is an extraneous premise; if the second figure relies on it, it becomes like the 'syllogism of equality' which requires an extra statement to connect the terms.
4
Follow the answer
The commentator replies that in the second figure, the principle is not an additional premise because its meaning is already contained in the two premises. Just as in the first figure, the principle 'the concomitant of a concomitant is a concomitant' is understood directly from the premises.

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Four Productive Moods of the Second Figure: Derivation and Example
Author derives the four productive moods of the second figure and gives the first mood as an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the four productive moods of the second figure using both the method of acquirement (tahsil) and the method of elimination (isqat), and identify the first mood from its premises and conclusion.

Core Idea
Two methods, one result: exactly four moods in the second figure, because the major must be universal and premises must differ in quality.

In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises. The two conditions—difference in quality and universality of the major—limit the valid moods to only four. The author explains how to derive them systematically using two complementary methods, then gives the first mood as an example, showing how it relates to the second mood of the first figure through conversion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two conditions of the second figure
For a second-figure syllogism to be productive, the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. These conditions prevent the conclusion from being unstable (true sometimes affirmative, sometimes negative).
2
Apply the method of acquirement (tahsil)
If the major is affirmative, it can only combine with negative minors (either universal or particular). If the major is negative, it combines with affirmative minors. This gives four combinations: (1) major affirm + minor univ neg, (2) major affirm + minor part neg, (3) major neg + minor univ affirm, (4) major neg + minor part affirm.
This method directly constructs the moods from the conditions.
3
Verify using the method of elimination (isqat)
Start from the total possible premise combinations. Eliminate those where the premises have the same quality (eight combinations eliminated). Then eliminate those where the major is particular (four more eliminated). The remaining four are the productive moods.
This confirms that no other combinations are valid.
4
Examine the first mood example
The first mood is: major negative universal, minor affirmative universal, conclusion universal negative. The example given is a kalam proof: 'Every body is originated; nothing self-sufficient from the agent is originated; therefore, no body is self-sufficient from the agent.'
Check that the premises differ in quality and the major is universal.
5
Reduce the first mood to first figure by conversion
By converting the major premise (from 'no self-sufficient is originated' to 'no originated is self-sufficient'), the syllogism becomes the second mood of the first figure (Celarent). This shows the interrelation between figures.

فصل في الأشكال] ٦٨٥ الشَّرْطَيْنِ لَزِمَ الاِخْتِلَافُ المُوجِبُ لِلْعُقْم، وَهُوَ أَنْ يَكُونَ الحَقُّ فِي النَّتِيجَةِ تَارَةُ الإِيْجَابُ، وَأُخْرَى السَّلْبُ، فَالمُنْتِجُ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ بِمُقْتَضَى الشَّرْطَيْنِ أَرْبَعَةٌ أَيْضاً . أَمَّا بَيَانُهَا بِطَرِيقِ التَّحْصِيلٍ، فَلِأَنَّ كُبْرَاهُ كُلِّيَّةٌ قَطعاً، فَإِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةٌ أَنْتَجَتْ مَعَ السَّالِبَتَيْنِ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةٌ أَنْتَجَتْ مَعَ المُوجَبَتَيْنِ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ . وَأَمَّا بِطَرِيقِ الإِسْقَاطِ، فَلِأَنَّ اخْتِلَافَ الكَيْفِ أَسْقَطَ ثَمَانِيَةٌ، وَهِيَ المُوجَبَتَانِ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ أَوْ جُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ ، أَوِ الصُّغْرَى فَقَط كُلِّيَّةٌ أَوْ عَكْسُهُ، وَالصَّالِبَتَانِ كَذَلِكَ، وَكُلِّيةُ الكُبْرَى أَسْقَطَتْ أَرْبَعَةَ الكُبْرَى الجُزْئِيَّةَ السَّالِبَةَ مَعَ المُوجَبَتَيْنِ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ، وَالجُزْنِيَّةَ المُوجَبَةَ مَعَ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ السَّالِبَتَيْنِ . (۱) - الضَّرْبُ الأَوَّلُ مِنَ المُنْتِجَةِ : مِنْ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ وَالصُّغْرَى فَقَط مُوجَبَةٌ، يُنْتِجُ : كُلِّيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ ؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلُّ جِرْمٍ حَادِثْ، وَلَا شَيْء مِنَ الغَنِيِّ عَنِ الفَاعِلِ بِحَادِثٍ يُنْتِجُ : «لَا شَيْء مِنَ الجِرْمِ بِغَنِيٍّ عَنِ الفَاعِلِ، وَبِعَكْسِ كُبْرَاهُ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى الضَّرْبِ الثَّانِي مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ.
Section on the figures. The two conditions necessitate the difference that causes sterility, which is that the truth in the conclusion is sometimes affirmative and sometimes negative. So the productive among its moods by virtue of the two conditions are also four. As for their explanation by the method of acquirement: because its major is definitely universal; if it is affirmative, it produces with the two negative minors; and if it is negative, it produces with the two affirmative minors. As for by the method of elimination: because the difference in quality eliminates eight (combinations): the two affirmatives both universal or both particular, or the minor only universal or the opposite, and the two negatives likewise; and the universality of the major eliminates four: the major particular negative with the two affirmative minors, and the major particular affirmative with the two negative minors. (1) The first mood of the productive ones: from two universals with the minor only affirmative, it produces a universal negative; for example: 'Every body is originated, and nothing self-sufficient from the agent is originated' produces 'Nothing from the body is self-sufficient from the agent'; and by the converse of its major, it reverts to the second mood of the first figure.
Source pages p. 685

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four valid moods of the second figure. First, the author restates that without the conditions, the conclusion is unstable. Then he explains the four productive moods in two ways: (1) by the method of tahsil (acquirement): since the major is universal, if it is affirmative, it combines with the two negative minors (so the minor must be negative), and if major is negative, it combines with two affirmative minors. This yields four combinations: major affirmative + minor universal negative, major affirmative + minor particular negative, major negative + minor universal affirmative, major negative + minor particular affirmative. These correspond to the four moods. (2) By the method of isqat (elimination): starting from all possible combinations of two premises, the difference in quality eliminates eight (those where both premises have the same quality), and the universality of the major eliminates four (where major is particular), leaving four valid moods. He then gives the first mood: two universals with minor affirmative (major negative) producing a universal negative conclusion. The example is from kalam: 'Every body is originated, nothing self-sufficient from the agent is originated, therefore no body is self-sufficient from the agent.' This mood, by converting the major, reduces to the second mood of the first figure (Celarent).

Key Terms
طريق التحصيل (ṭarīq al-taḥṣīl): The method of acquirement; deriving the valid moods by directly considering the conditions.
طريق الإسقاط (ṭarīq al-isqāṭ): The method of elimination; deriving the valid moods by eliminating invalid combinations.
الضرب (al-ḍarb): Mood of a syllogism; a combination of premises in a given figure. [Note: Stable across tradition.]

The Syllogism

The Four Productive Moods of the Second Figure
Author enumerates the four valid moods of the second figure with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive moods of the second figure and reduce each to the first figure via conversion.

Core Idea
Second figure productivity requires differing premise quality and a universal major.

The second figure of the categorical syllogism has its own conditions for productivity. In this lesson, we will explore the four valid moods (ضروب) of this figure as enumerated by the author. Understanding these moods and their reduction to the first figure is essential for mastering syllogistic logic in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two conditions for productivity
The author states that for the second figure, the major premise must be universal, and the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). These conditions ensure the conclusion is necessary.
These conditions eliminate 12 of the 16 possible premise combinations.
2
Derive the four moods by enumeration
Since the major is universal, if it is affirmative it combines with the two negative minors (two moods); if negative, with the two affirmative minors (two more moods).
This is the 'method of enumeration' (tahṣīl).
3
Examine each mood's example and conclusion
The author gives three examples from kalam: (1) universal negative from universal affirmative minor and universal negative major; (2) universal negative from universal negative minor and universal affirmative major; (3) particular negative from particular affirmative minor and universal negative major; (4) particular negative from particular negative minor and universal affirmative major.
The first two moods yield universal conclusions, the last two yield particular negatives.
4
Review reduction to the first figure
Each mood can be reduced to a valid first figure mood by converting one or both premises and possibly swapping them. For example, mood (2) after converting the minor and swapping reduces to the second mood of the first figure.
This reduction confirms validity and links the figures.

فصل في الأشكال

الشَّرْطَيْنِ لَزِمَ الاِخْتِلَافُ المُوجِبُ لِلْعُقْمِ، وَهُوَ أَنْ يَكُونَ الحَقُّ فِي النَّتِيجَةِ تَارَةً الإِيْجَابَ، وَأُخْرَى السَّلْبَ، فَالْمُنْتِجُ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ بِمُقْتَضَى الشَّرْطَيْنِ أَرْبَعَةٌ أَيْضاً. أَمَّا بَيَانُهَا بِطَرِيقِ التَّحْصِيلِ، فَلِأَنَّ كُبْرَاهُ كُلِّيَّةٌ قَطْعاً، فَإِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةً أَنْتَجَتْ مَعَ السَّالِبَتَيْنِ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةً أَنْتَجَتْ مَعَ المُوجَبَتَيْنِ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ. وَأَمَّا بِطَرِيقِ الإِسْقَاطِ، فَلِأَنَّ اخْتِلَافَ الكَيْفِ أَسْقَطَ ثَمَانِيَةً، وَهِيَ: المُوجَبَتَانِ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ أَوْ جُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ، أَوِ الصُّغْرَى فَقَطْ كُلِّيَّةً أَوْ عَكْسَهُ، وَالسَّالِبَتَانِ كَذَلِكَ، وَكُلِّيَّةُ الكُبْرَى أَسْقَطَتْ أَرْبَعَةً: الكُبْرَى الجُزْئِيَّةَ السَّالِبَةَ مَعَ المُوجَبَتَيْنِ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ، وَالجُزْئِيَّةَ المُوجَبَةَ مَعَ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ السَّالِبَتَيْنِ.

(1) - الضَّرْبُ الأَوَّلُ مِنَ المُنْتِجَةِ: مِنْ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ وَالصُّغْرَى فَقَطْ مُوجَبَةٌ، يُنْتِجُ: كُلِّيَّةً سَالِبَةً؛ نَحْوُ: «كُلُّ جِرْمٍ حَادِثٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الغَنِيِّ عَنِ الفَاعِلِ بِحَادِثٍ» يُنْتِجُ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الجِرْمِ بِغَنِيٍّ عَنِ الفَاعِلِ». وَبِعَكْسِ كُبْرَاهُ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى الضَّرْبِ الثَّانِي مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ.

(2) - الضَّرْبُ الثَّانِي: مِنْ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ وَالكُبْرَى فَقَطْ مُوجَبَةٌ عَكْسُ الأَوَّلِ، يُنْتِجُ أَيْضاً: كُلِّيَّةً سَالِبَةً؛ نَحْوُ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الجَائِزِ بِغَنِيٍّ عَنِ الفَاعِلِ، وَكُلُّ قَدِيمٍ غَنِيٌّ عَنِ الفَاعِلِ» يُنْتِجُ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الجَائِزِ بِقَدِيمٍ». وَبِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى وَجَعْلِهَا كُبْرَى يَرْجِعُ لِثَانِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، ثُمَّ تُعْكَسُ النَّتِيجَةُ.

(3) - الضَّرْبُ الثَّالِثُ: مِنْ جُزْئِيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ صُغْرَى وَسَالِبَةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ كُبْرَى، يُنْتِجُ: جُزْئِيَّةً سَالِبَةً؛ كَقَوْلِنَا: «بَعْضُ المَوْجُودِ قَدِيمٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الجَائِزِ بِقَدِيمٍ» يُنْتِجُ: «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ المَوْجُودِ بِجَائِزٍ». وَبِعَكْسِ الكُبْرَى يَرْجِعُ لِرَابِعِ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ.

(4) - الضَّرْبُ الرَّابِعُ: مِنْ جُزْئِيَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ صُغْرَى وَكُلِّيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ كُبْرَى، يُنْتِجُ: جُزْئِيَّةً سَالِبَةً؛ كَقَوْلِنَا: «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ الصِّفَاتِ بِمُمْكِنٍ، وَكُلُّ حَادِثٍ مُمْكِنٌ» يُنْتِجُ: «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ الصِّفَاتِ بِحَادِثٍ». وَوَجْهُ تَرْتِيبِ هَذِهِ الأَضْرُبِ عَلَى مَا ذَكَرْنَا: أَنَّ الأَوَّلَيْنِ يُنْتِجَانِ الكُلِّيَّةَ فَقُدِّمَا، وَأَنَّ الأَوَّلَ وَالثَّالِثَ اشْتَمَلَا عَلَى صُغْرَى الكَامِلِ – أَعْنِي: المُوجَبَةَ – فَقُدِّمَ كُلٌّ عَلَى مَا يَلِيهِ.
Chapter on the figures. The two conditions necessitate differing quality which causes sterility, meaning that the truth in the conclusion is sometimes affirmative and sometimes negative. The productive moods according to the two conditions are also four. As for their demonstration by the method of enumeration: because its major premise is necessarily universal, so if it is affirmative it produces with the two negative minors, and if it is negative it produces with the two affirmative minors. As for by the method of elimination: because difference in quality eliminates eight: the two affirmatives both universal or both particular, or the minor only universal or its opposite, and the two negatives likewise; and the universality of the major eliminates four: the major particular negative with the two affirmative minors, and the particular affirmative with the two negative minors.

(1) The first productive mood: from two universals with the minor only affirmative, it produces a universal negative; e.g., 'Every body is originated, and nothing that is independent of an agent is originated' produces 'Nothing of the body is independent of an agent.' By converting its major it reduces to the second mood of the first figure.

(2) The second mood: from two universals with the major only affirmative, the opposite of the first, it also produces a universal negative; e.g., 'Nothing contingent is independent of an agent, and every eternal is independent of an agent' produces 'Nothing contingent is eternal.' By converting the minor and making it the major it reduces to the second of the first figure, then the conclusion is converted.

(3) The third mood: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, it produces a particular negative; e.g., 'Some existents are eternal, and nothing contingent is eternal' produces 'Some existent is not contingent.' By converting the major it reduces to the fourth of the first figure.

(4) The fourth mood: from a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, it produces a particular negative; e.g., 'Some attributes are not possible, and every originated is possible' produces 'Some attribute is not originated.' The rationale for ordering these moods as we did: the first two produce universals so they are placed first, and the first and third include the perfect minor – I mean the affirmative – so each is placed before what follows it.
Source pages p. 685

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four productive moods (adrib) of the second figure (al-shakl al-thani) of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by stating the two conditions for productivity in this figure: (1) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and (2) the major premise must be universal. These conditions ensure a valid conclusion without sterility (‘uqm). The author then explains how the four moods are derived via two methods: enumeration (tahsil) and elimination (isqat). By enumeration: since the major is universal, if it is affirmative it combines with each of the two negative minors (giving two moods); if negative, with each of the two affirmative minors (giving two more). By elimination: starting from all 16 possible premise pairs, difference in quality eliminates 8, and universality of the major eliminates 4, leaving 4 productive moods.

Next, he lists the four moods with examples from theology and physics: (1) Both premises universal, minor affirmative, major negative – produces universal negative. Example: Every body is originated; nothing independent of an agent is originated → no body is independent of an agent. This reduces to first figure second mood by converting the major. (2) Both universal, major affirmative — opposite of first — produces universal negative. Example: Nothing contingent is independent of an agent; every eternal is independent → nothing contingent is eternal. Reduces by converting minor and swapping premises, then converting conclusion. (3) Minor particular affirmative, major universal negative — produces particular negative. Example: Some existents are eternal; nothing contingent is eternal → some existent is not contingent. Reduces by converting major to first figure fourth mood. (4) Minor particular negative, major universal affirmative — produces particular negative. Example: Some attributes are not possible; every originated is possible → some attribute is not originated. The author then explains the ordering: the first two moods produce universal conclusions, so they are prioritised; within that, the first and third have affirmative minors (the 'perfect' minor), so each precedes its counterpart.

Disagreement on Need for Reduction of Second and Third Figures
Author notes the three scholarly positions on reduction of second and third figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three scholarly positions on whether the productive moods of the second and third figures need reduction to the first figure.

Core Idea
The self-evidence of syllogistic figures is debated; reduction is not always required.

In categorical syllogisms, only the first figure is universally accepted as self-evident. The second and third figures also produce valid conclusions, but scholars disagree on whether these conclusions are immediately obvious or need to be clarified by converting the syllogism to the first figure. This lesson presents the three positions and the one chosen by a major commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Read the passage and list the three scholarly positions: (1) all productive moods of second and third figures need reduction; (2) none need reduction (they are self-evident); (3) the second figure does not need reduction but the third does.
The third position is attributed to a commentary on al-Mukhtasar.
2
Understand 'bayyin bi-nafsihi'
This term means 'self-evident' or 'clear by itself.' For a syllogistic mood to be self-evident, its validity must be immediately apparent without any conversion or reduction. Consider why some scholars might view the second figure as self-evident due to its natural structure.
3
Examine the evidence for the middle position
The passage says 'the occurrence of the figure testifies to it' (wuqūʿ al-shakl). This likely refers to the fact that the second figure's premises naturally present a clear inference, whereas the third figure's structure is less immediately convincing. Reflect on how the arrangement of terms in the second figure (e.g., P in both premises) makes the conclusion obvious.
Think of a concrete example: All A are B, no C is B → no C is A. This is often immediately accepted.
4
Apply to Islamic arguments
When you encounter a syllogism in a Kalam or Fiqh text that is not in the first figure, decide whether reduction is needed based on the position you adopt. For practice, take a second-figure syllogism like 'Every created thing is contingent; nothing eternal is contingent; therefore nothing eternal is created.' According to the middle position, no reduction is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَنْبِيهٌ: اخْتُلِفَ فِي المُنْتِجِ مِنْ هَذَا الشَّكْلِ وَمِنَ الثَّالِثِ: هَلْ يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى بَيَانٍ بِرَدِّهِ إِلَى المُنْتِجِ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، أَوْ هُوَ بَيِّنٌ بِنَفْسِهِ لَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى رَدٍّ، أَوِ الثَّانِي لَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى رَدٍّ، وَالثَّالِثُ يَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهِ، وَهُوَ الَّذِي اخْتَارَهُ فِي شَرْحِ المُخْتَصَرِ، وَيَشْهَدُ لَهُ وُقُوعُ الشَّكْلِ.
A note: There is disagreement concerning the productive [moods] of this figure and of the third: whether they need clarification by reducing them to the productive [moods] of the first figure, or they are self-evident and do not need reduction, or the second [figure] does not need reduction while the third needs it – and this is the position chosen in the commentary on al-Mukhtasar, and the occurrence of the figure testifies to it.
Source pages p. 686

Commentary
This tanbīh (note) highlights a scholarly disagreement regarding the second and third figures of the categorical syllogism. The question is whether the productive moods of these figures are self-evident (bayyin bi-nafsihī) or require clarification by being reduced (radd) to the productive moods of the first figure, which is the only figure whose productivity is universally accepted as self-evident. The author presents three positions: (1) All productive moods of the second and third figures need reduction. (2) They are self-evident and need no reduction. (3) A middle position: the second figure does not need reduction, but the third does. The author states that the third position is the one chosen in 'Sharḥ al-Mukhtaṣar' (likely a commentary on al-Mukhtasar by Ibn al-Hājib or similar), and he adds that the 'occurrence of the figure' (wuqūʿ al-shakl) testifies to this view. This likely means that the structure of the second figure is so clear that its validity is immediately apparent, whereas the third figure requires some manipulation to see the necessity of the conclusion.

Key Terms
ردّ

The Second Mood of the Second Figure: Structure and Reduction
Author describes the second mood of the second figure and its reduction to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second mood of the second figure, construct its premises, and reduce it to the first figure via conversion.

Core Idea
Second mood: minor negative, major affirmative, conclusion negative; reduces to first figure by converting the minor.

In this lesson, we examine the second mood of the second categorical figure. The author defines its composition: two universal premises with the major only affirmative—the reverse of the first mood. An example from Kalam illustrates the mood, and the reduction to the first figure is explained. Understanding this mood sharpens your skill in handling syllogisms with the middle term as predicate in both premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
From the statement 'from two universals, with the major only affirmative', locate the minor premise (universal negative) and major premise (universal affirmative). In the example, the minor is 'Nothing of the contingent is independent of the agent' (E) and the major is 'Every eternal is independent of the agent' (A).
Both premises are universal; the major is affirmative, contrary to the first mood's negative major.
2
Determine the conclusion
The text says the mood produces a universal negative conclusion. In the example, the conclusion is 'Nothing of the contingent is eternal' (E). Verify that this follows from the premises.
The middle term is 'independent of the agent'.
3
Reduce by converting the minor
Convert the minor premise (E proposition) to its converse, which remains universal negative (E). Use this converted proposition as the major of a first-figure syllogism. Keep the original major as the minor.
The converted minor is 'Nothing of the independent of the agent is contingent'.
4
Obtain the first-figure conclusion
The resulting first-figure syllogism (Celarent: major universal negative, minor universal affirmative) yields a universal negative conclusion: 'Nothing of the independent of the agent is eternal'.
This conclusion is then converted to get the original conclusion: 'Nothing of the contingent is eternal'.
5
Understand why the major is not converted
The commentary explains that the major is affirmative, and its converse would be particular (affirmative), which cannot serve as a major premise in the first figure (which requires a universal major for Celarent).
Thus only the minor is converted for reduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢) - الضَّرْبُ الثَّانِي : مِنْ كُلِّيَتَيْنِ وَالكُبْرَى فَقَط مُوجَبَةٌ عَكْسُ الأَوَّلِ، يُنْتِجُ أَيْضاً : كُلِّيَّةً سَالِبَةٌ؛ نَحْوُ : «لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الجَائِزِ بِغَنِيٌّ عَنِ الفَاعِلِ، وَكُلُّ قَدِيمِ غَنِيٌّ عَنِ الفَاعِلِ يُنْتِجُ لَا شَيْء مِنَ الجَائِزِ بِقَدِيمٍ ، وَبِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى وَجَعْلِهَا كُبْرَى يَرْجِعُ لِثَانِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، ثُمَّ تُعْكَسُ النَّتيجة . قوله : (وَبِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى وَجَعْلِهَا كُبْرَى) وإنَّما لم تنعكس الكبرى؛ لأنها موجبة، فعكسا جزئية، وهي لا تصح كبرى للأول.
The second mood: from two universals, with the major only affirmative, contrary of the first, also produces a universal negative; example: 'Nothing of the contingent is independent of the agent, and every eternal is independent of the agent' produces 'Nothing of the contingent is eternal.' And by converting the minor and making it major, it returns to the second mood of the first figure, then the result is converted. His statement: 'And by converting the minor...' — the major is not converted because it is affirmative, so its converse is particular, and that is not valid as a major for the first figure.
Source pages p. 686

Commentary
This passage defines the second mood of the second figure. The second figure has four moods; the first we saw earlier. Here the second mood consists of two universal premises (minor and major), but with the major only affirmative — this is the reverse of the first mood (which had a negative major). It produces a universal negative conclusion. The example given uses the subject 'contingent' (jā'iz) and 'eternal' (qadīm) with the predicate 'independent of the agent'. The author then explains that by converting the minor premise (making the predicate into the subject) and using it as the major of the first figure, we can reduce this mood to the second mood of the first figure (Celarent: universal negative major, universal affirmative minor, producing universal negative). The commentary clarifies why the major is not converted: because it is affirmative, its converse would be particular, and a particular premise cannot serve as the major in the first figure's moods.

شكل ثان
عكس

Key Takeaway
The second mood of the second figure has a universal negative minor and universal affirmative major, producing a universal negative conclusion, and reduces to the first figure by converting the minor.

Third Mood of the Second Figure and Its Reduction
Third mood of the second figure and its reduction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third mood of the second figure and reduce it to the first figure by converting the major premise.

Core Idea
The third mood of the second figure yields a particular negative after converting the major premise.

In this lesson, we examine the third mood of the second figure. The text describes its premises and conclusion, and shows how to reduce it to the first figure via conversion of the major premise. Understanding this reduction is key to mastering syllogistic inference in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The minor premise is a particular affirmative (Some A is B). The major premise is a universal negative (No C is B). The middle term is 'eternal'.
Example: 'Some of what exists is eternal' (جزئية موجبة صغرى) and 'Nothing contingent is eternal' (سالبة كلية كبرى).
2
Derive the conclusion
Given the universal negative major and particular affirmative minor, the conclusion is a particular negative (Some A is not C). In the example: 'Some of what exists is not contingent.'
The conclusion is a particular negative proposition (جزئية سالبة).
3
Convert the major premise
Convert the universal negative major 'No C is B' to 'No B is C'. This yields a universal negative major for the reduced syllogism.
Conversion of 'No C is B' is valid and results in 'No B is C'.
4
Identify the resulting first figure mood
After conversion, the premises become: minor (Some A is B) and major (No B is C). This is the fourth mood of the first figure (which is valid and yields the same conclusion).
The fourth mood of the first figure has a universal negative major and particular affirmative minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ۳ ) - الضَّرْبُ الثَّالِثُ : مِنْ جُزْئِيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ صُغْرَى وَسَالِبَةٍ كُلِّيَّة كُبْرَى، يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا : بَعْضُ المَوْجُودِ قَدِيمٌ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الجَائِزِ بِقَدِيمٍ يُنْتِجُ : «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ المَوْجُودِ بِجَائِز»، وَبِعَكْسِ الكُبْرَى يَرْجِعُ لِرَابِعِ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ . قوله : (كَقَوْلِنَا : بَعْضُ المَوْجُودِ قَدِيمٌ مثله : بعض الموجود ممكن، ولا شيء مِنَ القديم بممكن فـ بعض الموجود ليس بقديم .
The third mood: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, produces a particular negative; as our saying: 'Some of what exists is eternal, and nothing of the contingent is eternal' produces 'Not some of what exists is contingent.' And by converting the major, it returns to the fourth mood of the first figure. His statement: 'As our saying: Some of what exists is eternal' — similar to that is: 'Some of what exists is possible, and nothing of the eternal is possible, so some of what exists is not eternal.'
Source pages p. 686

Commentary
This passage describes the third mood of the second figure. It consists of a particular affirmative minor premise and a universal negative major premise. The conclusion is a particular negative. The example is: 'Some of what exists is eternal, and nothing contingent is eternal' — therefore 'Not some (i.e., some not) of what exists is contingent.' The reduction to the first figure is done by converting the major premise (which is universal negative) to a universal negative (since conversion of 'No A is B' is 'No B is A'), and then it becomes the fourth mood of the first figure. The commentary adds an alternative example: 'Some of what exists is possible, and nothing eternal is possible, so some of what exists is not eternal.'

Key Terms
جزئية موجبة
سالبة كلية

Key Takeaway
The third mood of the second figure (Festino) uses a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major to produce a particular negative conclusion, reducible to the first figure by converting the major.

Fourth Mood of the Second Figure: Irreducible Syllogism
Author describes the fourth mood and explains irreducibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth mood of the second figure and explain why it cannot be reduced to the first figure using conversion rules.

Core Idea
The fourth mood of the second figure is irreducible because its premises convert to forms unsuitable for the first figure.

We have seen four moods in the second figure. The first three can be reduced to the first figure by conversion or other operations. However, the fourth mood stands apart. Its premises—a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major—yield a particular negative conclusion, but attempts to convert them fail to meet the strict requirements of the first figure. Let us examine why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components
Write down the minor premise, major premise, and conclusion exactly as given. Note their quantity and quality: minor is particular negative (juzʾiyya sāliba), major is universal affirmative (kulliyya mūjaba), conclusion is particular negative.
In the example: minor = 'Not some attributes are possible' (particular negative), major = 'Every temporally originated thing is possible' (universal affirmative), conclusion = 'Not some attributes are temporally originated'.
2
Test reduction by conversion
Try to convert the major premise (universal affirmative) to fit as a major in the first figure. Its converse is a particular affirmative, but the first figure requires a universal major. Similarly, the minor (particular negative) has no valid converse.
The standard conversion rules: universal affirmative converts to particular affirmative; particular negative does not convert validly.
3
Conclude irreducibility
Since neither premise can be converted to fulfill the first figure's conditions, this mood is inherently irreducible. The commentator omitted the usual reduction discussion for this reason.
The first figure requires the major premise to be universal and the minor to be affirmative. This mood cannot meet those conditions through conversion alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ٤ ) - الضَّرْبُ الرَّابِعُ : مِنْ جُزْئِيَّة سَالِبَةٍ صُغْرَى، وَكُلِّيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ كُبْرَى، يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ ; كَقَوْلِنَا : لَيْسَ بَعْضُ الصِّفَاتِ بِمُمْكِنٍ، وَكُلُّ حَادِثِ مُمْكِنٌ يُنْتِجُ : «لَيْسَ بَعْضُ الصِّفَاتِ بِحَادِثِ . وَوَجْهُ تَرْتِيبِ هَذِهِ الأَضْرُبِ عَلَى مَا ذَكَرْنَا : أَنَّ الأَوَّلَيْن يُنْتِجَانِ الكُلِّيَّةَ فَقُدِّمَا، وَأَنَّ الأَوَّلَ وَالثَّالِثَ اشْتَمَلَا عَلَى صُغْرَى الكَامِل - أَعْنِي : المُوجَبَةَ , فَقُدِّمَ كُلٌّ عَلَى مَا يَلِيهِ . قوله : (الضَّرْبُ الرَّابِعُ لمَّا كان هذا الضَّرب لا يمكن رجوعه للأوّل أصلاً، ترك الشارح فيه التّعرُّض لذلك، وذلك لأنَّ كبراه كلّيّة موجبة فلو عكستها لانعكست جزئية موجبة، وهي لا تصلح لكبرى الأوَّل، وصغراه جزئيّة سالبة ولا عكس لها .
The fourth mood: from a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, produces a particular negative; as our saying: 'Not some of the attributes are possible, and every temporally originated thing is possible' produces 'Not some of the attributes are temporally originated.' And the reason for ordering these moods as we did: the first two produce universals so they were placed first; and the first and third include the perfect minor—I mean the affirmative—so each was placed after its adjacent. His statement: 'The fourth mood' — since this mood cannot be reduced to the first figure at all, the commentator left off discussing that, because its major is universal affirmative; if you convert it, it becomes particular affirmative, which is not valid as a major for the first figure; and its minor is particular negative, and there is no conversion for it.
Source pages p. 686

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth mood of the second figure. Its premises are a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion. The example uses 'attributes' (ṣifāt) and 'possible' (mumkin) with 'temporally originated' (ḥādith). The author then explains the ordering of the four moods: the first two produce universal conclusions, so they are prioritized; among these, the first and third have an affirmative minor (the 'perfect' minor), so they are placed in that order. The commentary adds that this mood cannot be reduced to the first figure because the major, being universal affirmative, converts to a particular affirmative, which cannot serve as a major in the first figure (which requires a universal major). The minor is particular negative and has no direct conversion (since the conversion of a particular negative is not valid). Thus no reduction is possible.

Key Terms
جزئية سالبة
كليّة موجبة
الضرب الرابع
عكس

C) Because the minor is universal
D) Because the conclusion is universal

Key Takeaway
The fourth mood of the second figure is irreducible because its premises cannot be validly converted to satisfy the first figure's requirement of a universal major and an affirmative minor.

Three Opinions on Self-Evidence of Syllogistic Figures
Three opinions on self-evidence of second and third figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three positions on the self-evidence of second and third figure conclusions and evaluate the evidence for the preferred view.

Core Idea
Syllogistic self-evidence is graded: first is axiomatic, fourth is not, the rest are debated.

Not all syllogistic figures are equal in epistemic clarity. While the first figure is universally accepted as self-evident and the fourth as not, the status of the second and third figures has been a matter of dispute. In this passage, al-Bajuri presents three distinct opinions and offers evidence for the view that the second figure is self-evident, a view he attributes to the author of Sharh al-Mukhtasar. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to assess the demonstrative strength of arguments in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Look for the three contrasting views: (1) both second and third need reduction, (2) both are self-evident, (3) second is self-evident but third needs reduction. The commentator states that the first view is held by the majority, the second is derived from al-Ghazali, and the third is preferred by some.
2
Understand the evidence for the preferred view
The preferred view (third position) is supported by citing common speech: the example of identifying someone by negation ('This is not so-and-so because it is tall and so-and-so is not tall') shows that the second figure is used naturally without conscious reduction. This suggests its conclusion is self-evident.
The example is a second figure syllogism with a negative mood (Camestres or Cesare).
3
Consider the implications of al-Ghazali's statement
Some scholars derive the second position (both figures self-evident) from al-Ghazali's claim that all three figures are found in the Quran. Reflect on why al-Ghazali might say that: if the Quran uses second and third figures, their conclusions must be clear enough to be persuasive without technical reduction.
4
Assess the argument for the majority view
The majority requires reduction for both figures, implying that only the first figure yields self-evident conclusions. Consider the logical conservatism: only Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio are fully transparent. This view demands that any non-first-figure argument be converted to establish certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَنْبِية: - :- اخْتُلِفَ فِي المُنْتِج مِنْ هَذَا الشَّكْلِ وَمِنَ الثَّالِثِ : هَلْ يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى بَيَانٍ بِرَدِّهِ إِلَى المُنْتِجِ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ ، أَوْ هُوَ بَيِّنٌ بِنَفْسِهِ لَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى رَدُّ، أَوِ الثَّانِي لَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى رَدُّ، وَالثَّالِثُ يَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهِ، وَهُوَ الَّذِي اخْتَارَهُ فِي شَرْحِ المُخْتَصَرِ»، وَيَشْهَدُ لَهُ وُقُوعُ الشَّكْلِ الثَّانِي كَثِيراً فِي كَلَام العَوَامُ؛ كَأَنْ يَقُولَ أَحَدُهُمْ : هَذَا فُلَانٌ مُقْبِلاً، فَيَقُولُ لَهُ الآخَرُ : هَذَا لَيْسَ بِفُلَانٍ، هَذَا طَوِيلٌ، وَفُلَانٌ لَيْسَ بِطَوِيلٍ، وَذَلِكَ يَدُلُّ عَلَى قُرْبِ إِنْتَاجِهِ، وَالأَوَّلُ قَوْلُ الأَكْثَرِ، وَالثَّانِي أَخَذَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ مِنْ قَوْلِ الغَزَالِيّ : إِنَّ الأَشْكَالَ الثَّلَاثَةَ مَوْجُودَةٌ فِي القُرْآنِ». قوله: (تَنْبِيةُ : اخْتُلِفَ ... إلخ) اتَّفقوا على أنَّ الشَّكل الأوَّل بيِّن بنفسه، وعلى أنَّ الرَّابع غير بين بنفسه، وإنَّما اختلفوا فيما سواهما على ثلاثة مذاهب ذكرها الشارح .
Note: There is disagreement concerning the product of this figure (second) and the third: does it require clarification by reduction to the product of the first figure, or is it self-evident not needing reduction, or the second does not need reduction while the third does—and that is what he chose in Sharh al-Mukhtasar—and evidence for that is the frequent occurrence of the second figure in common speech, as when one says: 'This is so-and-so approaching,' and another says: 'This is not so-and-so, this is tall, and so-and-so is not tall,' and that indicates the closeness of its production. The first is the opinion of the majority, and the second is taken by some from al-Ghazali's statement that the three figures are found in the Quran. His statement: 'Note: There is disagreement...' — They agree that the first figure is self-evident and that the fourth is not self-evident, and they only disagree about the other two (second and third) on three views as mentioned by the commentator.
Source pages p. 686

Commentary
This note (تنبيه) addresses an epistemological question: Are the conclusions of the second and third figures self-evident (بين بنفسه) or do they require demonstration by reduction to the first figure? Three positions are presented. The first position says they need reduction. The second says they are self-evident without reduction. The third (preferred by the author of Sharh al-Mukhtasar) says the second figure is self-evident but the third is not. Evidence for the second figure's self-evidence is its common occurrence in everyday speech, as in the example: someone says 'This is so-and-so approaching' and the responder says 'This is not so-and-so, this is tall, and so-and-so is not tall' — which implicitly uses a syllogism of the second figure. The commentary adds that all agree the first figure is self-evident and the fourth is not, and the disagreement is only about the second and third.

Key Terms
بين بنفسه
رد

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Second figure

D) Fourth figure

Key Takeaway
The self-evidence of syllogistic figures is graded: first is axiomatic, fourth is not, and the second and third are debated, with the preferred view granting self-evidence only to the second.

Conditions for Productive Third-Figure Syllogisms
Author states two conditions for productive third figure and lists six moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a third-figure categorical syllogism meets the two necessary conditions for productivity and identify the six valid moods.

Core Idea
Third figure needs an affirmative minor and at least one universal premise to yield a conclusion.

In this passage, the author lays out two conditions that must be satisfied for a third-figure syllogism to be productive (i.e., yield a valid conclusion). The first concerns quality: the minor premise must be affirmative. The second concerns quantity: one of the two premises must be universal. If either condition is violated, the syllogism becomes sterile (ʿuqum). Understanding these conditions allows you to recognize the six valid moods of the third figure and apply them in Islamic reasoning contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In a third-figure syllogism, the middle term is the subject of both premises. Find this term; it appears twice in the premises.
2
Check the minor premise's quality
The minor premise (the one containing the smaller term as predicate) must be affirmative. If it is negative, the subject and middle become mutually exclusive, blocking inference.
Example from the text: if the minor is 'No human is a horse', the terms are separated.
3
Check the quantity of premises
At least one premise must be universal. If both are particular, the sub-parts referred to may differ, preventing the terms from meeting.
Example: 'Some animal is human; some animal is rational' does not guarantee overlap.
4
Determine if conditions are met
If the minor is affirmative and at least one premise universal, the syllogism is productive. Otherwise, it is sterile (ʿuqum).
The six productive moods satisfy both conditions; e.g., Disamis (IAI) has an affirmative minor and one universal premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (۹۱) وَالثَّالِثُ : الإِيجَابُ فِي صُغْرَاهُمَا وَأَنْ تُرَى كُلِّيَّةٌ إِحْدَاهُمَا ذَكَرَ لِإِنْتاجِ الشَّكْلِ الثَّالِثِ أَيْضاً شَرْطَيْنِ : (۱) - أَحَدُهُمَا : بِاعْتِبَارِ الكَيْفِ، وَهُوَ : إِيْجَابُ صُغْرَاهُ ; لأَنَّهَا لَوْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةٌ، أَفَادَتِ المُبَايَنَةَ الكُلِّيَّةَ أَوِ الجُزْيَّةَ بَيْنَ الأَصْغَرِ وَالأَوْسَطِ المَحْكُومٍ عَلَيْهِ فِي الكُبْرَى بِالْأَكْبَرِ إِيْجَابَاً أَوْ سَلْباً، وَالحُكْمُ عَلَى أَحَدِ المُتَبَايَنَيْنِ لَا يُوجِبُ [ج/ ٣٦] الحُكْمَ عَلَى الآخَرِ، وَلِهَذَا حَصَلَ الاِخْتِلَافُ المُوجِبُ لِلْعُقْم. (۲) - وَثَانِي الشَّرْطَيْنِ : بِاعْتِبَارِ الكَمْ، وَهُوَ : كُلِّيَّةٌ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَإِلَّا جَازَ أَنْ يَكُونَ البَعْضُ المَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ فِي الصُّغْرَى غَيْرَ البَعْضِ المَحْكُوم عَلَيْهِ فِي الكُبْرَى، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ التِقَاء الأَصْغَرِ وَالأَكْبَرِ، وَلِهَذَا أَيْضاً حَصَلَ الاِخْتِلَافُ المُوجِبُ لِلْعُقْمِ عِنْدَ فَوَاتِ هَذَا الشَّرْطِ . وَبِالشَّرْطَيْنِ تَعْلَمُ أَنَّ ضُرُوبَهُ [٥٦/١] المُنْتِجَةَ سِتَّةُ : قوله : (كَأَنْ يَقُولَ) منه قول بعض العوام: فلان كريم، فيقول له الرَّاد عليه : «الكريم لا يردُّ سائله، وفلان يرده، وهو شائع بينهم جدا، غير أنَّهم يقدمون كبراه. قول النَّاظم : (وَالثَّالِثُ) : قوله : (الِاخْتِلَافُ المُوجِبُ لِلْعُقْم) مثاله إذا كانت الكبرى موجبة : لا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بفرس، وكلُّ إنسان حيوان أو ناطق»، وإذا كانت سالبة : لا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بفرس، ولا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بصهَّال أو حماره والحقُّ في الأوليَّين الإيجاب، وفي الأخريين السلب. قوله : (وَلِهَذَا أَيْضاً حَصَلَ الِاخْتِلَافُ المُوجِبُ لِلْعُقْمِ عِنْدَ فَوَاتِ هَذَا الشَّرْطِ) أي: كقولنا في إيجاب الكبرى: بعض الحيوان إنسان، وبعضه ناطق أو فرس، وفي سلبها : بعض الحيوان ضاحكٌ، وبعضه ليس بناطق أو صهال».
His statement: (91) 'And the third: affirmation in its minor and that one of them be universal' — he mentioned for the productivity of the third figure also two conditions: (1) One of them concerning quality: that its minor be affirmative; because if it were negative, it would indicate complete or partial mutual exclusivity between the smaller and the middle term which is judged in the major with the larger term affirmatively or negatively, and a judgment upon one of two mutually exclusive things does not necessitate judgment upon the other, and thus the discrepancy causing sterility occurs. (2) The second condition concerning quantity: that one of the two premises be universal; otherwise it is possible that the part judged in the minor is other than the part judged in the major, so the meeting of the smaller and larger does not necessarily occur, and thus also the discrepancy causing sterility occurs when this condition is absent. And by these two conditions you know that its productive moods are six. His statement: 'like when he says' — from that is the saying of some common people: 'So-and-so is generous,' and the responder says to him 'the generous does not turn away his beggar, and so-and-so turns him away' — and this is very common among them, except that they precede its major. The poet's saying: 'And the third' — his statement: 'the discrepancy causing sterility' — example when the major is affirmative: 'No human is a horse, and every human is an animal or rational'; and when the major is negative: 'No human is a horse, and no human is a whinnying creature or an ass' — and the truth in the first two is affirmative, and in the last two is negative. His statement: 'And also thus the discrepancy causing sterility occurs when this condition is absent' — that is, like our saying when the major is affirmative: 'Some animal is human, and some of it is rational or a horse', and when it is negative: 'Some animal is laughing, and some of it is not rational or whinnying'.
Source pages p. 687

Commentary
This passage lays out the two necessary conditions for the third figure to produce a valid conclusion. The first condition concerns the quality of the minor premise: it must be affirmative. If the minor were negative, it would indicate a complete or partial disjunction between the subject of the minor (the smaller term) and the middle term. Since the major premise predicates the larger term of the middle, the subject of the minor is separated from the middle, so no judgment about the larger term follows. An example: if the minor is 'No human is a horse' and the major is 'Every human is an animal', you cannot conclude anything about 'horse' and 'animal' because the minor separates them. The second condition concerns quantity: at least one premise must be universal. If both are particular, the part of the subject referred to in the minor might be different from the part referred to in the major, so the terms may not connect. With these two conditions, we know that the third figure has six productive moods. The commentary provides examples from common speech (like 'the generous does not turn away his beggar') and formal logical examples to illustrate what happens when conditions are violated (leading to 'discrepancy causing sterility' - i.e., no valid conclusion).

Key Terms
عقم (ʿuqum): Sterility or failure to produce a conclusion; a syllogism that does not yield a valid inference.
مباينة (mubāyana): Complete or partial mutual exclusivity between terms; if two terms are mutually exclusive, no common subject can be predicated of both.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the minor premise in the third figure is negative?
A) The conclusion is negative
B) No conclusion is possible (ʿuqum)
C) The conclusion is particular
D) The syllogism becomes first figure

Key Takeaway
For a third-figure syllogism to be productive, the minor must be affirmative and at least one premise must be universal; otherwise sterility results.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Test the syllogism: 'Some animals are not human; every animal is mortal; therefore what?' Does it meet the conditions? Identify any violation.
?
Construct a valid third-figure syllogism on a fiqh topic (e.g., about the permissibility of a transaction) that satisfies both conditions.

The Second Figure in Everyday Speech
Author notes the frequency of the second figure in common speech and cites al-Ghazali

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second figure of categorical syllogism in natural language arguments and explain why it is considered productive.

Core Idea
The second figure is not just a textbook curiosity—it appears in natural arguments daily.

The author of this passage makes a striking observation: the second figure of the categorical syllogism is not merely a technical form studied by logicians; it is actually frequent in the everyday speech of common people. To illustrate, he gives a concrete example of a typical exchange. This lesson will guide you in recognizing the second figure in ordinary discourse and understanding its logical productivity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises in the example
The example involves a dialogue. One person says 'This is so-and-so approaching.' The other replies 'This is not so-and-so, this is tall, and so-and-so is not tall.' The argument can be recast as two premises: 'This is tall' and 'So-and-so is not tall.'
2
Locate the middle term
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises and links them. Here, 'tall' (طويل) appears as the predicate in both premises: 'this is tall' and 'so-and-so is not tall.' So 'tall' is the middle term.
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in each premise.
3
Determine the figure
Since the middle term is predicate in both premises, the syllogism belongs to the second figure. The first premise has 'this' as subject and 'tall' as predicate; the second premise has 'so-and-so' as subject and 'not tall' as predicate. The conclusion, though not explicitly stated in the passage, is implied: 'this is not so-and-so.'
The conclusion is implied but not fully articulated in the quote.
4
Evaluate productivity
The author says the example shows the closeness of the second figure to productivity (intāj). A syllogism is productive if it yields a necessary conclusion from the premises. Even though the premises are singular (about specific individuals), the form itself can be made universal. This demonstrates that the second figure is naturally productive in common reasoning.
Productivity here means the syllogism can generate a valid conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأشكال الثَّانِي كَثِيراً فِي كَلَام العَوَامُ؛ كَأَنْ يَقُولَ أَحَدُهُمْ : هَذَا فُلَانٌ مُقْبِلاً، فَيَقُولُ لَهُ الآخَرُ : هَذَا لَيْسَ بِفُلَانٍ، هَذَا طَوِيلٌ، وَفُلَانٌ لَيْسَ بِطَوِيلٍ، وَذَلِكَ يَدُلُّ عَلَى قُرْبِ إِنْتَاجِهِ، وَالأَوَّلُ قَوْلُ الأَكْثَرِ، وَالثَّانِي أَخَذَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ مِنْ قَوْلِ الغَزَالِيّ : إِنَّ الأَشْكَالَ الثَّلَاثَةَ مَوْجُودَةٌ فِي القُرْآنِ».
Chapter on the figures. The second [figure] is frequent in the speech of common people: such that one of them says 'This is so-and-so approaching,' and the other says to him 'This is not so-and-so, this is tall, and so-and-so is not tall.' That indicates its closeness to productivity. The first is the opinion of the majority, and the second is taken by some from al-Ghazali's statement that the three figures are found in the Quran.
Source pages p. 687

Commentary
This passage is a remark on the second figure of the syllogism. The author notes that the second figure appears frequently in everyday speech of common people. The example given is a typical argument: one person says 'This is so-and-so approaching,' and another responds 'This is not so-and-so, this is tall, and so-and-so is not tall.' This example illustrates that the second figure is close to being productive (i.e., yields a conclusion). The author then mentions that the first view (that the second figure is frequent) is the opinion of the majority, while the second view (that the three figures are found in the Quran) is attributed to al-Ghazali. This shows that logicians recognize the prevalence of the second figure in natural reasoning.

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Two Conditions for a Productive Third-Figure Syllogism
Author explains the two conditions for the third figure's productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a third-figure categorical syllogism satisfies the conditions of an affirmative minor and at least one universal premise, thus avoiding sterility.

Core Idea
A third-figure syllogism needs an affirmative minor and one universal premise to avoid sterility.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism has specific requirements. Unlike the first figure, the middle term is subject in both premises, so the rules differ. The author explains two conditions: one about quality (the minor must be affirmative) and one about quantity (at least one premise must be universal). Without these, the syllogism is sterile (ʿuqim). In this lesson, we will unpack why these conditions are necessary and how to apply them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term in the third figure
In the third figure, the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ) is the subject in both the major and minor premises. This distinguishes it from the first figure. Recognize this structure before checking conditions.
2
Understand why the minor must be affirmative
If the minor premise were negative, it would create a separation (mubāyana) between the minor term and the middle term. Since the major premise judges the middle term (affirmatively or negatively), a judgment on one separated thing does not necessitate a judgment on the other, causing sterility.
The author's reasoning: 'لأنها لو كانت سالبة، أفادت المباينة الكلية أو الجزئية بين الأصغر والأوسط'
3
Understand why at least one premise must be universal
If both premises were particular, the part of the minor term judged in the minor could be different from the part of the major term judged in the major. This prevents the necessary connection between minor and major terms, again causing sterility.
The author: 'وإلا جاز أن يكون البعض المحكوم عليه في الصغرى غير البعض المحكوم عليه في الكبرى'
4
Test a third-figure syllogism against the conditions
Take a third-figure syllogism and check: (1) Is the minor premise affirmative? (2) Is at least one premise universal? If both conditions hold, the syllogism is potentially productive; if not, it is sterile.
Example from the passage: 'Every wheat is a staple, every wheat is usury-able' – minor is affirmative, both premises are universal, so it meets the conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَالثَّالِثُ : الإِيجَابُ فِي صُغْرَاهُمَا وَأَنْ تُرَى كُلِّيَّةٌ إِحْدَاهُمَا ذَكَرَ لِإِنْتاجِ الشَّكْلِ الثَّالِثِ أَيْضاً شَرْطَيْنِ : (۱) أَحَدُهُمَا : بِاعْتِبَارِ الكَيْفِ، وَهُوَ : إِيْجَابُ صُغْرَاهُ ؛ لأَنَّهَا لَوْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةٌ، أَفَادَتِ المُبَايَنَةَ الكُلِّيَّةَ أَوِ الجُزْيَّةَ بَيْنَ الأَصْغَرِ وَالأَوْسَطِ المَحْكُومٍ عَلَيْهِ فِي الكُبْرَى بِالْأَكْبَرِ إِيْجَابَاً أَوْ سَلْباً، وَالحُكْمُ عَلَى أَحَدِ المُتَبَايَنَيْنِ لَا يُوجِبُ الحُكْمَ عَلَى الآخَرِ، وَلِهَذَا حَصَلَ الاِخْتِلَافُ المُوجِبُ لِلْعُقْم. (۲) وَثَانِي الشَّرْطَيْنِ : بِاعْتِبَارِ الكَمْ، وَهُوَ : كُلِّيَّةٌ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَإِلَّا جَازَ أَنْ يَكُونَ البَعْضُ المَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ فِي الصُّغْرَى غَيْرَ البَعْضِ المَحْكُوم عَلَيْهِ فِي الكُبْرَى، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ التِقَاء الأَصْغَرِ وَالأَكْبَرِ، وَلِهَذَا أَيْضاً حَصَلَ الاِخْتِلَافُ المُوجِبُ لِلْعُقْمِ عِنْدَ فَوَاتِ هَذَا الشَّرْطِ . وَبِالشَّرْطَيْنِ تَعْلَمُ أَنَّ ضُرُوبَهُ المُنْتِجَةَ سِتَّةُ : أَمَّا بِطَرِيقِ التَّحْصِيلِ، فَلِأَنَّ صُغْرَاهُ إِذَا كَانَتْ كُلِّيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ أَنْتَجَتْ مَعَ الكُبْرِيَّاتِ المَحْصُورَاتِ الأَرْبَعِ ، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ جُزْيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ أَنْتَجَتْ مَعَ الكُليّتيْنِ. وَأَمَّا بِطَرِيقِ الإِسْقَاطِ، فَلِأَنَّ إِيْجَابَ الصُّغْرَى أَسْقَطَ ثَمَانِيَةٌ كَمَا مَرَّ فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، وَكُلِّيَّةَ إِحْدَاهِمَا أَسْقَطَتِ اثْنَيْنِ الجُزْئِيَّةِ المُوجَبَةِ الصُّغْرَى مَعَ الجُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ الكُبْرَيَّيْنِ ، وَلَا يُنْتِجُ هَذَا الشَّكْلُ إِلَّا جُزْئِيَّةٌ أَبَداً ؛ الأَنَّ أَخَصَّ الضُّرُوبِ المُنْتِجَةِ المُرَكَبِ مِنْ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ، أَوِ الثَّانِيَةَ سَالِبَةٌ وَهُمَا لَا يُنْتِجَانِ الكُلِّيَّةَ؛ لِجَوَازِ أَنْ يَكُونَ الأَصْغَرُ أَعَمَّ مِنَ الأَكْبَرِ، فَلَا يَصِحُ حَمْلُ الأَكْبَرِ عَلَيْهِ كُلِّيًّا إِيْجَاباً وَلَا سَلْباً ; كَقَوْلِنَا : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ، أَوْ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ، وَإِذَا لَمْ تُنْتِجِ الكُلِّيَّتَانِ كُلِّيَّةٌ لَمْ يُنْتِجْهَا غَيْرُهُمَا ; لأَنَّ مَا لَا يَلْزَمُ الأَخَصُّ لَا يَلْزَمُ الأَعَمُّ. (۱) الضَّرْبُ الأَوَّلُ مِنَ السِّنَّةِ المُنْتِجَةِ : مِنْ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ، يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ , نَحْو : كُلُّ بُرِّ مُقْتَاتٌ، وَكُلُّ بُرِّ رَبَوِيٌّ» يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ المُقْتَاتِ رَبَوِيُّ». (۲) الضَّرْبُ الثَّانِي : مِنْ كُلِّيَتَيْنِ وَالكُبْرَى سَالِبَةٌ , يُنتِجُ : جُزْيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ ; نَحْوُ : «كُلُّ مُتَحَيِّز مَوْجُودٌ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ المُتَحَيِّزِ بِقَدِيمٍ يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ المَوْجُودِ لَيْسَ بِقَدِيمٍ . (۳) الضَّرْبُ الثَّالِثُ : مِنْ جُزْئِيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ صُغْرَى وَكُلِّيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ كُبْرَى , يُنْتِجُ : جُزْيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ ; نَحْوُ : بَعْضُ الفَاعِلِ مَرْفُوعٌ، وَكُلُّ فَاعِلٍ يُمْنَعُ حَذْفُهُ يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ المَرْفُوعِ يُمْنَعُ حَذْفُهُ . وَتَرْجِعُ هَذِهِ الضُّرُوبُ الثَّلَاثَةُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى . ( ٤ ) الضَّرْبُ الرَّابِعُ : مِنْ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ وَالصُّغْرَى فَقَط كُلِّيَّةٌ , يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ , نَحْوُ : كُلُّ عَرَضٍ صِفَةٌ، وَبَعْضُ العَرَضِ سَيَّالُ» يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ الصِّفَةِ سَيَّالٌ».
His statement: 'And the third: affirmation in its two [premises] and that one of them is universal.' He mentioned for the productivity of the third figure as well two conditions: (1) One of them, with respect to quality, and it is: the affirmation of its minor; because if it were negative, it would indicate complete or partial separation between the minor and the middle that is judged in the major as affirmative or negative, and the judgment on one of the two separate things does not necessitate the judgment on the other, and for this the difference that causes sterility occurs. (2) The second of the two conditions, with respect to quantity, and it is: universality of one of the two premises; otherwise it would be possible that the part judged in the minor is other than the part judged in the major, so the meeting of the minor and the major is not necessary, and for this as well the difference that causes sterility occurs when this condition is missing. And with the two conditions you know that its productive moods are six. As for by the method of enumeration, because its minor when it is universal affirmative produces with the four quantified majors, and when it is particular affirmative it produces with the two universals. And as for by the method of elimination, because the affirmation of the minor eliminated eight as passed in the first figure, and the universality of one of them eliminated two: the particular affirmative minor with the two particular majors. And this figure never produces except a particular; because the most specific of the productive moods composed of two universals affirmative, or the second is negative, and they do not produce the universal; because it is possible that the minor is more general than the major, so it is not correct to predicate the major on it universally affirmatively or negatively; like our saying: 'Every human is an animal, and every human is rational,' or: 'Nothing of human is a horse.' And when the two universals do not produce a universal, nothing else produces it; because what is not necessary for the more specific is not necessary for the more general. (1) The first mood of the six productive: from two universal affirmatives, it produces a particular affirmative, e.g., 'Every wheat is a staple, and every wheat is usury-able' produces 'Some staple is usury-able.' (2) The second mood: from two universals with the major negative, it produces a particular negative, e.g., 'Every body is existent, and nothing of body is eternal' produces 'Some existent is not eternal.' (3) The third mood: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, it produces a particular affirmative, e.g., 'Some agent is nominative, and every agent prevents its deletion' produces 'Some nominative prevents its deletion.' And these three moods reduce to the first figure by conversion of the minor. (4) The fourth mood: from two affirmatives with only the minor universal, it produces a particular affirmative, e.g., 'Every accident is a quality, and some accident is flowing' produces 'Some quality is flowing.'
Source pages p. 687

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by citing the matn: 'And the third [figure requires] affirmation in its two [premises] and that one of them is universal.' He then elaborates on the two conditions for productivity in the third figure. First, with respect to quality, the minor premise must be affirmative. If it were negative, we would have a separation (mubāyana) between the minor and middle terms, and a judgment on one of two separate things does not necessitate a judgment on the other, leading to sterility (ʿuqm). Second, with respect to quantity, at least one of the two premises must be universal. If both were particular, there could be a mismatch between the parts, preventing the meeting of the minor and major. The author then notes that with these conditions, the productive moods are six. He then explains two methods to derive these six moods: by enumeration (taḥṣīl) and by elimination (isqāṭ). By enumeration: if the minor is universal affirmative, it produces with each of the four quantified majors (that is, the four Aristotelian categorical propositions: A, E, I, O); if the minor is particular affirmative, it produces only with the two universal majors (A and E). By elimination: the requirement that the minor be affirmative eliminates eight possible combinations (as in the first figure), and the requirement of one universal eliminates two more (the minor particular affirmative with the two particular majors). He then explains that the third figure only yields particular conclusions, never universal. The reason is that even the most productive combination — two universal premises (both affirmative, or one affirmative and one negative) — cannot yield a universal conclusion because the minor term may be broader than the major, so a universal predication would be false. He gives examples: 'Every human is an animal, every human is rational' — this does not justify 'Every animal is rational' because animal is broader; similarly, 'No human is a horse' does not imply 'No animal is a horse.' After establishing this, the author lists four of the six productive moods with examples. (1) Two universal affirmatives yield a particular affirmative: 'Every wheat is a staple, every wheat is usury-able' → 'Some staple is usury-able.' This is a valid argument from the science of fiqh (since wheat is ribawi). (2) Two universals with a negative major yield a particular negative: 'Every body is existent, nothing of body is eternal' → 'Some existent is not eternal' — a kalam example about God's attributes. (3) A particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major yield a particular affirmative: 'Some agent is nominative, every agent prevents its deletion' → 'Some nominative prevents its deletion' — an Arabic grammar example. These three moods reduce to the first figure by conversion of the minor premise. (4) Two affirmatives with the minor only universal yield a particular affirmative: 'Every accident is a quality, some accident is flowing' → 'Some quality is flowing' — another kalam example. These examples demonstrate that the third figure, though always producing particular conclusions, is useful and valid.

Checkpoint
Why must the minor premise in a third-figure syllogism be affirmative?
A) Because if it were negative, the middle term would not be predicate.
B) Because a negative minor would separate the minor term from the middle term, making the conclusion unwarranted.
C) Because the major premise must be affirmative.
D) Because the conclusion must be particular.

Key Takeaway
A third-figure syllogism requires an affirmative minor and at least one universal premise to be productive; if either condition is missing, the syllogism is sterile.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the third-figure syllogism: 'Some agent is nominative, every agent prevents its deletion', verify that it satisfies the condition of an affirmative minor and at least one universal premise. If not, explain why it is sterile.
?
Create a third-figure syllogism on an Islamic topic (e.g., from fiqh or kalam) that violates the condition of a universal premise. Explain why it is sterile.
