The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
The Six Productive Moods of the Third Figure
Author enumerates the six productive moods and explains why conclusions are particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six productive moods of the third figure, explain why they all yield particular conclusions, and reduce the first three moods to the first figure via conversion of the minor.

Core Idea
The third figure has six productive moods; all conclusions are particular, never universal.

Once the two conditions are met, the third figure yields exactly six productive moods. The author derives them by two methods: enumeration and elimination. A remarkable feature is that even with two universal premises, the conclusion can only be particular. We will examine these moods and understand why universals are impossible. Then we will see how three of these moods reduce to the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Derive the moods by enumeration
The minor must be affirmative. If it is universal affirmative, it can combine with each of the four quantified majors (A, E, I, O) → 4 moods. If it is particular affirmative, it can only combine with universal majors (A, E) → 2 moods. Total 6.
This is the taḥṣīl method.
2
Derive the moods by elimination
Start with all 16 possible combinations of two premises (4x4). Condition: minor affirmative eliminates 8 (those with negative minors). Condition: one universal eliminates 2 more (minor particular affirmative with particular majors). Remaining 6.
This is the isqāṭ method.
3
Understand why only particular conclusions
Even the most productive combination (two universals) cannot yield a universal conclusion because the minor term may be more general than the major. Example: 'Every human is animal, every human is rational' cannot give 'Every animal is rational'.
The author says: 'لجواز أن يكون الأصغر أعم من الأكبر'
4
Learn the four explicit moods given
The passage lists four moods: (1) AAA (both universal affirmative) yields I; (2) AEA (universal affirmative major, universal negative minor?) Actually: two universals with major negative yields O; (3) IAI (particular affirmative minor, universal affirmative major) yields I; (4) AII (universal affirmative minor, particular affirmative major) yields I.
The mood notation is not given, but the description and examples show these.
5
Reduce the first three moods to the first figure
The author states that the first three moods reduce to the first figure by converting the minor premise (‛aks al-ṣughrā). This reveals their validity through the first figure's rules.
Conversion of the minor changes the figure; this is a standard technique.

Source Text

قوله : (وَالثَّالِثُ : الإِيجَابُ فِي صُغْرَاهُمَا وَأَنْ تُرَى كُلِّيَّةٌ إِحْدَاهُمَا ذَكَرَ لِإِنْتاجِ الشَّكْلِ الثَّالِثِ أَيْضاً شَرْطَيْنِ : (۱) أَحَدُهُمَا : بِاعْتِبَارِ الكَيْفِ، وَهُوَ : إِيْجَابُ صُغْرَاهُ ؛ لأَنَّهَا لَوْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةٌ، أَفَادَتِ المُبَايَنَةَ الكُلِّيَّةَ أَوِ الجُزْيَّةَ بَيْنَ الأَصْغَرِ وَالأَوْسَطِ المَحْكُومٍ عَلَيْهِ فِي الكُبْرَى بِالْأَكْبَرِ إِيْجَابَاً أَوْ سَلْباً، وَالحُكْمُ عَلَى أَحَدِ المُتَبَايَنَيْنِ لَا يُوجِبُ الحُكْمَ عَلَى الآخَرِ، وَلِهَذَا حَصَلَ الاِخْتِلَافُ المُوجِبُ لِلْعُقْم. (۲) وَثَانِي الشَّرْطَيْنِ : بِاعْتِبَارِ الكَمْ، وَهُوَ : كُلِّيَّةٌ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَإِلَّا جَازَ أَنْ يَكُونَ البَعْضُ المَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ فِي الصُّغْرَى غَيْرَ البَعْضِ المَحْكُوم عَلَيْهِ فِي الكُبْرَى، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ التِقَاء الأَصْغَرِ وَالأَكْبَرِ، وَلِهَذَا أَيْضاً حَصَلَ الاِخْتِلَافُ المُوجِبُ لِلْعُقْمِ عِنْدَ فَوَاتِ هَذَا الشَّرْطِ . وَبِالشَّرْطَيْنِ تَعْلَمُ أَنَّ ضُرُوبَهُ المُنْتِجَةَ سِتَّةُ : أَمَّا بِطَرِيقِ التَّحْصِيلِ، فَلِأَنَّ صُغْرَاهُ إِذَا كَانَتْ كُلِّيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ أَنْتَجَتْ مَعَ الكُبْرِيَّاتِ المَحْصُورَاتِ الأَرْبَعِ ، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ جُزْيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ أَنْتَجَتْ مَعَ الكُليّتيْنِ. وَأَمَّا بِطَرِيقِ الإِسْقَاطِ، فَلِأَنَّ إِيْجَابَ الصُّغْرَى أَسْقَطَ ثَمَانِيَةٌ كَمَا مَرَّ فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، وَكُلِّيَّةَ إِحْدَاهِمَا أَسْقَطَتِ اثْنَيْنِ الجُزْئِيَّةِ المُوجَبَةِ الصُّغْرَى مَعَ الجُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ الكُبْرَيَّيْنِ ، وَلَا يُنْتِجُ هَذَا الشَّكْلُ إِلَّا جُزْئِيَّةٌ أَبَداً ؛ الأَنَّ أَخَصَّ الضُّرُوبِ المُنْتِجَةِ المُرَكَبِ مِنْ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ، أَوِ الثَّانِيَةَ سَالِبَةٌ وَهُمَا لَا يُنْتِجَانِ الكُلِّيَّةَ؛ لِجَوَازِ أَنْ يَكُونَ الأَصْغَرُ أَعَمَّ مِنَ الأَكْبَرِ، فَلَا يَصِحُ حَمْلُ الأَكْبَرِ عَلَيْهِ كُلِّيًّا إِيْجَاباً وَلَا سَلْباً ; كَقَوْلِنَا : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ، أَوْ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ، وَإِذَا لَمْ تُنْتِجِ الكُلِّيَّتَانِ كُلِّيَّةٌ لَمْ يُنْتِجْهَا غَيْرُهُمَا ; لأَنَّ مَا لَا يَلْزَمُ الأَخَصُّ لَا يَلْزَمُ الأَعَمُّ. (۱) الضَّرْبُ الأَوَّلُ مِنَ السِّنَّةِ المُنْتِجَةِ : مِنْ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ، يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ , نَحْو : كُلُّ بُرِّ مُقْتَاتٌ، وَكُلُّ بُرِّ رَبَوِيٌّ» يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ المُقْتَاتِ رَبَوِيُّ». (۲) الضَّرْبُ الثَّانِي : مِنْ كُلِّيَتَيْنِ وَالكُبْرَى سَالِبَةٌ , يُنتِجُ : جُزْيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ ; نَحْوُ : «كُلُّ مُتَحَيِّز مَوْجُودٌ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ المُتَحَيِّزِ بِقَدِيمٍ يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ المَوْجُودِ لَيْسَ بِقَدِيمٍ . (۳) الضَّرْبُ الثَّالِثُ : مِنْ جُزْئِيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ صُغْرَى وَكُلِّيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ كُبْرَى , يُنْتِجُ : جُزْيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ ; نَحْوُ : بَعْضُ الفَاعِلِ مَرْفُوعٌ، وَكُلُّ فَاعِلٍ يُمْنَعُ حَذْفُهُ يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ المَرْفُوعِ يُمْنَعُ حَذْفُهُ . وَتَرْجِعُ هَذِهِ الضُّرُوبُ الثَّلَاثَةُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى . ( ٤ ) الضَّرْبُ الرَّابِعُ : مِنْ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ وَالصُّغْرَى فَقَط كُلِّيَّةٌ , يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ , نَحْوُ : كُلُّ عَرَضٍ صِفَةٌ، وَبَعْضُ العَرَضِ سَيَّالُ» يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ الصِّفَةِ سَيَّالٌ».
His statement: 'And the third: affirmation in its two [premises] and that one of them is universal.' He mentioned for the productivity of the third figure as well two conditions: (1) One of them, with respect to quality, and it is: the affirmation of its minor; because if it were negative, it would indicate complete or partial separation between the minor and the middle that is judged in the major as affirmative or negative, and the judgment on one of the two separate things does not necessitate the judgment on the other, and for this the difference that causes sterility occurs. (2) The second of the two conditions, with respect to quantity, and it is: universality of one of the two premises; otherwise it would be possible that the part judged in the minor is other than the part judged in the major, so the meeting of the minor and the major is not necessary, and for this as well the difference that causes sterility occurs when this condition is missing. And with the two conditions you know that its productive moods are six. As for by the method of enumeration, because its minor when it is universal affirmative produces with the four quantified majors, and when it is particular affirmative it produces with the two universals. And as for by the method of elimination, because the affirmation of the minor eliminated eight as passed in the first figure, and the universality of one of them eliminated two: the particular affirmative minor with the two particular majors. And this figure never produces except a particular; because the most specific of the productive moods composed of two universals affirmative, or the second is negative, and they do not produce the universal; because it is possible that the minor is more general than the major, so it is not correct to predicate the major on it universally affirmatively or negatively; like our saying: 'Every human is an animal, and every human is rational,' or: 'Nothing of human is a horse.' And when the two universals do not produce a universal, nothing else produces it; because what is not necessary for the more specific is not necessary for the more general. (1) The first mood of the six productive: from two universal affirmatives, it produces a particular affirmative, e.g., 'Every wheat is a staple, and every wheat is usury-able' produces 'Some staple is usury-able.' (2) The second mood: from two universals with the major negative, it produces a particular negative, e.g., 'Every body is existent, and nothing of body is eternal' produces 'Some existent is not eternal.' (3) The third mood: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, it produces a particular affirmative, e.g., 'Some agent is nominative, and every agent prevents its deletion' produces 'Some nominative prevents its deletion.' And these three moods reduce to the first figure by conversion of the minor. (4) The fourth mood: from two affirmatives with only the minor universal, it produces a particular affirmative, e.g., 'Every accident is a quality, and some accident is flowing' produces 'Some quality is flowing.'

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by citing the matn: 'And the third [figure requires] affirmation in its two [premises] and that one of them is universal.' He then elaborates on the two conditions for productivity in the third figure. First, with respect to quality, the minor premise must be affirmative. If it were negative, we would have a separation (mubāyana) between the minor and middle terms, and a judgment on one of two separate things does not necessitate a judgment on the other, leading to sterility (ʿuqm). Second, with respect to quantity, at least one of the two premises must be universal. If both were particular, there could be a mismatch between the parts, preventing the meeting of the minor and major. The author then notes that with these conditions, the productive moods are six. He then explains two methods to derive these six moods: by enumeration (taḥṣīl) and by elimination (isqāṭ). By enumeration: if the minor is universal affirmative, it produces with each of the four quantified majors (that is, the four Aristotelian categorical propositions: A, E, I, O); if the minor is particular affirmative, it produces only with the two universal majors (A and E). By elimination: the requirement that the minor be affirmative eliminates eight possible combinations (as in the first figure), and the requirement of one universal eliminates two more (the minor particular affirmative with the two particular majors). He then explains that the third figure only yields particular conclusions, never universal. The reason is that even the most productive combination — two universal premises (both affirmative, or one affirmative and one negative) — cannot yield a universal conclusion because the minor term may be broader than the major, so a universal predication would be false. He gives examples: 'Every human is an animal, every human is rational' — this does not justify 'Every animal is rational' because animal is broader; similarly, 'No human is a horse' does not imply 'No animal is a horse.' After establishing this, the author lists four of the six productive moods with examples. (1) Two universal affirmatives yield a particular affirmative: 'Every wheat is a staple, every wheat is usury-able' → 'Some staple is usury-able.' This is a valid argument from the science of fiqh (since wheat is ribawi). (2) Two universals with a negative major yield a particular negative: 'Every body is existent, nothing of body is eternal' → 'Some existent is not eternal' — a kalam example about God's attributes. (3) A particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major yield a particular affirmative: 'Some agent is nominative, every agent prevents its deletion' → 'Some nominative prevents its deletion' — an Arabic grammar example. These three moods reduce to the first figure by conversion of the minor premise. (4) Two affirmatives with the minor only universal yield a particular affirmative: 'Every accident is a quality, some accident is flowing' → 'Some quality is flowing' — another kalam example. These examples demonstrate that the third figure, though always producing particular conclusions, is useful and valid.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Why the Fourth Figure Yields Only Particular Conclusions
Author proves that the fourth figure cannot produce universal conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the fourth figure of categorical syllogisms cannot produce universal conclusions, using the methods of acquisition and elimination.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's conclusions are always particular; even two universal premises fail due to term generality.

This passage delves into a key limitation of the fourth figure in Aristotelian logic: it can never yield a universal conclusion. The author, a commentator on Bannani's logical text, presents two systematic methods to demonstrate this restriction. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly evaluating syllogisms in Islamic theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
The author introduces two approaches: the method of acquisition (tariq al-tahsil) which lists productive premise combinations, and the method of elimination (tariq al-isqat) which removes invalid combinations based on quality and quantity.
These methods are complementary and together prove the figure's limitation.
2
Analyze the acquisition method
Under tahsil, if the minor premise is universal affirmative, it combines with all four quantified major premises to produce a conclusion. If the minor is particular affirmative, it only combines with the two universal majors. Notice that no combination yields a universal conclusion.
This shows that even with a universal minor, the conclusion remains particular.
3
Analyze the elimination method
Under isqat, the affirmativeness of the minor eliminates eight invalid combos (as in the first figure). Additionally, the universality of one premise eliminates the case of a particular affirmative minor with two particular majors. This leaves only productive combos, all of which yield particular conclusions.
Elimination confirms that no universal conclusion is possible.
4
Understand the core reason
Even the strongest mood (two universal premises) fails to produce a universal conclusion because the minor term (asghar) may be more general than the major term (akbar). For example, 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational' do not allow concluding 'Every animal is rational', since 'human' is more specific than 'animal'.
This logical principle is general: what is not entailed by the more specific is not entailed by the more general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَمَّا بِطَرِيقِ التَّحْصِيلِ، فَلِأَنَّ صُغْرَاهُ إِذَا كَانَتْ كُلِّيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ أَنْتَجَتْ مَعَ الكُبْرِيَّاتِ المَحْصُورَاتِ الأَرْبَعِ ، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ أَنْتَجَتْ مَعَ الكُليّتيْنِ. وَأَمَّا بِطَرِيقِ الإِسْقَاطِ، فَلِأَنَّ إِيْجَابَ الصُّغْرَى أَسْقَطَ ثَمَانِيَةٌ كَمَا مَرَّ فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، وَكُلِّيَّةَ إِحْدَاهِمَا أَسْقَطَتِ اثْنَيْنِ الجُزْئِيَّةِ المُوجَبَةِ الصُّغْرَى مَعَ الجُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ الكُبْرَيَّيْنِ ، وَلَا يُنْتِجُ هَذَا الشَّكْلُ إِلَّا جُزْئِيَّةٌ أَبَداً ; الأَنَّ أَخَصَّ الضُّرُوبِ المُنْتِجَةِ المُرَكَبِ مِنْ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ، أَوِ الثَّانِيَةَ سَالِبَةٌ وَهُمَا لَا يُنْتِجَانِ الكُلِّيَّةَ ] (١) ; لِجَوَازِ أَنْ يَكُونَ الأَصْغَرُ أَعَمَّ مِنَ الأَكْبَرِ، فَلَا يَصِحُ حَمْلُ الأَكْبَرِ عَلَيْهِ كُلِّيًّا إِيْجَاباً وَلَا سَلْباً ; كَقَوْلِنَا : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ، أَوْ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ، وَإِذَا لَمْ تُنْتِجِ الكُلِّيَّتَانِ كُلِّيَّةٌ لَمْ يُنْتِجْهَا غَيْرُهُمَا ; لأَنَّ مَا لَا يَلْزَمُ الأَخَصُّ لَا يَلْزَمُ الأَعَمُّ.
As for the method of acquisition (tahsil): because its minor premise, if it is universal affirmative, produces with the four quantified major premises; and if it is particular affirmative, it produces with the two universal premises. As for the method of elimination (isqat): because the affirmativeness of the minor eliminates eight [combinations] as previously in the first figure, and the universality of one of them eliminates two: the particular affirmative minor with the two particular major premises. This figure only ever produces a particular [conclusion]; because the most specific of the productive moods composed of two universal affirmatives, or of a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, do not yield a universal [conclusion]; due to the possibility that the minor term is more general than the major, so it is not valid to attribute the major to it universally either affirmatively or negatively; as in our saying: 'Every human is an animal, and every human is rational' or 'Nothing of human is a horse'. And if the two universal premises do not produce a universal [conclusion], then nothing else produces it; because what is not entailed by the more specific is not entailed by the more general.
Source pages p. 688

Commentary
This passage explains why the fourth figure (shakl rabi') cannot yield a universal conclusion, only a particular one. The author presents two methods: the method of acquisition (tahsil) and the method of elimination (isqat). In the acquisition method, he notes the possible combinations of minor and major that produce a conclusion: if the minor is universal affirmative, it produces with all four quantified major premises (i.e., universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative); if the minor is particular affirmative, it produces only with the two universal majors. In the elimination method, he explains that the affirmative nature of the minor eliminates eight invalid combinations (as learned in the first figure), and the universality of one premise eliminates two more (the case of particular affirmative minor with particular major premises). The key point is that this figure always yields a particular conclusion because even the most restricted productive moods—composed of two universal affirmatives or of a universal affirmative minor with a universal negative major—cannot yield a universal conclusion. The reason is that it is possible for the minor term (asghar) to be more general (a'amm) than the major term (akbar). For example, if we say 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational', we cannot conclude 'Every animal is rational' because the minor 'human' is more specific than 'animal'. Similarly, with a negative universal major. Since even the strongest combinations (two universals) fail to produce a universal, no weaker combination can do so, because what is not necessitated by the more specific is also not necessitated by the more general. Thus, the fourth figure only yields particular conclusions, as the commentator will illustrate with the six productive moods.

The Syllogism

Six Productive Moods of the Fourth Figure
Commentator lists the six productive moods of the fourth figure with examples and reduction to first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the six productive moods of the fourth figure and explain whether and how it reduces to the first figure.

Core Idea
The fourth figure has exactly six productive moods, each yielding a particular conclusion.

The fourth figure of categorical syllogisms is less intuitive than the first, but it has six valid moods that produce conclusions. Each mood has a specific combination of quantity and quality in its premises. In this lesson, we will go through each mood with examples from Islamic sciences and examine how they can be reduced to the first figure, except for the sixth mood which stands on its own.

Guided Path
1
Mood 1 – Two universal affirmatives
Both premises are universal affirmative (A). The conclusion is particular affirmative (I). Example: 'Every wheat is a staple food, and every wheat is ribawi' → 'Some staple foods are ribawi'. This reduces by converting the minor premise.
Conversion of the minor swaps subject and predicate, turning 'Every wheat is a staple food' into 'Some staple foods are wheat' to fit the first figure.
2
Mood 2 – Universal affirmative minor, universal negative major
Minor A, major E, conclusion O (particular negative). Example: 'Everything corporeal is existent, nothing corporeal is eternal' → 'Some existents are not eternal'. Also reduces by converting the minor.
3
Mood 3 – Particular affirmative minor, universal affirmative major
Minor I, major A, conclusion I. Example: 'Some agents are raised, every agent prevents its deletion' → 'Some raised elements prevent deletion'. Reduces by converting the minor.
4
Mood 4 – Universal affirmative minor, particular affirmative major
Minor A, major I, conclusion I. Example: 'Every accident is a quality, some accidents are fluid' → 'Some qualities are fluid'. Reduces by converting the major premise, then swapping premises, then converting the conclusion.
The reduction involves multiple steps: convert major, make it the minor, and then convert the conclusion to match the first figure.
5
Mood 5 – Particular affirmative minor, universal negative major
Minor I, major E, conclusion O. Example: 'Some qualities are eternal, nothing of the qualities is self-subsistent' → 'Some eternal things are not self-subsistent'. Reduces by converting the minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( ٤ ) - الضَّرْبُ الرَّابِعُ : مِنْ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ وَالصُّغْرَى فَقَط كُلِّيَّةٌ ، يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ ، نَحْوُ : كُلُّ عَرَضٍ صِفَةٌ، وَبَعْضُ العَرَضِ سَيَّالُ» يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ الصِّفَةِ سَيَّالٌ». وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الكُبْرَى ثُمَّ جَعْلُهَا صُغْرَى ، ثُمَّ عَكْسُ النَّتيجة . (٥) - الضَّرْبُ الخَامِسُ : مِنْ جُزْئِيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ صُغْرَى، وَكُلِّيَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ كُبْرَى، يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ; نَحْوُ : بَعْضُ الصِّفَاتِ قَدِيمٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الصَّفَاتِ بِقَائِمٍ بِنَفْسِهِ يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ القَدِيمِ لَيْسَ بِقَائِمٍ بِنَفْسِهِ»، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ صُغْرَاهُ . (٦) - الضَّرْبُ السَّادِسُ : مِنْ كُلِّيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ صُغْرَى، وَجُزْئِيَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ كُبْرَى، يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ ، نَحْوُ : كُلُّ حَادِثِ مُفْتَقِرٌ ، وَبَعْضُ الحَادِثِ لَيْسَ بِجِرْم، يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ المُفْتَقِرِ لَيْسَ بِجِرْمٍ، وَهَذَا لَا يَرْجِعُ لِلأَوَّلِ بِوَجْهِ ; لِأَنَّ كُبْرَاهُ لَا تَصْلُحُ لِلأَوَّلِ صُغْرَى وَلَا كُبرى، وَبَيَانُهُ بِطَرِيقِ التَّعَقُلِ : أَنَّ كُلَّ مَا هُوَ فَرْدٌ لِلأَوْسَطِ فَهُوَ فَرْدٌ لِلأَصْغَرِ، كَمَا أَفَادَتْهُ الصُّغْرَى، وَهُوَ مَعْنَى النَّتِيجَةِ . وَوَجْهُ تَرَتُبِ هَذِهِ الأَضْرُبِ (١) عَلَى مَا ذَكَرْنَا : أَنَّ الأَوَّلَ أَخَصُّ الضُّرُوبِ المُنْتِجَةِ لِلإِيْجَابِ، وَالثَّانِي أَخَصُّ الضُّرُوبِ المُنْتِجَةِ لِلسَّلْبِ، وَالأَخَصُ أَشْرَفُ مِنَ الأَعَمِّ، وَقُدِّمَ الثَّالِثُ عَلَى الرَّابع ; لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى كُبْرَى الأَوَّلِ، وَالرَّابِعُ عَلَى مَا بَعْدَهُ; لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى إِيجَابِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْن مَعاً، وَالخَامِسُ عَلَى السَّادِسِ ; لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى كُبْرَى الأَوَّلِ كَالثَّالِثِ .
The first productive mood: from two universal affirmatives, it produces a particular affirmative, e.g., 'Every wheat is a staple food, and every wheat is ribawi' yields 'Some staple foods are ribawi'. The second mood: from two universals with the major negative, it produces a particular negative, e.g., 'Everything corporeal is existent, and nothing corporeal is eternal' yields 'Some existents are not eternal'. The third mood: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, it produces a particular affirmative, e.g., 'Some agents are raised (in case), and every agent prevents its deletion' yields 'Some raised (elements) prevent deletion'. These three moods reduce to the first figure by conversion of the minor premise. The fourth mood: from two affirmatives with only the minor universal, it produces a particular affirmative, e.g., 'Every accident is a quality, and some accidents are fluid' yields 'Some qualities are fluid'. It reduces to the first figure by converting the major premise, then making it the minor, then converting the conclusion. The fifth mood: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, it produces a particular negative, e.g., 'Some qualities are eternal, and nothing of the qualities is self-subsistent' yields 'Some eternal things are not self-subsistent'. It reduces to the first figure by conversion of its minor. The sixth mood: from a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, it produces a particular negative, e.g., 'Every originated thing is dependent, and some originated things are not a body' yields 'Some dependent things are not a body'. This does not reduce to the first figure by any means, because its major is not suitable as a minor or major in the first figure. Its explanation by the method of rational consideration is: that every individual of the middle term is an individual of the minor term, as the minor premise indicates, and that is the meaning of the conclusion. The reason for the ordering of these moods as we have mentioned: because the first is the most specific of the moods producing affirmation, and the second is the most specific of the moods producing negation, and the more specific is more noble than the more general. The third is placed before the fourth because it includes the major of the first figure; the fourth is placed before what comes after it because it includes the affirmation of both premises together; the fifth is placed before the sixth because it includes the major of the first figure, like the third.
Source pages p. 688

Commentary
This passage enumerates the six productive moods (adrub muntija) of the fourth figure, each yielding a particular conclusion. The author lists them with examples from various fields (fiqh, kalam, Arabic grammar) and explains how they reduce to the first figure, except the sixth mood which does not reduce. The first mood has two universal affirmatives, producing a particular affirmative; the second has a universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, producing a particular negative; the third has a particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major, producing a particular affirmative; the fourth has a universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major, producing a particular affirmative; the fifth has a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, producing a particular negative; the sixth has a universal affirmative minor and particular negative major, producing a particular negative. The reduction methods: the first three reduce by converting the minor premise; the fourth reduces by converting the major then swapping premises then converting the conclusion; the fifth reduces by converting the minor; the sixth does not reduce because the major premise (particular negative) cannot serve as a minor or major in the first figure. The author then explains the rationale for the ordering: the first and second are the most specific for affirmation and negation respectively, and the rest are ordered by nobility and inclusion of features of the first figure. The examples given are from various domains: the first uses ribawi items (fiqh), the second uses kalam (eternal vs. created), the third uses Arabic grammar (fa'il/agent and raf'/raising), the fourth uses attributes and accidents (kalam), the fifth uses qualities and self-subsistence (kalam), the sixth uses originated things and bodies (kalam).

The Syllogism

Condition for Productivity in the Fourth Figure
Commentator explains the condition for the fourth figure when minor is not particular affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given fourth-figure mood satisfies the condition for productivity when the minor is not particular affirmative.

Core Idea
When the minor is not particular affirmative, avoid combining two deficiencies of the same type.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism has a special condition when its minor premise is not a particular affirmative. In that case, productivity (a valid conclusion) requires that the mood does not contain two 'deficiencies' (khissatan) of the same kind. Deficiencies are weaknesses like particularity or negation. The well-known rule restricts valid moods to five out of the possible combinations. Understanding this rule helps you avoid invalid arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two states of the fourth figure
The commentator says the fourth figure has two states depending on the minor premise. First, look at the minor premise: is it particular affirmative? If yes, a different condition applies (not covered here). If it is not particular affirmative, we use the rule in this lesson.
The matn hints at an exception when the minor is particular affirmative.
2
Define 'deficiency' (khissa) in this context
A deficiency (خِسَّة) is a weakness in a syllogistic mood, such as being particular (جزئية) or negative (سالبة). Two deficiencies of the same type means, for example, both premises are particular, or both are negative.
The commentator mentions 'of the same type' – mixing particular and negative is allowed.
3
Apply the condition: avoid two deficiencies of the same type
When the minor is not particular affirmative, check if the mood contains two deficiencies of the same kind. For instance, if both premises are particular, or both are negative, then the condition is violated and the mood is invalid. If only one deficiency exists or they are of different types, the mood may be productive.
This condition restricts productivity to five specific moods.
4
Practice with an example
Take a fourth-figure mood: minor = universal affirmative, major = universal negative. The minor is not particular affirmative. Deficiencies: the major is negative (one deficiency), minor has none. Only one deficiency of one type, so condition holds – mood is productive (e.g., Celantes).
If minor = particular negative, major = universal negative: both are negative (two deficiencies of same type) → invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (۹۲) وَرَابِعٌ : عَدَمُ جَمْعِ الخِسَّتَيْن إِلَّا بِصُورَةٍ فَفِيهَا يَسْتَبِينُ (۹۳) صُغْرَاهُمَا مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّة كُبْرَاهُمَا سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّة ذَكَرَ أَنَّ الشَّكْلَ الرَّابِعَ لَهُ حَالَتَانِ يَخْتَلِفُ شَرْطُ إِنْتَاجِهِ بِاخْتِلَافِهِمَا، وَذَلِكَ أَنَّهُ إِذَا لَمْ تَكُنْ صُغْرَاهُ مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ، فَشَرْطُ إِنْتَاجِهِ : أَنْ لَا يَجْتَمِعَ فِيهِ خِسَّتَانِ مِنْ نَوْعِ وَاحِدٍ. هَذَا الضَّابِطُ هُوَ الْمَشْهُورُ، وَهُوَ الَّذِي ذَكَرَهُ الْأَقْدَمُونَ، فَانْحَصَرَ الْإِنْتَاجُ فِي خَمْسَةِ أَضْرُبِ بِمُقْتَضَى ذَلِكَ الاشْتِرَاطِ.
His saying: 'And the fourth [figure]: the not combining of the two deficiencies except in a form in which it becomes clear that their minor is particular affirmative and their major is universal negative.' He mentioned that the fourth figure has two states, and its condition for productivity differs according to them. That is, when its minor is not particular affirmative, its condition for productivity is that two deficiencies of the same type do not combine in it. This rule is the well-known one, and it is what the earlier scholars mentioned, so productivity is restricted to five moods according to that condition.
Source pages p. 689

Commentary
This passage explains the condition for the fourth figure's productivity as presented in the matn (likely from al-Sullam). The commentator first quotes the matn: 'And the fourth [figure]: the not combining of the two deficiencies except in a form in which it becomes clear that their minor is particular affirmative and their major is universal negative.' Then he explains that the fourth figure has two states depending on the minor premise. If the minor is a particular affirmative (as in the exception mentioned in the matn), then it is productive under certain conditions. If the minor is not a particular affirmative, then the condition for productivity is that two 'deficiencies' (khissatan) of the same type do not combine. The term 'deficiency' here refers to a weakness in the syllogism, such as particularity or negativity. The well-known rule from earlier logicians is that productivity is restricted to five moods when the minor is not particular affirmative, meaning that out of the possible combinations, only five are valid under that condition. This is a standard doctrine in the logic of the fourth figure.

The Fifth Mood of the First Figure
Author defines the fifth mood and explains its reduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fifth mood of the first figure (affirmative particular minor, universal negative major) and reduce it to the first figure by converting the minor premise.

Core Idea
The fifth mood yields a negative particular and reduces via conversion of its minor.

The fifth mood of the first figure combines an affirmative particular minor with a universal negative major. Its conclusion is a negative particular. The author also shows how to reduce this mood to the perfect first figure by converting the minor premise, a key technique for validating syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Find the minor premise: affirmative particular (e.g., 'Some attributes are eternal'). Find the major premise: universal negative (e.g., 'No attribute is self-subsistent').
2
Extract the conclusion
The conclusion must be a negative particular: 'Some eternal is not self-subsistent'. Confirm it follows from the premises.
3
Perform reduction by conversion
Convert the minor premise 'Some attributes are eternal' to 'Some eternal is attribute' (by conversion of an affirmative particular, which is valid). Then swap the premises: new major = converted minor, new minor = original major? Actually, the text says 'by conversion of its minor' and then making it the minor? The passage says: 'يرجع إلى الأول بعكس صغراه' - it reduces to the first by conversion of its minor. So take the minor 'بعض الصفات قديم' convert to 'بعض القديم صفات', then this becomes the minor? Wait, the reduction method earlier: 'بعكس الكبرى ثم جعلها صغرى' for the third mood. For the fifth, it says 'بعكس صغراه', so only one step. Let's think: The original mood is: minor: Some A is B (A=attributes, B=eternal). major: No A is C (C=self-subsistent). Conclusion: Some B is not C. To reduce to first figure, we want a premise set where middle term is subject in major and predicate in minor. Converting minor gives: Some B is A. Now we have: minor: Some B is A, major: No A is C. This is first figure (middle term A is subject in major and predicate in minor) yielding: Some B is not C. So reduction works.
The conversion of an affirmative particular (I) is valid and yields another I proposition.
4
Verify validity
Check that the reduced syllogism is in the first figure (e.g., Darapti? Actually, it is Ferio? Wait, new premises: Some B is A (I), No A is C (E). This is first figure mood Ferio (EIO) if the minor were universal? But here minor is particular. Actually, the first figure requires the minor to be affirmative and major universal. Here minor is particular affirmative (I) and major universal negative (E). That is not one of the four perfect moods. But the author says it reduces to the first by conversion of its minor. Perhaps the resulting mood is considered a valid first figure mood? Or it is reduced to the first by converting the minor and then the conclusion? The text earlier said for the fourth mood: 'بعكس الكبرى ثم جعلها صغرى، ثم عكس النتيجة' - reduction can involve multiple steps. For the fifth, only 'بعكس صغراه' is mentioned. So the resulting syllogism might be the third mood (?) of the first figure. Actually, the standard moods of the first figure are: Barbara (AAA), Celarent (EAE), Darii (AII), Ferio (EIO). So the reduced form with premises I (some B is A) and E (no A is C) is not one of these. But the conclusion is Some B is not C, which is O. So the mood would be IEO? That is not a valid mood in any figure because a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major with a particular negative conclusion is not valid in the first figure. Something is off. Perhaps the reduction is to the third figure? But the text explicitly says 'يرجع إلى الأول'. Let's examine the actual example: Minor: Some attributes are eternal (I). Major: No attribute is self-subsistent (E). Conclusion: Some eternal is not self-subsistent (O). After converting the minor, we get: Some eternal is attribute (I). If we keep the major as is: No attribute is self-subsistent (E). Now the terms: middle = attribute, minor = eternal, major = self-subsistent. The premises are: I (Some eternal is attribute) and E (No attribute is self-subsistent). This is not a first figure because in the first figure, the middle term must be subject in the major and predicate in the minor. Here, middle (attribute) is predicate in the minor (Some eternal is attribute) and subject in the major (No attribute is self-subsistent). So that fits first figure. The mood, however, is IEO (I minor, E major, O conclusion). Is that a valid first figure? In the standard list, no. But maybe the tradition recognizes extended moods? Or perhaps after converting the minor, we also need to convert the conclusion? The text for the fifth mood only says 'بعكس صغراه', not mentioning conclusion conversion. So maybe the resulting syllogism is considered a first figure mood that is not one of the four perfect but still valid. The author might be referring to the fact that after conversion, the premises are in the form where the middle term is subject in major and predicate in minor, which is the defining condition of the first figure, even if the mood is not one of the four perfect ones. For our lesson, we can simply state that the reduction is done by converting the minor premise, and the resulting syllogism is in the first figure.
The exact validity of the reduced form may require additional steps, but the author asserts the reduction works.

وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الكُبْرَى ثُمَّ جَعْلُهَا صُغْرَى ، ثُمَّ عَكْسُ النَّتيجة . (٥) - الضَّرْبُ الخَامِسُ : مِنْ جُزْنِيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ صُغْرَى، وَكُلِّيَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ كُبْرَى، يُنْتِجُ : جُزْنِيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ؛ نَحْوُ : بَعْضُ الصِّفَاتِ قَدِيمٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الصَّفَاتِ بِقَائِمٍ بِنَفْسِهِ يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ القَدِيمِ لَيْسَ بِقَائِمٍ بِنَفْسِهِ»، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ صُغْرَاهُ . And it reduces to the first by conversion of the major then making it the minor, then conversion of the conclusion. (5) The fifth mood: from an affirmative particular minor, and a universal negative major, it produces a negative particular; e.g., 'Some attributes are eternal, and no attribute is self-subsistent' produces 'Some eternal is not self-subsistent.' And it reduces to the first by conversion of its minor. (6) The sixth mood: from an affirmative universal minor, and a negative particular major, it produces a negative particular; e.g., 'Every contingent is dependent, and some contingent is not a body' produces 'Some dependent is not a body.' And this does not reduce to the first at all, because its major is not suitable for the first as a minor nor as a major. Its demonstration by reasoning: every individual of the middle term is an individual of the minor term, as the minor premise indicates, and that is the meaning of the conclusion. And the reason for the order of these moods according to what we mentioned: that the first is the most specific of the productive moods for affirmation, and the second is the most specific of the productive moods for negation, and the most specific is nobler than the more general. And the third is placed before the fourth because it includes the major premise of the first, and the fourth before what follows it because it includes the productivity of both premises together, and the fifth before the sixth because it includes the major premise of the first like the third.
Source pages p. 689

Commentary
This passage explains the fifth and sixth moods of the first figure and their order. The author first describes reduction: the fifth mood (affirmative particular minor, universal negative major) yields a negative particular conclusion, and reduces to the first figure by converting the minor premise. The sixth mood (universal affirmative minor, negative particular major) also yields a negative particular, but cannot be reduced directly. The author gives a rational demonstration: the middle term's individuals are individuals of the minor term, which is already the conclusion. Then the author explains why the six moods are ordered as they are: the first mood is the most specific productive for affirmation, the second for negation; the third is before the fourth because it shares the major of the first, the fourth because it produces from both premises, and the fifth before the sixth because it also shares the major of the first. This ordering reflects a logical hierarchy based on specificity and completeness.

The Sixth Mood of the First Figure
Author defines the sixth mood and explains its irreducible nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sixth mood of the first figure (universal affirmative minor, negative particular major) and explain why it cannot be reduced to the first figure.

Core Idea
The sixth mood yields a negative particular but resists direct reduction to the first figure.

The sixth mood combines a universal affirmative minor with a negative particular major, producing a negative particular conclusion. Unlike the other moods, it cannot be reduced to the perfect first figure because its major premise is unsuitable to serve as either the minor or major after conversion. The author provides a direct rational demonstration instead.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Find the minor premise: universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every contingent is dependent'). Find the major premise: negative particular (e.g., 'Some contingent is not a body').
2
Extract the conclusion
The conclusion is a negative particular: 'Some dependent is not a body'. Confirm that it follows from the premises.
3
Attempt reduction to first figure
Try to manipulate the premises to fit the first figure. Consider converting the major: 'Some contingent is not a body' converts to 'Some body is not contingent' (valid conversion of O). Now premises would be: minor: 'Every contingent is dependent' (A), new major: 'Some body is not contingent' (O). In this new syllogism, the middle term would be 'contingent'. In the major, 'contingent' is predicate, in the minor it is subject. That fits first figure? Actually, in first figure, middle term must be subject in major and predicate in minor. Here, in new major, 'contingent' is predicate, so not first figure. Alternatively, converting the minor? Minor is A, convert to I: 'Some dependent is contingent'. Then we have: minor: Some dependent is contingent (I), major: Some contingent is not body (O). Now middle term 'contingent' is predicate in minor and subject in major, so first figure form. But the conclusion would be? The original conclusion is 'Some dependent is not body'. Does the new syllogism produce that? Let's check: Some dependent is contingent, Some contingent is not body. Does that imply Some dependent is not body? Not necessarily; this is not a valid syllogism in any figure. So reduction fails. The author explicitly says it does not reduce because its major is not suitable for the first figure as a minor or major.
The major premise is negative particular (O), which cannot serve as a universal premise required for first figure perfection.
4
Understand the author's direct demonstration
The author says: 'كل ما هو فرد للأوسط فهو فرد للأصغر' (every individual of the middle term is an individual of the minor term). This is derived from the minor premise (All contingent is dependent). So the minor term includes all individuals of the middle term. The conclusion 'Some dependent is not body' means that some individual of the minor term is not a body, which follows if some individual of the middle term is not a body (major premise). This direct reasoning does not rely on reduction.

The author first describes reduction: the fifth mood (affirmative particular minor, universal negative major) yields a negative particular conclusion, and reduces to the first figure by converting the minor premise. The sixth mood (universal affirmative minor, negative particular major) also yields a negative particular, but cannot be reduced directly. The author gives a rational demonstration: the middle term's individuals are individuals of the minor term, which is already the conclusion. Then the author explains why the six moods are ordered as they are: the first mood is the most specific productive for affirmation, the second for negation; the third is before the fourth because it shares the major of the first, the fourth because it produces from both premises, and the fifth before the sixth because it also shares the major of the first. This ordering reflects a logical hierarchy based on specificity and completeness.

The Ordering of the First Figure Moods
Author explains the rationale for the order of the six moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical criteria (specificity, inclusion of first mood's major, productivity of both premises) used to order the six moods of the first figure.

Core Idea
Mood order reflects hierarchy: most specific for affirmation/negation come first, then those sharing the first mood's major, then those productive from both premises.

After presenting the six moods of the first figure, the author explains the rationale for their order. The order is not arbitrary but based on logical nobility: more specific moods are nobler than more general ones, and moods that share the major premise of the first mood are placed before those that do not. Understanding this order helps one appreciate the internal structure of the syllogistic system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first two moods (most specific)
The first mood is the most specific productive mood for affirmation (Barbara: AAA). The second is the most specific for negation (Celarent: EAE). They are placed first because the most specific is nobler (أشرف) than the more general.
2
Recognize why the third mood precedes the fourth
The third mood (Darii: AII) includes the major premise of the first mood (universal affirmative). The fourth mood (Ferio: EIO) does not; it has a universal negative major. So the third is placed before the fourth because sharing the first mood's major gives it precedence.
3
Understand why the fourth precedes the fifth and sixth
The fourth mood is productive from both premises together (i.e., both premises are universal? Actually, the text says 'لاشتماله على إنجاب المقدمتين معاً' — because it includes the productivity of both premises together. Maybe meaning both premises are universal? The fourth mood (EIO) has a universal major and particular minor? Wait, Ferio has universal negative major and particular affirmative minor, so not both universal. Perhaps it means that the fourth mood produces a conclusion from both premises without needing reduction? Or that it uses both the negative and affirmative? The author's exact meaning may require context, but for the lesson, we can note that the fourth mood has a special property that makes it precede the non-reducible moods.
4
Explain why the fifth precedes the sixth
The fifth mood (IEO, but in tradition it is like the third?) shares the major premise of the first mood (universal affirmative) just like the third mood. The sixth mood does not. Therefore, the fifth is placed before the sixth.

And it reduces to the first by conversion of the major then making it the minor, then conversion of the conclusion. Conditions for the Fourth Figure and the Two Deficiencies
Author explains the two conditions for the fourth figure and defines the two types of deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for a productive fourth-figure syllogism and determine whether a given mood in the fourth figure is productive or sterile based on the presence of the two deficiencies.

Core Idea
The fourth figure is productive only under specific conditions avoiding the two deficiencies of particularity and negation.

In this passage, the author explains the conditions for the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. He distinguishes two states depending on the minor premise, and introduces the concept of 'khissatan' (two deficiencies) that cause sterility. Understanding these conditions is crucial for evaluating arguments in this figure, which appears in advanced logical works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two states
Determine whether the minor premise is an affirmative particular proposition or not. The condition for productivity differs accordingly.
2
Understand the two deficiencies (khissatan)
Recognize that the deficiencies are particularity (in quantity) and negation (in quality). A deficiency in quantity means the premise is particular; in quality, it means negative.
3
Apply the condition for the first state
If the minor is NOT affirmative particular, then the two deficiencies must NOT co-occur. They can be of one type (two particulars or two negatives in separate premises) or of two types (a particular and a negative in one premise or each in a different premise).
4
Apply the condition for the second state
If the minor is affirmative particular, the only condition is that the major premise must be universal negative. In this case, the two deficiencies may appear together.
5
Count the sterile moods
The author states that 11 moods are sterile in the fourth figure: 8 are eliminated by the first condition, and 3 by the second condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (۹۲) وَرَابِعٌ : عَدَمُ جَمْعِ الخِسَّتَيْن إِلَّا بِصُورَةٍ فَفِيهَا يَسْتَبِينُ (۹۳) صُغْرَاهُمَا مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّة كُبْرَاهُمَا سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّة ذَكَرَ أَنَّ الشَّكْلَ الرَّابِعَ لَهُ حَالَتَانِ يَخْتَلِفُ شَرْطُ إِنْتَاجِهِ بِاخْتِلَافِهِمَا، وَذَلِكَ أَنَّهُ إِذَا لَمْ تَكُنْ صُغْرَاهُ مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ، فَشَرْطُ إِنْتَاجِهِ : أَنْ لَا يَجْتَمِعَ فِيهِ خِسَّتَانِ مِنْ نَوْعِ وَاحِدٍ أَوْ مِنْ نَوْعَيْنِ فِي مُقَدِّمَةٍ أَوْ مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ ، وَخِسَّةُ الكَمِّ الجُزْئِيَّةُ وَخِسَّةُ الكَيْفِ السَّلْبُ، فَالخِسَّتَانِ مِنْ نَوْعِ وَاحِدٍ أَنْ يَشْتَمِلَ عَلَى جُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ أَوْ عَلَى سَالِبَتَيْنِ، وَلَا يَكُونَانِ إِلَّا فِي مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ وَالخِسَّتَانِ مِنْ نَوْعَيْنِ أَنْ يَشْتَمِلَ عَلَى جُزْنِيَّةٍ وَسَلْبٍ سَوَاءٌ اجْتَمَعَا فِي مُقَدِّمَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ أَوْ كَانَ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا فِي مُقَدِّمَةٍ ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ صُغْرَاهُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ فَلَيْسَ شَرْطُهُ ذَلِكَ ، بَلْ شَرْطُ إِنْتَاجِهِ أَنْ تَكُونَ كُبْرَاهُ سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ ،فَقَط ، وَإِنِ اسْتَبَانَ - أَيْ : ظَهَرَ - فِيهِ الخِسَتَانِ؛ إِذْ لَوْ كَانَتْ كُبْرَاهُ غَيْرَهَا أَوِ اجْتَمَعَ الخِسَتَانِ فِي الحَالَةِ الْأُوْلَى لَتَحَقَّقَ الاخْتِلَافُ المُوجِبُ لِلعُقْم ، فَالعَقِيمُ مِنْ ضُرُوبِ هَذَا الشَّكْلِ أَحَدَ عَشَرَ ضَرْباً ؛ لِأَنَّ شَرْطَ الحَالَةِ الأُوْلَى يُسْقِطْ ثَمَانِيَةَ ، وَشَرْطُ الحَالَةِ الثَّانِيَةِ يُسْقِطْ ثَلَاثَةٌ، وَذَلِكَ فِيهَا ظَاهِرٌ. وَبَيَانُهُ فِي الحَالَةِ الأَوْلَى : أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى فِيهَا إِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ جُزْئِيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ، وَهَذِهِ لِاجْتِمَاعِ الخِسَّتَيْنِ فِيهَا لَمْ تُنْتِجُ مَعَ شَيْءٍ مِنَ المَحْصُورَاتِ الأَرْبَعِ، فَأَضْرُبُهَا كُلُّهَا عَقِيمَةٌ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ، وَهَذِهِ لَا تُنْتِجُ إِلَّا مَعَ وَاحِدَةٍ وَهِيَ المُوجَبَةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ لِاجْتِمَاعِ الخِسَّتَيْنِ مَعَ مَا عَدَاهَا ، فَسَقَطَ مِنْ أَضْرُبهَا ثَلَاثَةٌ .
His statement: 'And the fourth: not gathering the two deficiencies except in a form in which it becomes clear (93) its minor is affirmative particular, its major is negative universal.' He mentioned that the fourth figure has two states, and its condition of productivity differs with their difference. That is: if its minor is not affirmative particular, then its condition of productivity is that two deficiencies do not gather in it, either of one type or of two types, in one premise or two premises. The deficiency of quantity is particularity, and the deficiency of quality is negation. The two deficiencies of one type are that it includes two particulars or two negatives, and they can only be in two premises. The two deficiencies of two types are that it includes a particular and a negative, whether they gather in a single premise or each is in a premise. And if its minor is affirmative particular, then its condition is not that, but rather its condition of productivity is only that its major is universal negative, even if the two deficiencies appear in it. For if its major were other than that, or if the two deficiencies gathered in the first state, then the difference that necessitates sterility would be realized. The sterile moods of this figure are eleven, because the condition of the first state drops eight, and the condition of the second state drops three, as is clear. Its explanation in the first state: the minor is either particular negative, and these, because of the gathering of the two deficiencies in it, do not produce with any of the four categorical propositions, so all its moods are sterile; or it is universal negative, and this only produces with one [proposition], namely the universal affirmative, because of the gathering of the two deficiencies with other than it, so three of its moods fall away.
Source pages p. 689

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for a valid syllogism in the fourth figure. The author first cites the matn stating that the fourth figure is unproductive unless the minor is affirmative particular and the major is negative universal. The sharh then clarifies: there are two states. In the first state, when the minor is not affirmative particular, the condition is that the two deficiencies (khissatan) do not co-occur. The two deficiencies are particularity (in quantity) and negation (in quality). They can be of one type (two particulars or two negatives, necessarily in separate premises) or of two types (a particular and a negative, in one premise or two). In the second state, when the minor is affirmative particular, the only condition is that the major is universal negative. The author then explains that the sterile moods total eleven: eight are eliminated by the first condition (which forbids the two deficiencies in non-particular-affirmative-minor cases), and three by the second condition (which requires the major to be universal negative). The explanation is given: when the minor is particular negative, it cannot produce with any of the four categoricals; when the minor is universal negative, it only produces with a universal affirmative. This systematic analysis shows how the fourth figure's moods are reduced to the productive ones.

عقم
شكل رابع

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure's productivity hinges on avoiding the combination of two types of deficiency (particularity and negation) unless the minor is affirmative particular, in which case only the major being universal negative suffices.

The Syllogism

Critique of al-Katibi's Condition for the Fourth Figure
Author critiques al-Katibi's condition and affirms necessary refinement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate al-Katibi's condition for the fourth figure and identify the required refinement that excludes sterile moods.

Core Idea
Al-Katibi's condition is too broad: avoiding the two deficiencies is insufficient; the minor must not be affirmative particular.

Al-Katibi proposed a condition for the fourth figure that would admit eight moods, including three that the earlier logicians considered sterile. The author of our passage demonstrates that the condition as stated is overinclusive, using a counterexample. The commentator's restriction is necessary to preserve the intended scope of productive moods. This lesson unpacks the critique and the refinement.

Guided Path
1
State al-Katibi's condition
Al-Katibi held that the fourth figure is productive if either both premises are affirmative with a universal minor, or they differ in quality with one universal. Write out the condition in your own words.
This condition is less restrictive than that of the author and earlier logicians.
2
Identify the moods admitted by al-Katibi
Under al-Katibi's condition, eight moods are productive. The passage lists three that involve differing quality with one universal: particular negative minor + universal affirmative major; its opposite; and universal negative minor + particular affirmative major. These three are the focus of the critique.
The author says these three are sterile according to the accepted view.
3
Understand the author's counterexample
The author gives an example that meets al-Katibi's condition (no gathering of the two deficiencies) yet does not produce: 'Some animals are human, every horse is an animal.' Check that both premises are affirmative, the minor is particular, and the major is universal. The minor is affirmative particular, which the author argues is the hidden cause of sterility.
This example shows that 'not having the two deficiencies' is not sufficient.
4
Formulate the refined condition
The commentator adds the clause: 'and that is if its minor is not affirmative particular.' So the correct condition includes both the avoidance of the two deficiencies and the additional restriction that the minor premise must not be affirmative particular. Write out the refined condition.
The author suggests adding a verse to the poem to make this explicit.

وأما الكاتبيُّ ومن تبعه فجعلوا ضابط إنتاج هذا الشكل أن يكون فيه إما إيجاب المقدمتين كلّيّة الصُّغرى، أو اختلافهما بالكيف مع كلِّيَّة أحدهما ، فدخل في الإنتاج بمقتضى هذا الشرط ثمانية أضرب ؛ لأنَّ اختلافهما بالكيف مع كلِّيَّة أحدهما يقتضي أن ينتج ثلاثة أضرب مما اجتمعت فيه الخستان: الأوَّل: جزئيَّة سالبة صغرى مع موجبة كلّيّة كبرى، والثَّاني عكسه، والثالث : كلية سالبة صغرى مع موجبة جزئية كبرى، وهذه الثَّلاثة عقيمة عند المصنّف والأقدمين، ولما كان ظاهر النظم يدلُّ على أنَّ الشَّرط هو عدم اجتماع الخصَّتين مطلقاً كانتِ الصُّغرى جزئية موجبة أم لا، وأنَّه متى لم تجتمع الخسَّتان حصل الإنتاج، وهو فاسد؛ إذ قولنا : «بعض الحيوان إنسان، وكلُّ فرس حيوان لم يجتمع فيه الخستان وهي مع ذلك لا تنتج، قيَّده الشارح بقوله : «وذلك إذا لم تكن صغراه موجبة جزئيّة، فلو زاد هذا البيت وهو : مَالَمْ تَكُنْ مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّه صُغْرَى فَمَعْ سَالِبَةٍ كُلِّيَّه لوفّي بالمرام.
As for al-Katibi and those who follow him, they made the condition for the productivity of this figure that either both premises are affirmative with the minor universal, or they differ in quality with one of them universal. So by this condition, eight moods enter into productivity, because the difference in quality with one universal necessitates that three moods wherein the two deficiencies gather are productive: first, a particular negative minor with a universal affirmative major; second, its opposite; third, a universal negative minor with a particular affirmative major. But these three are sterile according to the author and the ancients. And since the apparent meaning of the poem indicates that the condition is the non-gathering of the two deficiencies absolutely, whether the minor is affirmative particular or not, and that whenever the two deficiencies do not gather, production occurs—and this is false, because our statement 'Some animals are human, and every horse is an animal' does not have the two deficiencies gathering, yet it does not produce—the commentator restricted it by his statement: 'And that is if its minor is not affirmative particular.' So if he had added this verse: 'When the minor is not affirmative particular, then [it is] with a universal negative,' it would have fulfilled the purpose.
Source pages p. 690

Commentary
This passage critiques al-Katibi's alternative condition for the fourth figure. Al-Katibi held that productivity requires either both premises affirmative with a universal minor, or differing in quality with one universal. This would admit eight moods, including three that actually contain the two deficiencies (e.g., a particular negative minor with a universal affirmative major, etc.). The author (presumably the writer of the sharh or hashiya) rejects these as sterile, citing the older logicians' consensus. The author argues that the matn's apparent meaning—that the deficiency must be avoided absolutely—is too broad: it would include cases like 'Some animals are human, every horse is an animal' (which has no deficiency but does not produce). Therefore, the commentator (from earlier) correctly restricted the condition by adding the clause that the minor must not be affirmative particular. The author suggests adding a verse to clarify: when the minor is not affirmative particular, then it requires a universal negative major. This refinement ensures that only the truly productive moods are counted.

ضابط
خستين

Key Takeaway
The condition for the fourth figure must not only avoid the two deficiencies but also require that the minor premise is not affirmative particular.

Verify that it is productive using an Islamic example (e.g., 'Some animals are impermissible to eat, every pig is an animal' is not productive because minor is affirmative particular).

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Conditions for Productivity and Barren Modes in the Fourth Figure
Author states the condition for productivity and enumerates eleven barren premise combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two defects (khissatan) that cause barrenness in a categorical syllogism and apply the condition for productivity in the fourth figure to determine whether a given mood is productive.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's productivity depends on nuanced combinations of the two defects, not just their absence.

The author of the commentary on al-Sullam al-Munawwaq explains the precise conditions under which a fourth-figure syllogism yields a conclusion. He defines the two defects of particularity and negativity, then shows how their various combinations either allow or block inference. A careful analysis reveals eleven barren modes, and the commentator refines the rule to avoid a counterexample that would otherwise seem productive.

Guided Path
1
Define the two defects
Identify the two defects: particularity (juz'iyya) in quantity and negativity (salab) in quality. Note that they can appear in one or both premises.
The defects are called 'khissatan' (singular 'khissah').
2
Classify the defect combinations
Determine whether the defects are from one type (both particular or both negative) or from two types (one particular, one negative). Recognize that the condition for productivity varies based on this classification.
The two defects from one type occur only when each defect is in a separate premise; from two types, they can be in one or two premises.
3
Examine the special case of a particular affirmative minor
Understand that when the minor premise is particular affirmative, the condition for productivity is not the absence of both defects, but specifically that the major premise be universal negative. If the major is otherwise, the syllogism is barren.
This special case eliminates three modes.
4
Count the barren modes
Follow the author's counting: the first state (minor not particular affirmative) eliminates eight modes, and the second state (minor particular affirmative) eliminates three, totaling eleven barren modes in the fourth figure.
The counting depends on the quantified premise possibilities (affirmative/negative, universal/particular).
5
Compare with al-Katibi's rule and the commentator's correction
See how al-Katibi's broader rule would falsely admit three modes that are actually barren, and how the commentator restricts the condition to avoid a counterexample (e.g., 'Some animal is human, every horse is animal' has no defect combination but does not produce).
The correct condition is the one given by the commentator: when the minor is particular affirmative, the major must be universal negative.

شيخ الثاني على السلم المرونق أَوْ مِنْ نَوْعَيْنِ فِي مُقَدِّمَةٍ أَوْ مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَخِسَّةُ الكَمِّ الجُزْئِيَّةُ وَخِسَّةُ الكَيْفِ السَّلْبُ، فَالخِسَّتَانِ مِنْ نَوْعِ وَاحِدٍ أَنْ يَشْتَمِلَ عَلَى جُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ أَوْ عَلَى سَالِبَتَيْنِ، وَلَا يَكُونَانِ إِلَّا فِي مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ وَالخِسَّتَانِ مِنْ نَوْعَيْنِ أَنْ يَشْتَمِلَ عَلَى جُزْئِيَّةٍ وَسَلْبٍ سَوَاءٌ اجْتَمَعَا فِي مُقَدِّمَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ أَوْ كَانَ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا فِي مُقَدِّمَةٍ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ صُغْرَاهُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ فَلَيْسَ شَرْطُهُ ذَلِكَ، بَلْ شَرْطُ إِنْتَاجِهِ أَنْ تَكُونَ كُبْرَاهُ سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ فَقَط، وَإِنِ اسْتَبَانَ - أَيْ : ظَهَرَ - فِيهِ الخِسَتَانِ؛ إِذْ لَوْ كَانَتْ كُبْرَاهُ غَيْرَهَا أَوِ اجْتَمَعَ الخِسَتَانِ فِي الحَالَةِ الْأُوْلَى لَتَحَقَّقَ الاخْتِلَافُ المُوجِبُ لِلعُقْم، فَالعَقِيمُ مِنْ ضُرُوبِ هَذَا الشَّكْلِ أَحَدَ عَشَرَ ضَرْباً؛ لِأَنَّ شَرْطَ الحَالَةِ الأُوْلَى يُسْقِطْ ثَمَانِيَةَ، وَشَرْطُ الحَالَةِ الثَّانِيَةِ يُسْقِطْ ثَلَاثَةٌ، وَذَلِكَ فِيهَا ظَاهِرٌ. وَبَيَانُهُ فِي الحَالَةِ الأَوْلَى : أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى فِيهَا إِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ جُزْئِيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ، وَهَذِهِ لِاجْتِمَاعِ الخِسَّتَيْنِ فِيهَا لَمْ تُنْتِجُ مَعَ شَيْءٍ مِنَ المَحْصُورَاتِ الأَرْبَعِ، فَأَضْرُبُهَا كُلُّهَا عَقِيمَةٌ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ، وَهَذِهِ لَا تُنْتِجُ إِلَّا مَعَ وَاحِدَةٍ وَهِيَ المُوجَبَةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ لِاجْتِمَاعِ الخِسَّتَيْنِ مَعَ مَا عَدَاهَا، فَسَقَطَ مِنْ أَضْرُبهَا ثَلَاثَةٌ . قصارة . مع وأما الكاتبيُّ ومن تبعه فجعلوا ضابط إنتاج هذا الشكل أن يكون فيه إما إيجاب المقدمتين كلّيّة الصُّغرى، أو اختلافهما بالكيف مع كلِّيَّة أحدهما، فدخل في الإنتاج بمقتضى هذا الشرط ثمانية أضرب؛ لأنَّ اختلافهما بالكيف مع كلِّيَّة أحدهما يقتضي أن ينتج ثلاثة أضرب مما اجتمعت فيه الخستان: الأوَّل: جزئيَّة سالبة صغرى مع موجبة كلّيّة كبرى، والثَّاني عكسه، والثالث : كلية سالبة صغرى مع موجبة جزئية كبرى، وهذه الثَّلاثة عقيمة عند المصنّف والأقدمين، ولما كان ظاهر النظم يدلُّ على أنَّ الشَّرط هو عدم اجتماع الخصَّتين مطلقاً كانتِ الصُّغرى جزئية موجبة أم لا، وأنَّه متى لم تجتمع الخسَّتان حصل الإنتاج، وهو فاسد؛ إذ قولنا : «بعض الحيوان إنسان، وكلُّ فرس حيوان لم يجتمع فيه الخستان وهي مع ذلك لا تنتج، قيَّده الشارح بقوله : «وذلك إذا لم تكن صغراه موجبة جزئيّة، فلو زاد هذا البيت وهو : مَالَمْ تَكُنْ مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّه صُغْرَى فَمَعْ سَالِبَةٍ كُلِّيَّه لوفّي بالمرام.
[Commentary on al-Sullam al-Munawwaq] Or from two types in one premise or two premises. The defect of quantity is particularity, and the defect of quality is negativity. So the two defects from one type are that they include two particularities or two negatives, and they are only in two premises. The two defects from two types are that they include a particularity and a negativity, whether they come together in one premise or each is in a premise. And if its minor is particular affirmative, then its condition is not that; rather, the condition for its productivity is that its major be universal negative only, even if the two defects appear in it. For if its major were other than that, or the two defects came together in the first state, then the difference that necessitates barrenness would be realized. So the barren modes of this figure are eleven modes, because the condition of the first state eliminates eight, and the condition of the second state eliminates three, and that is clear in them. Its explanation in the first state: The minor in it is either particular negative, and this, due to the combination of the two defects in it, does not produce with any of the four quantified premises, so all its modes are barren. Or it is universal negative, and this produces only with one, namely the universal affirmative, because the two defects combine with others besides it, so three of its modes drop. A statement? With. As for al-Katibi and those who followed him, they made the rule for the productivity of this figure that there is either affirmation of both premises with universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with universality of one of them. So by virtue of this condition, eight modes enter into productivity, because their difference in quality with universality of one of them requires that three modes from those in which the two defects come together produce: first: particular negative minor with universal affirmative major; second: its opposite; third: universal negative minor with particular affirmative major. These three are barren according to the author and the ancients. And since the apparent meaning of the text indicates that the condition is the absence of the two defects absolutely, whether the minor is particular affirmative or not, and that whenever the two defects do not combine, production occurs—and that is false, for our statement 'Some animal is human, and every horse is animal' does not combine the two defects, yet it does not produce—so the commentator restricted it by his statement: 'That is when its minor is not particular affirmative.' So if he added this verse: 'As long as the minor is not particular affirmative, then with a universal negative [major]' it would fulfill the purpose.
Source pages p. 690

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on al-Sullam al-Munawwaq, discussing the conditions for productivity in the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by clarifying the meaning of 'two defects' (khissatayn): one type is both premises having the same defect (both particular or both negative) occurring in two premises; the other type is having one particular and one negative premise, which can be in one or both premises. He then states a key condition: if the minor premise is a particular affirmative, the condition for productivity is not the absence of both defects, but rather that the major premise must be a universal negative only. If the major is otherwise, or if the two defects combine in the first state, the syllogism is barren. He then counts the barren modes as eleven: the first state (where the minor is either particular negative or universal negative) eliminates eight modes, and the second state (the special case with particular affirmative minor) eliminates three. Then the author contrasts this with the view of al-Katibi and his followers, who held that productivity requires either both premises affirmative with the minor universal, or difference in quality with universality of one premise. According to them, eight modes are productive, including three that the author and earlier logicians consider barren. The commentator rejects al-Katibi's view, giving a counterexample: 'Some animal is human, and every horse is animal' has no combination of the two defects (since the minor is particular affirmative, which is not a defect per se, and the major is universal affirmative) but does not produce a conclusion. Therefore, the proper condition is the one given by the commentator: the minor must not be particular affirmative unless the major is universal negative. The passage ends with a suggested verse to clarify the condition.

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
First Two Productive Modes of the Fourth Figure
The author lists and illustrates the first two of the five productive modes of the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first two productive modes of the fourth figure, verify their conclusions, and reduce them to the first figure using transposition and conversion.

Core Idea
The fourth figure only yields particular conclusions; all productive modes reduce to the first figure by swapping premises and converting the conclusion.

The fourth figure of categorical syllogisms is the least intuitive, but its productive modes follow a clear pattern. The author first eliminates the barren modes through counterexamples, then presents the five productive modes, starting with two that produce particular affirmative conclusions. Both reduce to the first figure by transposition (making the minor the major and vice versa) and conversion of the conclusion. Understanding this reduction is key to seeing the logical necessity behind these modes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the barren modes
The author first shows which premise combinations yield no necessary conclusion. For the fourth figure, when the minor is universal affirmative, it only fails with a particular negative major. He provides counterexamples (naqd bil-mawad) to demonstrate that the same form can produce both true and false conclusions, proving barrenness.
The counterexamples use terms like human, animal, stone, horse – generic but effective.
2
Recognize the five productive modes
After eliminating eleven barren modes, five remain. The first two are: (1) two universal affirmatives → particular affirmative, (2) universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major → particular affirmative. In both, the conclusion is particular because the minor term can be more general than the major term.
This is the key difference from the first figure, where two universal affirmatives yield a universal affirmative.
3
Analyze the first mode (two universal affirmatives)
Example: 'Every possible is contingent' (major), 'Every created thing is possible' (minor). The conclusion is 'Some contingent is created.' The minor term 'created thing' is less general than 'contingent', so we cannot conclude 'Every contingent is created.' The syllogism is valid but only particular.
The reduction to the first figure: swap premises so that 'Every created thing is possible' becomes major, 'Every possible is contingent' becomes minor, then convert the conclusion to 'Some created thing is contingent' (which is the same as 'Some contingent is created').
4
Analyze the second mode (universal affirmative minor, particular affirmative major)
Example: 'Every possible is contingent' (major), 'Some existent is possible' (minor). Conclusion: 'Some contingent is existent.' Again, the conclusion is particular affirmative. Reduction: swap premises and convert conclusion to get 'Some existent is contingent' (equivalent).
The same reduction method works for all fourth-figure productive modes.
5
Apply the reduction method yourself
Take the example from kalam: 'Every created thing is contingent' and 'Every accident is created' (but this is two universals – we need a fourth figure where the middle term is predicate of major and subject of minor). Actually, the fourth figure requires the middle term to be predicate of the major and subject of the minor. So 'Every created thing is contingent' (M-P), 'Every accident is created' (S-M) gives conclusion 'Some contingent is accident'. Reduce by swapping premises and converting conclusion: 'Every accident is created' (major), 'Every created thing is contingent' (minor) gives 'Every accident is contingent' (first figure Barbara), then convert to 'Some contingent is accident'.
The conversion is just swapping subject and predicate of the universal affirmative to a particular affirmative.
