فصل في الأشكال ٦٩١ وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مُوجَبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةً وَهِيَ تُنْتِجُ مَعَ كُبْرَيَاتٍ ثَلَاثِ المُوجَبَتَيْنِ وَالكُلِّيَّةِ لِسَالِبَةٍ فَقَط وَلَا تُنْتِجُ مَعَ الجُزْئِيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ، فَسَقَطَ مِنْ أَضْرُبِهَا وَاحِدٌ، وَبِهِ تَكْمُلُ ثَمَانِيَةُ أَضْرُبٍ عَقِيمَةٍ الحَالَةِ الأُوْلَى، وَبَيَانُ عُقْمِهِ بِالنَّقْضِ بِالمَوَادِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ، وَبَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ أَوْ بَعْضُ الحَجَرِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ، وَالحَقُّ فِي الأَوَّلِ الإِيجَابُ وَفِي الثَّانِي السَّلْبُ فِي وَبَيَانُ عُقْمِ الأَضْرُبِ الثَّلَاثَةِ مَعَ الكُلِّيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ الصُّغْرَى أَنَّ أَخَصَّهَا ذُو الكُلِّيَّتَيْنِ، وَالِاخْتِلَافُ فِيهِ مُحَقَّقٌ تَقُولُ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الصَّاهِلِ أَوْ: مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِإِنْسَانٍ، وَالحَقُّ فِي الأَوَّلِ الإِيْجَابُ وَفِي الثَّانِي السَّلْبُ، وَيُفْهَمُ مِنْهُ عُقْمَ الآخَرَيْنِ؛ لأَنَّ مَا لَا يَلْزَمُ الأَخَصَّ لَا يَلْزَمُ الأعم. وَبَيَانُ عُقْمِ الأَرْبَعَةِ مَعَ الجُزْئِيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ الصُّغْرَى أَنَّ أَخَصَّهَا ذُو الكُبْرَى الكُلِّيَّةِ مُوجَبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ، وَالِاخْتِلَافُ فِيهِمَا مُحَقَّقُ، تَقُولُ مَعَ المُوجَبَةِ [ج / ٣٧]: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ وَكُلَّ صَاهِلٍ أَوْ كُلَّ حِمَارٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَالحَقُّ فِي الأَوَّلِ الإِنْجَابُ وَفِي الثَّانِي السَّلْبُ، وَتَقُولُ مَعَ السَّالِبَةِ : بَعْضُ الفَرَسِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ النَّاطِقِ أَوْ : مِنَ الحِمَارِ بِفَرَسٍ، وَالحَقُّ فِي الأَوَّلِ الإِيْجَابُ وَفِي الثَّانِي السَّلْبُ. بِفَرَ وَبَيَانُ ذَلِكَ فِي الحَالَةِ الثَّانِيَةِ : أَنَّ شَرْطَ كَوْنِ الكُبْرَى كُلِّيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ أَسْقَطَ الكُبْرِيَّاتِ الثَّلَاثَ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ، وَدَلِيلُ عُقْمِهَا النَّقْصُ؛ تَقُولُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ نَاطِقِ أَوْ كُلُّ فَرَسٍ حَيَوَانُ»، وَالحَقُّ فِي الأَوَّلِ الإِيْجَابُ وَفِي الثَّانِي السَّلْبُ، وَكَذَا بَاقِي الثَّلَاثِ، فَهَذِهِ أَحَدٌ عَشَرَ ضَرْباً كُلُّهَا عَقِيمَةٌ، وَبَقِيَتْ مِنَ السِّتَّةِ عَشَرَ خَمْسَةُ هِيَ المُنْتِجَةُ لِهَذَا الشَّكْلِ، وَقَدْ عَرَفْتَهَا مِمَّا ذَكَرْنَاهُ . وہ (١) - الضَّرْبُ الأَوَّلُ : مِنْ كُلِّيَتَيْنِ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ ، يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ؛ لِجَوَازِ كَوْنِ الأَصْغَرِ أَعَمَّ مِنَ الأَكْبَرِ؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلُّ مُمْكِن مُفْتَقِرٌ ، وَكُلُّ حَادِثِ مُمْكِنٌ يُنْتِجُ : «بَعْضُ المُفْتَقِرِ حَادِتٌ، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ بِالتَّبْدِيلِ ؛ أَيْ: جَعْلِ الصُّغْرَى كُبْرَى، وَالكُبْرَى صُغْرَى، وَعَكْسِ النَّيِّجَةِ . (٢) - الثَّانِي : مِنْ كُلِّيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ صُغْرَى، وَجُزْئِيَّةٍ مُوَجَبَةٍ كُبْرَى، يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ؛ نَحْرُ : كُلُّ مُمْكِن مُفْتَقِرُ، وَبَعْضُ المَوْجُودِ مُمْكِنٌ يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ المُفْتَقِرِ مَوْجُودٌ»، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ أَيْضاً بِالتَّبْدِيلِ، وَعَكْسِ النَّيِّيجَةِ .
Section on the Figures. 691. And as for [the case where the minor is] universal affirmative, it produces with three majors: the two affirmative and the universal negative only, and does not produce with the particular negative. So one of its modes drops, and with that the eight barren modes of the first state are completed. The explanation of its barrenness by counterexample with materials: you say, 'Every human is rational, and some animal is not human' or 'some stone is not human'; the truth in the first is affirmation and in the second is negation. And the explanation of the barrenness of the three modes with the universal negative minor: that the most specific of them is that which has two universals, and the difference in it is realized. You say: 'Nothing of human is horse, and nothing of neighing [or: of stone] is human'; the truth in the first is affirmation and in the second is negation. From this, the barrenness of the other two is understood, because what does not follow from the more specific does not follow from the more general. And the explanation of the barrenness of the four [modes] with the particular negative minor: that the most specific of them is that which has a universal major, whether affirmative or negative, and the difference in them is realized. With the affirmative [major] you say: 'Some animal is not [human] and every neighing [or: every donkey] is animal'; the truth in the first is affirmation and in the second is negation. With the negative [major] you say: 'Some horse is not human, and nothing of the rational [or: of the donkey] is horse'; the truth in the first is affirmation and in the second is negation. And the explanation of that in the second state: that the condition of the major being universal negative eliminates the three majors as previously, and the proof of their barrenness is deficiency; you say: 'Some animal is human, and every rational [or: every horse] is animal'; the truth in the first is affirmation and in the second is negation. Similarly for the remaining three. So these are eleven modes, all barren. And from the sixteen, five remain, which are the productive ones for this figure. And you have already known them from what we mentioned. (1) The first mode: from two universal affirmatives, it produces a particular affirmative, because it is possible for the minor term to be more general than the major term. Example: 'Every possible is contingent, and every created thing is possible' produces 'Some contingent is created.' It reduces to the first figure by transposition, i.e., making the minor a major and the major a minor, and converting the conclusion. (2) The second: from a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major, it produces a particular affirmative. Example: 'Every possible is contingent, and some existent is possible' produces 'Some contingent is existent.' It also reduces to the first figure by transposition and conversion of the conclusion.
Source pages p. 691

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of the discussion on barren modes and the enumeration of productive modes of the fourth figure. The author first completes the explanation of barren modes in the first state (where minor is not particular affirmative). He shows that when the minor is universal affirmative, it produces with three types of majors (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative) and fails only with particular negative. This gives eight barren modes for the first state. He then provides counterexamples (naqd bil-mawad) using terms like 'human', 'rational', 'animal', 'stone', 'horse', 'neighing', 'donkey' to demonstrate barrenness by showing that the same form can yield both true and false conclusions. After establishing the eleven barren modes, he states that five modes are productive. He describes the first two: (1) two universal affirmatives yield a particular affirmative, e.g., 'Every possible is contingent, every created thing is possible' gives 'Some contingent is created.' This reduces to the first figure (Barbara) by swapping the premises and converting the conclusion. (2) Universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major yield a particular affirmative, e.g., 'Every possible is contingent, some existent is possible' gives 'Some contingent is existent,' also reducible to the first figure. The author notes that the minor term may be more general than the major, preventing a universal conclusion. This follows the standard Aristotelian logic where the fourth figure (or Galen's fourth) has only particular conclusions.

Key Terms
الضَّرْب
التَّبْدِيل
عَكْس النَّتيجة

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure's first two productive modes both yield particular affirmative conclusions, and they reduce to the first figure by transposition of premises and conversion of the conclusion.

?

?
Verify the barrenness of the mode with universal affirmative minor and particular negative major by providing your own counterexample using Islamic terms, following the author's method of naqd bil-mawad.

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Eleven Sterile Moods of the Second Figure
Author enumerates the eleven sterile moods of the second figure and demonstrates their sterility via material counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify any sterile mood in the second figure and demonstrate its sterility using a material counterexample (naqd bi-l-mawad).

Core Idea
A sterile mood has true premises yet no necessary conclusion – proven by a single counterexample.

In the second figure of categorical syllogisms, not all premise combinations yield a valid conclusion. The logicians enumerate sixteen possible moods, but only five are productive. This lesson guides you through the eleven sterile ones and the method of exposing them: material counterexamples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second figure structure
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. The major premise must be universal negative for the mood to be productive. If the major is not universal negative, the mood may be sterile.
2
Recognize the three cases of sterile moods
The author groups sterile moods by the minor premise: universal affirmative (8 cases), universal negative (3 cases), and particular negative (4 cases). Each group is examined systematically.
3
Apply material counterexample (naqd bi-l-mawad)
To prove sterility, find a substitution for the terms such that both premises are true but the conclusion is not forced (sometimes true, sometimes false). If such a substitution exists, no necessary conclusion follows.
4
Work through the author's examples
For example, with minor universal affirmative and major particular negative, the author uses 'Every human is rational' and 'Some animal is not human'. Here, the conclusion could be 'Some animal is not rational' (false) or 'Some rational is not human' (true), so it is indeterminate.
The author's examples may not strictly adhere to second figure form, but they illustrate the method.
5
Use the 'most specific' technique
For groups of sterile moods, the author identifies the most specific combination (e.g., both premises universal) and demonstrates its sterility. The other, more general moods are then sterile by extension because what fails for the specific fails for the general.

فصل في الأشكال. وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مُوجَبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةً وَهِيَ تُنْتِجُ مَعَ كُبْرَيَاتٍ ثَلَاثِ المُوجَبَتَيْنِ وَالكُلِّيَّةِ لِسَالِبَةٍ فَقَط وَلَا تُنْتِجُ مَعَ الجُزْئِيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ ، فَسَقَطَ مِنْ أَضْرُبِهَا وَاحِدٌ، وَبِهِ تَكْمُلُ ثَمَانِيَةُ أَضْرُبٍ عَقِيمَةٍ الحَالَةِ الأُوْلَى، وَبَيَانُ عُقْمِهِ بِالنَّقْضِ بِالمَوَادِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ، وَبَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ أَوْ بَعْضُ الحَجَرِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ، وَالحَقُّ فِي الأَوَّلِ الإِيجَابُ وَفِي الثَّانِي السَّلْبُ. وَبَيَانُ عُقْمِ الأَضْرُبِ الثَّلَاثَةِ مَعَ الكُلِّيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ الصُّغْرَى أَنَّ أَخَصَّهَا ذُو الكُلِّيَّتَيْنِ، وَالِاخْتِلَافُ فِيهِ مُحَقَّقٌ تَقُولُ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الصَّاهِلِ أَوْ: مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِإِنْسَانٍ ، وَالحَقُّ فِي الأَوَّلِ الإِيْجَابُ وَفِي الثَّانِي السَّلْبُ، وَيُفْهَمُ مِنْهُ عُقْمَ الآخَرَيْنِ ؛ لأَنَّ مَا لَا يَلْزَمُ الأَخَصَّ لَا يَلْزَمُ الأعم. وَبَيَانُ عُقْمِ الأَرْبَعَةِ مَعَ الجُزْئِيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ الصُّغْرَى أَنَّ أَخَصَّهَا ذُو الكُبْرَى الكُلِّيَّةِ مُوجَبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ ، وَالاخْتِلَافُ فِيهِمَا مُحَقَّقُ ، تَقُولُ مَعَ المُوجَبَةِ [ج / ۳۷]: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ وَكُلَّ صَاهِلٍ أَوْ كُلَّ حِمَارٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَالحَقُّ فِي الأَوَّلِ الإِنْجَابُ وَفِي الثَّانِي السَّلْبُ، وَتَقُولُ مَعَ السَّالِبَةِ : بَعْضُ الفَرَسِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ النَّاطِقِ أَوْ : مِنَ الحِمَارِ بِفَرَسٍ ، وَالحَقُّ فِي الأَوَّلِ الإِيْجَابُ وَفِي الثَّانِي السَّلْبُ. وَبَيَانُ ذَلِكَ فِي الحَالَةِ الثَّانِيَةِ : أَنَّ شَرْطَ كَوْنِ الكُبْرَى كُلِّيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ أَسْقَطَ الكُبْرَيَاتِ الثَّلَاثَ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ ، وَدَلِيلُ عُقْمِهَا النَّقْصُ؛ تَقُولُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ نَاطِقِ أَوْ كُلُّ فَرَسٍ حَيَوَانُ»، وَالحَقُّ فِي الأَوَّلِ الإِيْجَابُ وَفِي الثَّانِي السَّلْبُ، وَكَذَا بَاقِي الثَّلَاثِ، فَهَذِهِ أَحَدٌ عَشَرَ ضَرْباً كُلُّهَا عَقِيمَةٌ.
Chapter on the figures. And if it is universal affirmative, it produces with three major premises: the two affirmative and the universal negative only, and does not produce with the particular negative. So one of its moods falls away, and with it eight sterile moods are completed in the first case. And the demonstration of its sterility by counterexample with materials is to say: 'Every human is rational', and 'Some animal is not human' or 'Some stone is not human', and the truth in the first is affirmative and in the second negative. And the demonstration of the sterility of the three moods with the universal negative minor is that the most specific of them is the one with two universal premises, and the difference in it is verified. You say: 'Nothing of human is a horse', and 'Nothing of the neighing [thing] or of the stone is a human', and the truth in the first is affirmative and in the second negative. From that the sterility of the other two is understood, because what is not necessary for the more specific is not necessary for the more general. And the demonstration of the sterility of the four with the particular negative minor is that the most specific of them is the one with the major premise universal, either affirmative or negative, and the difference in them is verified. You say with the affirmative: 'Some animal is not something', and 'Every neighing thing or every donkey is an animal', and the truth in the first is affirmative and in the second negative. And you say with the negative: 'Some horse is not human', and 'Nothing of the rational or of the donkey is a horse', and the truth in the first is affirmative and in the second negative. And the demonstration of that in the second case is that the condition of the major being universal negative dropped the three major premises as previously, and the proof of its sterility is deficiency. You say: 'Some animal is human', and 'Every rational or every horse is an animal', and the truth in the first is affirmative and in the second negative, and likewise the remaining three. So these are eleven moods, all sterile.
Source pages p. 691

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration and proof of the sterile moods in the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The second figure requires that the major premise be a universal negative for a valid conclusion. The author breaks down the possible combinations of premises (16 moods) and shows which ones are sterile. First, he considers the case where the minor premise is universal affirmative: such a minor can only produce conclusions with three types of major (the two affirmatives and the universal negative), but not with the particular negative, so one mood falls away, making eight sterile moods in this category. To demonstrate sterility, he uses material counterexamples (naqd bi-l-mawad). For example, from 'Every human is rational' and 'Some animal is not human' (or 'some stone is not human') the conclusion would be both true and false depending on the substitution, so no necessary conclusion holds. Next, he treats the case of a universal negative minor: the most specific sterile mood is when both premises are universal, and by counterexample (e.g., 'Nothing human is a horse' and 'Nothing neighing is a human') we see the conclusion is not necessary. The other two follow because what fails for the specific fails for the general. Then he treats the case of a particular negative minor: the most specific is when the major is universal (affirmative or negative). Counterexamples are given for both. Finally, he discusses the second case (the condition that the major must be universal negative) which drops three possible majors, and the sterility is shown by a deficiency counterexample. In total, eleven moods are sterile, leaving five productive moods.

Five Productive Moods of the Second Figure
Author lists the five productive moods of the second figure with examples and reduction methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five productive moods of the second figure by their premise types and conclusions, and describe the reduction method (tabdīl / 'aks) for each.

Core Idea
The second figure has five productive moods, all reduced to the first via premise swapping and conclusion conversion.

In the previous lesson, you learned the conditions for valid syllogisms and the sterile moods of the second figure. Now we examine the five productive moods that yield valid conclusions, using examples from kalām cosmology. Mastery of these moods and their reduction techniques is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood by premise types
For each of the five moods, note the quantifier (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of both premises. The author lists them in order: e.g., first mood: both universal and affirmative.
Use the key terms 'kulliyya' (universal) and 'juz'iyya' (particular).
2
Determine the conclusion
Check whether the conclusion is universal or particular, and affirmative or negative. The conclusion's quantifier and quality are uniquely determined by the premise types.
The reason for particularity in the first two moods is that the minor term may be more general than the major.
3
Recognize the example's term pattern
The examples use terms like 'possible', 'contingent', 'originated', 'eternal', 'perishable'. Note how the middle term appears in each premise.
In the second figure, the middle term is predicate of both premises (though the examples given are after reduction, focus on the original structure).
4
Apply the reduction technique
Each mood reduces to the first figure either by tabdīl (swapping premises and converting conclusion) or by 'aks (converting both premises). Practice the reduction for each mood using the described method.
Tabdīl is used for moods 1-3; 'aks of both premises for moods 4-5.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبَقِيَتْ مِنَ السِّتَّةِ عَشَرَ خَمْسَةُ هِيَ المُنْتِجَةُ لِهَذَا الشَّكْلِ، وَقَدْ عَرَفْتَهَا مِمَّا ذَكَرْنَاهُ . (١) - الضَّرْبُ الأَوَّلُ : مِنْ كُلِّيَتَيْنِ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ ، يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ؛ لِجَوَازِ كَوْنِ الأَصْغَرِ أَعَمَّ مِنَ الأَكْبَرِ؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلُّ مُمْكِن مُفْتَقِرٌ ، وَكُلُّ حَادِثِ مُمْكِنٌ يُنْتِجُ : «بَعْضُ المُفْتَقِرِ حَادِثٌ، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ بِالتَّبْدِيلِ ؛ أَيْ: جَعْلِ الصُّغْرَى كُبْرَى، وَالكُبْرَى صُغْرَى، وَعَكْسِ النَّتِيجَةِ . (٢) - الثَّانِي : مِنْ كُلِّيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ صُغْرَى، وَجُزْئِيَّةٍ مُوَجَبَةٍ كُبْرَى، يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ ; نَحْوُ : كُلُّ مُمْكِن مُفْتَقِرُ، وَبَعْضُ المَوْجُودِ مُمْكِنٌ يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ المُفْتَقِرِ مَوْجُودٌ»، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ أَيْضاً بِالتَّبْدِيلِ، وَعَكْسِ النَّتِيجَةِ . (٣) - الثَّالِثُ : مِنْ كُلِّيَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ صُغْرَى، وَكُلِّيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ كُبْرَى، يُنْتِجُ : كُلِّيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ ، نَحْوُ : «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ المُمْكِنِ بِقَدِيمٍ، وَكُلُّ فَانٍ مُمْكِنٌ» يُنْتِجُ : «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ القَدِيمِ بِفَانٍ»، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ أَيْضاً بِالتَّبْدِيلِ، وَعَكْسِ النَّتِيجَةِ . (٤) - الرَّابِعُ : عَكْسُ الثَّالِثِ ، يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ ; نَحْوُ : كُلُّ فَانٍ مُمْكِنٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ القَدِيمِ بِفَانٍ يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ المُمْكِنِ لَيْسَ بِقَدِيمٍ، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ مَعاً. (٥) - الخَامِسُ : مِنْ جُزْئِيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ صُغْرَى ، وَكُلِّيَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ كُبْرَى; نَحْوُ : بَعْضُ المَوْجُودِ حَادِثٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ المُمْتَنِعِ بِمَوْجُودٍ يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ الحَادِثِ لَيْسَ بِمُمْتَنِعٍ، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِهِمَا أَيْضاً . وَوَجْهُ تَرْتِيبِ هَذِهِ الأَضْرُبِ : أَنَّ الأَوَّلَ أَخَصُّ الضُّرُوبِ المُنْتِجَةِ لِلإِيجَابِ، وَالثَّانِي مُشَارِكُهُ فِي إِيْجَابِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَالثَّالِثُ سَهْلُ الرَّدْ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ بِالتَّبْدِيلِ، وَالرَّابِعُ أَخَصُّ مِنَ الخَامِسِ.
And there remained from the sixteen, five that are productive for this figure, and you have known them from what we mentioned. (1) The first mood: from two universal premises, both affirmative; it produces a particular affirmative, because it is possible that the minor be more general than the major. For example: 'Every possible is contingent', and 'Every originated thing is possible' produces 'Some contingent is originated'. And it reduces to the first figure by permutation (tabdīl), i.e., making the minor the major and the major the minor, and inverting the conclusion. (2) The second: from a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major; it produces a particular affirmative. For example: 'Every possible is contingent', and 'Some existing thing is possible' produces 'Some contingent is existing'. And it also reduces to the first figure by permutation and inversion of the conclusion. (3) The third: from a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major; it produces a universal negative. For example: 'Nothing possible is eternal', and 'Every perishable is possible' produces 'Nothing eternal is perishable'. And it also reduces to the first figure by permutation and inversion of the conclusion. (4) The fourth: the converse of the third; it produces a particular negative. For example: 'Every perishable is possible', and 'Nothing eternal is perishable' produces 'Some possible is not eternal'. And it reduces to the first figure by inverting both premises together. (5) The fifth: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major; for example: 'Some existing thing is originated', and 'Nothing impossible is existing' produces 'Some originated is not impossible'. And it reduces to the first figure by inverting both of them as well. And the reason for the ordering of these moods: the first is the most specific of the productive moods for the affirmative, the second shares with it in having both premises affirmative, the third is easy to reduce to the first by permutation, and the fourth is more specific than the fifth.
Source pages p. 691

Commentary
This passage enumerates the five productive moods of the second figure. After eliminating the eleven sterile moods, these five remain. The author lists each with its premise types, conclusion, an example from the realm of possibility and contingency (common in kalām cosmology), and the method of reduction to the first figure. The first mood has two universal affirmatives and yields a particular affirmative conclusion. The reason for particularity is that the minor term may be more general than the major. The reduction is by 'tabdīl' (permutation): swapping the premises and converting the conclusion. The second mood has a universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major, also producing a particular affirmative, and reduces similarly. The third mood has a universal negative minor and universal affirmative major, producing a universal negative; it reduces by permutation and conversion of the conclusion. The fourth mood is the converse arrangement of the third: universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, producing a particular negative; it reduces by inverting both premises (i.e., conversion of each premise). The fifth mood has a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, producing a particular negative; it also reduces by inverting both premises. The author then explains the ordering rationale: the first is the most specific among affirmative-producing moods, the second shares the affirmative character, the third is easiest to reduce, and the fourth is more specific than the fifth.

Key Terms
التبديل
عكس النتيجة
منتج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the correct reduction method for the first productive mood of the second figure (two universal affirmatives) to the first figure?
A) Invert both premises only
B) Swap the premises and invert the conclusion
C) Convert the minor and major separately
D) No reduction is possible

Key Takeaway
The second figure's five productive moods are distinguished by premise quantifiers and quality, and each reduces to the first figure through tabdīl (premise swapping) and/or 'aks al-natīja (conclusion conversion).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a second-figure syllogism using the first productive mood (two universal affirmatives) on a topic from fiqh, such as: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore, some forbidden is nabidh?' Check if the conclusion follows and reduce it to the first figure via tabdīl.
?
Take the third mood (universal negative minor, universal affirmative major) and reduce it to the first figure by swapping premises and converting the conclusion. Then verify that the reduced syllogism is valid in the first figure.

Total Productive Moods in the Four Figures
Summary of productive mood counts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of productive moods in each syllogistic figure and verify their total sum.

Core Idea
Only 19 out of 64 possible mood-figure combinations are productive.

You have learned the conditions for validity in each figure. The author now provides a final tally: first figure has 4 productive moods, second also 4, third 6, and fourth 5. This summary is essential for quickly assessing any syllogism's validity.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four figures
Briefly review the middle term positions: first figure (M-P, S-M), second (P-M, S-M), third (M-P, M-S), fourth (P-M, M-S).
Each figure has its own requirements for the quantity and quality of premises.
2
Identify the productive moods for each figure
From the passage: first = 4, second = 4, third = 6, fourth = 5. These are the only moods that yield conclusions.
These numbers are based on the classical conditions: e.g., first figure requires universal major and affirmative minor.
3
Verify the total
Add: 4+4+6+5 = 19. This is the total number of productive moods.
The author explicitly states 'total is nineteen moods'.
4
Contrast with total possible moods
Each figure has 16 possible combinations of propositions (A,E,I,O for major and minor). Four figures give 64. Productive 19 means 45 are sterile.
The passage also mentions this: 'productive among them are nineteen, sterile are forty-five'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (٩٤) فَمُنْتِجُ لِأَوَّلِ : أَرْبَعَةُ، كَـ «الثَّانِ، ثُمَّ «ثَالِتٌ»: فَسِنَّةُ (٩٥) وَرَابِعُ : بِخَمْسَةٍ قَدْ أَنْتَجَا وَغَيْرُ مَا ذَكَرْتُهُ لَنْ يُنْتِجَا هَذِهِ فَذْلَكَةٌ حَصَّلَ فِيهَا عَدَدَ المُنْتِج مِنْ ضُرُوبِ الأَشْكَالِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ، وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ تَمْثِيلُهَا مُرَتَّبَةٌ، وَمَجْمُوعُهَا تِسْعَةَ عَشَرَ ضَرْباً . وَأَشَارَ بِقَوْلِهِ : (وَغَيْرُ مَا ذَكَرْتُهُ لَنْ يُنْتِجَا إِلَى أَنَّ البَاقِي مِنْ أَضْرُبٍ كُلِّ شَكْلٍ هُوَ العَقِيمُ، وَقَدْ مَرَّ أَنَّ أَضْرُبَ كُلِّ شَكْلِ سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ، تُضْرَبُ فِي أَرْبَعَةِ أَشْكَالِ، بِأَرْبَعَةٍ وَسِتِّيْنَ، المُنْتِجُ مِنْهَا تِسْعَةَ عَشَرَ ، وَالعَقِيمُ مَا بَقِيَ وَهُوَ خَمْسَةٌ وَأَرْبَعُونَ ضَرْباً . وَقَدْ جَرَتْ عَادَةُ كَثِيرٍ مِنَ الشُّرَّاحِ بِوَضْعِ جَدَاوِلَ هُنَا تَتَضَمَّنُ جَمِيعَ الضُّرُوبِ المُنْتِجَ مِنْهَا وَالعَقِيمَ، مُمَتِّلِينَ لَهَا بِالأَخَرُفِ عَلَى عَادَتِهِمْ، وَقَدْ تَرَكْتُ ذَلِكَ لِقِلَّةِ جَدْوَاهُ، مَعَ مَا فِي تِلْكَ الأَحْرُفِ مِنَ التَّخْلِيطِ، وَاللهُ تَعَالَى المُوَفِّقُ .
His saying: (94) 'So productive for the first: four, like the second, then third: six (95) and fourth: five have produced, and other than what I mentioned will not produce.' This is a summary in which the number of productive moods of the four figures is obtained, and their examples have preceded, arranged. Their total is nineteen moods. And he indicated by his saying 'and other than what I mentioned will not produce' that the remaining moods of each figure are sterile. And it has passed that the moods of each figure are sixteen, multiplied by four figures gives sixty-four, the productive among them are nineteen, and the sterile are the remainder, which is forty-five moods. And the practice of many commentators has been to place tables here containing all the productive and sterile moods, representing them with letters according to their custom. And I have left that due to its little benefit, along with the confusion in those letters. And Allah Almighty is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 692

Commentary
This passage is a concluding summary from the commentary on the matn (likely al-Sullam al-Murawniq). The author presents the total count of productive moods across all four figures: first figure has 4, second has 4? Wait, the text says 'like the second' meaning the second also has 4? Actually the verse says 'for the first: four, like the second, then third: six, and fourth: five'. So first=4, second=4, third=6, fourth=5, total 19. The author clarifies that these are the only productive moods; the rest (45) are sterile. He also notes that many commentators insert tables of all moods using letters, but he omits them because they are of little benefit and prone to confusion. This reflects a practical approach: the student should focus on the productive moods rather than memorizing all 64 possibilities.

Key Terms
فذلكة
ضرب منتج
ضرب عقيم

Key Takeaway

?

Why the Author Avoided Tabulation
Critique of tabulation practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the author's reason for not including tables of all moods and evaluate the pedagogical value of such tables.

Core Idea
Tables of all moods with letters cause confusion rather than clarity.

Many commentators insert elaborate tables showing all 64 moods, but the author of this commentary deliberately omits them. Understanding his reasoning will sharpen your own pedagogical judgment and help you avoid unnecessary complexity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common practice
The passage states that 'the practice of many commentators has been to place tables here containing all the productive and sterile moods, representing them with letters'.
Letters were often used to represent the mood, e.g., A for universal affirmative, etc.
2
Understand the author's objection
The author says he 'left that due to its little benefit, along with the confusion in those letters'.
He finds the tables unhelpful and confusing.
3
Analyse the reason
The tables may obscure the essential logical principles. The student should focus on the conditions that make a mood productive rather than memorizing a large grid of letter combinations.
This reflects a prioritization of understanding over rote memorization.
4
Apply the lesson to your own study
When learning logic, avoid over-reliance on tables. Instead, internalize the rules: e.g., in the first figure, the major premise must be universal and the minor affirmative.
This approach is more robust for real reasoning.

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Productive Moods of the First Figure and Their Reduction
Author enumerates the productive moods of the first figure and totals nineteen across all figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the productive moods of the first figure from their premise types and reduce them to the first mood using conversion and contraposition.

Core Idea
Nineteen productive moods exist; most reduce to the first figure's first mood.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the matn lines that list the productive moods of the first figure. We see the third, fourth, and fifth moods, each defined by a specific combination of premise quantity and quality. The commentary also explains how each mood can be reduced to the first mood using logical transformations. This is essential for mastering syllogistic validity in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
Each mood is defined by the quantity and quality of its minor and major premises. For example, the third mood has a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major. Locate these descriptors in the Arabic.
Use the terms: كُلِّيَّة سَالِبَة (universal negative), كُلِّيَّة مُوجَبَة (universal affirmative), جُزْئِيَّة مُوجَبَة (particular affirmative).
2
Read the example syllogism
Each mood is illustrated with a kalam example. For the third mood: 'Nothing possible is eternal, and everything perishable is possible' produces 'Nothing eternal is perishable.' Verify that the premises and conclusion match the mood's description.
The terms are from kalam: possible (مُمْكِن), eternal (قَدِيم), perishable (فَانٍ).
3
Understand the reduction method
The commentary states that each mood reduces to the first mood (the first productive mood) by operations like conversion (عَكْس) and contraposition (تَبْدِيل) of the conclusion or of both premises. For the third mood, reduction uses conversion and contraposition of the conclusion.
Conversion swaps subject and predicate; contraposition (also called 'aks al-naqid) involves negating both and swapping.
4
Total the productive moods across figures
The matn and commentary state that the first figure has 4 productive moods, the second has 4, the third has 6, and the fourth has 5, totalling 19. Out of 64 possible mood-figure combinations, 45 are sterile.
Memorize these numbers: 4+4+6+5=19. This is standard in the Sullam tradition.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳) - الثَّالِثُ : مِنْ كُلِّيَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ صُغْرَى، وَكُلِّيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ كُبْرَى، يُنْتِجُ : كُلِّيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ ، نَحْوُ : «لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ المُمْكِنِ بِقَدِيمٍ، وَكُلُّ فَانٍ مُمْكِنٌ» يُنْتِجُ : «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ القَدِيمِ بِفَانٍ»، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ أَيْضاً بِالتَّبْدِيلِ، وَعَكْسِ النَّتيجَةِ .
(٤) - الرَّابِعُ : عَكْسُ الثَّالِثِ ، يُنْتِجُ : جُزْئِيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلُّ فَانٍ مُمْكِنٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ القَدِيمِ بِفَانٍ يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ المُمْكِنِ لَيْسَ بِقَدِيمِ، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ مَعاً.
(٥) - الخَامِسُ : مِنْ جُزْئِيَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ صُغْرَى ، وَكُلِّيَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ كُبْرَى؛ نَحْوُ : بَعْضُ المَوْجُودِ حَادِثٌ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ المُمْتَنِعِ بِمَوْجُودِ يُنْتِجُ : بَعْضُ الحَادِثِ لَيْسَ بِمُمْتَنِعٍ، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِهِمَا أَيْضاً . وَوَجْهُ تَرْتِيبِ هَذِهِ الأَضْرُبِ : أَنَّ الأَوَّلَ أَخَصُّ الضُّرُوبِ المُنْتِجَةِ لِلإِيجَابِ، وَالثَّانِي مُشَارِكُهُ فِي إِيْجَابِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَالثَّالِثُ سَهْلُ الرَّدْ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ بِالتَّبْدِيلِ، وَالرَّابِعُ أَخَصُّ مِنَ الخَامِسِ.
قَوْلُهُ : (٩٤) فَمُنْتِجُ لِأَوَّلِ : أَرْبَعَةُ، كَـ «الثَّانِ، ثُمَّ «ثَالِتٌ»: فَسِنَّةُ (٩٥) وَرَابِعُ : بِخَمْسَةٍ قَدْ أَنْتَجَا. وَغَيْرُ مَا ذَكَرْتُهُ لَنْ يُنْتِجَا. هَذِهِ فَذْلَكَةٌ حَصَّلَ فِيهَا عَدَدَ المُنْتِج مِنْ ضُرُوبِ الأَشْكَالِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ، وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ تَمْثِيلُهَا مُرَتَّبَةٌ، وَمَجْمُوعُهَا تِسْعَةَ عَشَرَ ضَرْباً . وَأَشَارَ بِقَوْلِهِ : (وَغَيْرُ مَا ذَكَرْتُهُ لَنْ يُنْتِجَا) إِلَى أَنَّ البَاقِي مِنْ أَضْرُبٍ كُلِّ شَكْلٍ هُوَ العَقِيمُ، وَقَدْ مَرَّ أَنَّ أَضْرُبَ كُلِّ شَكْلِ سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ، تُضْرَبُ فِي أَرْبَعَةِ أَشْكَالِ، بِأَرْبَعَةٍ وَسِتِّيْنَ، المُنْتِجُ مِنْهَا تِسْعَةَ عَشَرَ ، وَالعَقِيمُ مَا بَقِيَ وَهُوَ خَمْسَةٌ وَأَرْبَعُونَ ضَرْباً .
(3) The third [mood] is from a universal negative minor premise and a universal affirmative major premise; it produces a universal negative conclusion. Example: 'Nothing possible is eternal, and everything perishable is possible' produces 'Nothing eternal is perishable.' It also reduces to the first [mood] by conversion and contraposition of the conclusion. (4) The fourth is the converse of the third; it produces a particular negative conclusion. Example: 'Everything perishable is possible, and nothing eternal is perishable' produces 'Some possible is not eternal.' It reduces to the first by converting both premises together. (5) The fifth is from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major. Example: 'Some existent is originated, and nothing impossible is existent' produces 'Some originated is not impossible.' It also reduces to the first by converting both. The reason for ordering these moods: the first is the most specific of the productive moods for affirmation, the second shares with it in the affirmation of both premises, the third is easy to reduce to the first by conversion, and the fourth is more specific than the fifth. His saying: '(94) So the productive [moods] for the first [figure] are four; then for the second, then the third: six; (95) and the fourth: five have produced. And other than what I mentioned will not produce.' This is a summary that gives the number of productive moods from the four figures, and their total is nineteen. And by his saying 'And other than what I mentioned will not produce' he indicates that the remainder of the moods of each figure are sterile. It has been mentioned that the moods of each figure are sixteen; multiplied by four figures gives sixty-four; the productive among them are nineteen, and the sterile are the rest, which is forty-five moods.
Source pages p. 692

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expansion on the matn lines that enumerate the productive moods of the four figures of the categorical syllogism. The commentary first lists the third, fourth, and fifth moods of the first figure (the first two were given earlier). Each mood is defined by the quantity and quality of its premises and conclusion, and an example is given from the Islamic theological context: e.g., 'Nothing possible is eternal' uses terms from kalam about contingency and preeternity. The commentary then explains the rationale for ordering these moods: the first is the most specific affirmative, etc. It then quotes the matn lines that state the number of productive moods for each figure: first figure has 4, second has 4? Wait: 'أَرْبَعَةُ' for first, then 'ثُمَّ «ثَالِتٌ»: فَسِنَّةُ' meaning second? Actually matn says: first figure: four; second figure: ? The OCR is corrupted but traditional count: first figure 4, second figure 4, third figure 6, fourth figure 5. The commentary confirms total 19. It then explains that out of 16 possible mood-figure combinations per figure, only 19 total are productive; the rest (45) are sterile. This is a standard result in Aristotelian logic as taught in the Sullam tradition.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Conclusion Follows the Lower Premise: Condition for Universal Conclusion
Author explains that the conclusion follows the inferior premise in quality and quantity, and critiques that universal premises always yield universal conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the quality and quantity of a categorical syllogism's conclusion from its premises and identify when a universal conclusion requires the minor term to be universally distributed.

Core Idea
The conclusion takes the lower quality/quantity; universal conclusion from two universal premises needs the minor term distributed.

In this lesson, we examine a cardinal rule of categorical syllogisms: the conclusion always follows the lower (akhass) of the premises in both quality (affirmative vs. negative) and quantity (universal vs. particular). The author of the matn states this rule, and the commentator elaborates. However, the commentator also points out a critical refinement: while the rule implicitly suggests that two universal premises always yield a universal conclusion, this is not unconditionally true. A further condition—the universal distribution of the minor term—must be satisfied. This lesson will help you apply this rule correctly and avoid a common logical pitfall.

Guided Path
1
Learn the basic rule
The conclusion follows the lower quality (negation over affirmation) and lower quantity (particular over universal). If one premise is negative, the conclusion is negative; if one is particular, the conclusion is particular.
2
Apply the rule to a mixed case
If one premise is particular affirmative and the other universal negative, the conclusion is particular negative. This combination is the lowest in both aspects.
3
Recognize the implied claim
The rule's implication suggests that if both premises are universal, the conclusion should be universal. But is that always true?
4
Identify the condition
The commentator states that for the conclusion to be universal when both premises are universal, the minor term must be universally distributed in the minor premise or in its converse (if the minor term is the predicate, it must be distributed after conversion).
5
Test with examples
Example: Both premises universal, minor not distributed → conclusion cannot be universal. Example: If minor is distributed, universal conclusion is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [في الأشكال]
وَلَمَّا كَانَ حَالُ النَّتِيجَةِ يَخْتَلِفُ بِالكَمْ وَالكَيْفِ، أَشَارَ المُصَنِّفُ إِلَى ضَابِطٍ يُعْرَفُ بِهِ حَالُهَا ، فَقَالَ: (٩٦) وَتَتْبَعُ النَّتِيجَةُ الأَخَـرَّ مِنْ تِلْكَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ هَكَذَا زُكِنْ وَخِسَّةُ الكَيْفِ : السَّلْبُ، وَخِسَّةُ الكَم : الجُزْيَّةُ . وَقَوْلُهُ : «زُكِنْ بِالبِنَاءِ لِلمَفْعُولِ ؛ أَي : عُلِمَ؛ وَالمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ مَتَى كَانَ فِي إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ أَوْ فِيهِمَا خِسَّةَ اكْتَسَبَتْهَا النَّتِيجَةُ، فَإِذَا كَانَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا سَالِبَةٌ فَالنَّتِيجَةُ سَالِبَةٌ، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا جُزْئِيَّةٌ فَالنَّتِيجَةُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ ، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ وَالأُخْرَى كُلِّيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ فَالنَّتِيجَةُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ، وَفِي ذَلِكَ قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : إِنَّ الزَّمَانَ لَتَابِعِ أَرْذَالَهُ تَبْعَ النَّـتِـيـجَـةِ لِلأَخَسُ الْأَرْذَلِ. وَهَذَا المَنْظُوقُ فِي كَلَامِ النَّاظِمِ مُسَلَّمٌ، وَدَلَّ مَنْهُومُهُ عَلَى أَمْرَيْنِ : أَحَدُهُمَا : أَنَّهُ مَتَى كَانَتِ المُقَدِّمَتَانِ مُوجَبَيْنِ مَعاً ، فَالرَّيجَةُ مُوجَبَةٌ، وَهَذَا أَيْضاً مُسَلَّمٌ وَالثَّانِي: أَنَّهُ مَتَى كَانَتَا كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ ، فَالنَّتيجَةُ كُلِّيَّةٌ، وَهَذَا لَا يَصِحُ عَلَى إِطْلَاقِهِ، بَلْ يُشْتَرَط فِي كُلِّيَّتِهَا مَعَ كُلِّيَّةِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ عُمُومُ وَضْعِ الأَصْغَرِ فِي الصُّغْرَى، أَوْ فِي عَكْسِهَا .
وَإِنَّمَا يُنْتِجُهَا كُلِّيَّتَانِ عَمَّتْ وَوَضْعُ أَصْغَرِ فِي الصُّغْرَى قَدْ عَمَّ أَوْ فِي العَكْسِ ذَاكَ يُدْرَى.
Section on the figures. Since the status of the conclusion varies by quantity and quality, the author pointed to a rule by which its status is known: he said: (96) 'And the conclusion follows the lower of those premises; thus it is known: the lower in quality is negation, and the lower in quantity is particularity.' His saying 'zukina' is passive, meaning 'known.' The meaning is: whenever there is a lower quality or quantity in one or both premises, the conclusion acquires it. So if one of them is negative, the conclusion is negative; if one of them is particular, the conclusion is particular; and if one is particular affirmative and the other universal negative, the conclusion is particular negative. Concerning this, some said: 'Indeed, time follows its lowest part; the conclusion follows the basest, the most worthless.' This explicit meaning in the author's words is accepted, and its implication indicates two things: one, that whenever both premises are affirmative together, the conclusion is affirmative—this too is accepted. The second, that whenever they are both universal, the conclusion is universal—but this is not absolutely valid; rather, for its universality together with the universality of the premises, a condition is required: the universal position of the minor term in the minor premise, or in its converse. And it is only produced by two universal premises that are comprehensive, and the position of the minor in the minor premise has been universal, or in its converse; that is known.
Source pages p. 693

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the matn's statement that the conclusion follows the lower (akhass) of the premises. The commentary first affirms the rule: if one premise is negative, the conclusion is negative; if one is particular, the conclusion is particular; and the combination of particular affirmative and universal negative yields particular negative. This is illustrated with a poetic metaphor comparing it to time. Then it notes that the implication of the matn's phrasing suggests two corollaries: (1) if both premises are affirmative, the conclusion is affirmative—this holds; (2) if both premises are universal, the conclusion is universal—but this is not true without a condition. The commentator corrects this: for the conclusion to be universal when both premises are universal, the minor term must be taken universally in the minor premise (i.e., the minor premise must be a universal statement about all individuals of the subject) or in its converse (if the minor is the predicate in the minor premise, it must be taken universally after conversion). In other words, universal distribution of the minor term is required. This is a standard refinement to avoid invalid moods where both premises are universal but the conclusion is particular (e.g., in the third figure). The passage thus guards against the overgeneralization that any two universal premises guarantee a universal conclusion.

Key Terms
أخس
كلية
وضع الأصغر

B) The minor term must be universally distributed in the minor premise or in its converse
C) The mood must be in the first figure
D) Both premises must be affirmative

Key Takeaway
The conclusion always acquires the lower quality and quantity from the premises; however, a universal conclusion from two universal premises is only valid if the minor term is universally distributed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the following syllogism: 'Every good deed is rewarded by Allah (a); every prayer is a good deed (b); therefore every prayer is rewarded by Allah (c).' Identify the minor term and check whether the condition for a universal conclusion is satisfied. Explain why the conclusion is valid.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Rule: Conclusion Follows the Lower Premise
Author states the rule that conclusion follows the lower premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) of a syllogism's conclusion from the premises, using the rule that the conclusion follows the 'lower' of the premises.

Core Idea
The conclusion inherits the 'lower' quality (negation) and quantity (particularity) from the premises.

When constructing a categorical syllogism, it is crucial to know how the conclusion's form depends on the premises. The classical logicians formulated a concise rule: the conclusion follows the 'lower' (akhass) of the premises. This means that if any premise is negative, the conclusion is negative; if any premise is particular, the conclusion is particular. This lesson unpacks this rule and its application to Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quality of each premise
Determine whether each premise is affirmative or negative. For example, 'Every A is B' is affirmative; 'No A is B' is negative.
2
Identify the quantity of each premise
Determine whether each premise is universal or particular. 'Every A is B' is universal; 'Some A is B' is particular.
3
Apply the 'lower' rule for quality
If any premise is negative, the conclusion is negative. Otherwise, it is affirmative.
Negation is 'lower' than affirmation.
4
Apply the 'lower' rule for quantity
If any premise is particular, the conclusion is particular. Otherwise, it is universal.
Particularity is 'lower' than universality.
5
Combine to get conclusion form
For example, if one premise is particular affirmative and the other universal negative, the conclusion is particular negative.
This matches the source's example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [في الأشكال] وَلَمَّا كَانَ حَالُ النَّتِيجَةِ يَخْتَلِفُ بِالكَمْ وَالكَيْفِ، أَشَارَ المُصَنِّفُ إِلَى ضَابِطٍ يُعْرَفُ بِهِ حَالُهَا ، فَقَالَ: (٩٦) وَتَتْبَعُ النَّتِيجَةُ الأَخَسَّ مِنْ تِلْكَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ هَكَذَا زُكِنْ وَخِسَّةُ الكَيْفِ : السَّلْبُ، وَخِسَّةُ الكَم : الجُزْيَّةُ . وَقَوْلُهُ : «زُكِنْ بِالبِنَاءِ لِلمَفْعُولِ ؛ أَي : عُلِمَ; وَالمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ مَتَى كَانَ فِي إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ أَوْ فِيهِمَا خِسَّةَ اكْتَسَبَتْهَا النَّتِيجَةُ، فَإِذَا كَانَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا سَالِبَةٌ فَالنَّتِيجَةُ سَالِبَةٌ، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا جُزْئِيَّةٌ فَالنَّتِيجَةُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ ، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ وَالأُخْرَى كُلِّيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ فَالنَّتِيجَةُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ، وَفِي ذَلِكَ قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : إِنَّ الزَّمَانَ لَتَابِعِ أَرْذَالَهُ تَبْعَ النَّـتِـيـجَـةِ لِلأَخَسُ الْأَرْذَلِ وَهَذَا المَنْظُوقُ فِي كَلَامِ النَّاظِمِ مُسَلَّمٌ، وَدَلَّ مَنْهُومُهُ عَلَى أَمْرَيْنِ : أَحَدُهُمَا : أَنَّهُ مَتَى كَانَتِ المُقَدِّمَتَانِ مُوجَبَيْنِ مَعاً ، فَالرَّيجَةُ مُوجَبَةٌ، وَهَذَا أَيْضاً مُسَلَّمٌ وَالثَّانِي: أَنَّهُ مَتَى كَانَتَا كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ ، فَالنَّتيجَةُ كُلِّيَّةٌ، وَهَذَا لَا يَصِحُ عَلَى إِطْلَاقِهِ، بَلْ يُشْتَرَط فِي كُلِّيَّتِهَا مَعَ كُلِّيَّةِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ عُمُومُ وَضْعِ الأَصْغَرِ فِي الصُّغْرَى، أَوْ فِي عَكْسِهَا .
Chapter on the Figures. Since the status of the conclusion differs in quantity and quality, the author pointed to a rule by which its status is known, and said: (96) 'And the conclusion follows the lower of those premises, thus it is known that the lower of quality is negation, and the lower of quantity is particularity.' His saying 'zūkina' is in the passive voice: i.e., it is known. The meaning is that whenever in one of the two premises or in both there is a baseness, the conclusion acquires it. So if one of them is negative, the conclusion is negative; if one of them is particular, the conclusion is particular; if one of them is particular affirmative and the other universal negative, the conclusion is particular negative. Regarding that, some said: 'Time follows its worst, the conclusion follows the lowest baser.' This explicit meaning in the poet's words is accepted, and its implied meaning indicates two things: one, that whenever the two premises are both affirmative, the conclusion is affirmative—this is also accepted; and the second, that whenever they are both universal, the conclusion is universal—but this is not absolutely valid; rather, for its universality along with the universality of the two premises, it is required that the minor term have universal placement in the minor premise or in its converse.
Source pages p. 693

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the poem, explaining the rule that the conclusion 'follows the lower' of the premises. The author begins by stating that the conclusion's status (its quality and quantity) varies, and the poet provided a guideline. The commentator interprets the poet's words: 'the lower of quality is negation' means if one premise is negative, the conclusion is negative; 'the lower of quantity is particularity' means if one premise is particular, the conclusion is particular. Additionally, if one premise is particular affirmative and the other universal negative, the conclusion is particular negative. A poetic citation illustrates this. The commentator then extracts two implied implications: when both premises are affirmative, the conclusion is affirmative (accepted); when both are universal, the conclusion is universal (not accepted unconditionally). Here the commentator corrects the poet: for both universal premises to yield a universal conclusion, the minor term must have 'universal placement' (distribution) in the minor premise or in its converse. This condition is critical because without it, two universal premises may yield a particular conclusion, as seen in some syllogistic figures.

Key Takeaway
The conclusion's quality and quantity are determined by the 'lower' of the premises: negation is lower than affirmation, particularity is lower than universality.

The Syllogism

Condition for Universal Conclusion: Minor Term Distribution
Author adds condition for universality from both universal premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to check whether a universal conclusion follows from two universal premises by verifying that the minor term is distributed (universally placed) in the minor premise or its converse.

Core Idea
Two universal premises do not guarantee a universal conclusion unless the minor term is universally placed.

The rule 'conclusion follows the lower premise' seems to suggest that if both premises are universal, the conclusion is universal. However, the commentator corrects this: it is not absolutely true. A universal conclusion requires that the minor term have universal placement (i.e., be distributed) in the minor premise or in its converse. This lesson explores this condition and its importance in syllogistic reasoning within Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor term
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion. For a conclusion 'All S are P', the minor term is S.
2
Locate the minor term in the minor premise
Find the proposition that contains the minor term (usually the second premise). Check if the minor term is the subject or predicate.
3
Check distribution in the minor premise
A term is distributed if it is the subject of a universal proposition or the predicate of a universal negative. For a universal conclusion, the minor term must be distributed in the minor premise.
4
If not distributed, check the converse
The condition can also be satisfied if the minor term is distributed in the converse of the minor premise (i.e., after converting the premise).
5
Determine conclusion universality
Only if the minor term is distributed in either the minor premise or its converse can the conclusion be universal. Otherwise, it must be particular.
This corrects the assumption that two universal premises always yield a universal conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [في الأشكال] وَلَمَّا كَانَ حَالُ النَّتِيجَةِ يَخْتَلِفُ بِالكَمْ وَالكَيْفِ، أَشَارَ المُصَنِّفُ إِلَى ضَابِطٍ يُعْرَفُ بِهِ حَالُهَا ، فَقَالَ: (٩٦) وَتَتْبَعُ النَّتِيجَةُ الأَخَسَّ مِنْ تِلْكَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ هَكَذَا زُكِنْ وَخِسَّةُ الكَيْفِ : السَّلْبُ، وَخِسَّةُ الكَم : الجُزْيَّةُ . وَقَوْلُهُ : «زُكِنْ بِالبِنَاءِ لِلمَفْعُولِ ؛ أَي : عُلِمَ; وَالمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ مَتَى كَانَ فِي إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ أَوْ فِيهِمَا خِسَّةَ اكْتَسَبَتْهَا النَّتِيجَةُ، فَإِذَا كَانَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا سَالِبَةٌ فَالنَّتِيجَةُ سَالِبَةٌ، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا جُزْئِيَّةٌ فَالنَّتِيجَةُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ ، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ وَالأُخْرَى كُلِّيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ فَالنَّتِيجَةُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ سَالِبَةٌ، وَفِي ذَلِكَ قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : إِنَّ الزَّمَانَ لَتَابِعِ أَرْذَالَهُ تَبْعَ النَّـتِـيـجَـةِ لِلأَخَسُ الْأَرْذَلِ وَهَذَا المَنْظُوقُ فِي كَلَامِ النَّاظِمِ مُسَلَّمٌ، وَدَلَّ مَنْهُومُهُ عَلَى أَمْرَيْنِ : أَحَدُهُمَا : أَنَّهُ مَتَى كَانَتِ المُقَدِّمَتَانِ مُوجَبَيْنِ مَعاً ، فَالرَّيجَةُ مُوجَبَةٌ، وَهَذَا أَيْضاً مُسَلَّمٌ وَالثَّانِي: أَنَّهُ مَتَى كَانَتَا كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ ، فَالنَّتيجَةُ كُلِّيَّةٌ، وَهَذَا لَا يَصِحُ عَلَى إِطْلَاقِهِ، بَلْ يُشْتَرَط فِي كُلِّيَّتِهَا مَعَ كُلِّيَّةِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ عُمُومُ وَضْعِ الأَصْغَرِ فِي الصُّغْرَى، أَوْ فِي عَكْسِهَا .

Determining Conclusions from Syllogistic Moods
Enumeration of productive moods and their conclusion types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the type of conclusion (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) for any productive mood in the four figures and count the moods producing universality.

Core Idea
The second figure yields only negatives, the third only particulars; universality requires both premises universal, affirmation requires both affirmative.

Building on the previously established conditions for syllogistic validity, the author now applies them to enumerate which moods in each figure produce which types of conclusions. This systematic classification is essential for the logician to know, at a glance, what conclusion a given set of premises will yield—a skill that directly transfers to constructing proofs in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify figure restrictions
Recognize that the second figure requires a negative premise (so it can only produce negative conclusions), and the third figure lacks distribution of the minor term (so it can only produce particular conclusions).
These restrictions are stated in the passage: second figure 'produces only negation', third 'produces only particularity'.
2
Apply the condition of universality
A universal conclusion requires both premises to be universal. If either premise is particular, the conclusion must be particular.
The text labels this 'sharṭ al-kull' (condition of universality).
3
Apply the condition of affirmation
An affirmative conclusion requires both premises to be affirmative. If one premise is negative, the conclusion must be negative.
This is 'sharṭ al-ījāb' (condition of affirmation).
4
Combine conditions to determine conclusion type
For each mood, check whether the premises satisfy both conditions, only universality, only affirmation, or neither. The passage lists the outcomes for each mood.
For example, first mood of first figure has both universal and affirmative → universal affirmative; second mood of first figure has universal but one negative → universal negative; etc.
5
Count moods producing universality
The passage states that five moods produce universal conclusions: two in the first figure, two in the second, one in the fourth.
This count excludes the third figure, which produces only particular conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So when you know that, test with it the productive moods in what has preceded; you will know what each mood produces, and it becomes clear that the second figure produces only negation because negation is required in one of its premises, and that the third figure produces only particularity because it lacks universal placement of the minor term. And Allah, the Exalted, is the Grantor of success. His saying: 'you will know what each mood produces' means: if the two premises are both universal affirmative, the conclusion is universal affirmative due to the presence of the conditions of universality and affirmation—this is specific to the first mood of the first figure, hence the first figure is the noblest of figures because it produces the two noblest. And if the condition of affirmation is absent because one premise is negative, then it is universal negative—this occurs in the second mood of the first, the first two moods of the second, and the third of the fourth. If the condition of universality is absent, it is particular: with the condition of affirmation it is particular affirmative, as in the third mood of the first, the first, third, and fourth of the third, and the first two of the fourth. And without the condition of affirmation, it is particular negative, as in the fourth of the first. By this you know that the moods producing universality are five: two from the first, two from the second, one from the fourth; and those producing affirmation...

Commentary
This passage applies the previously stated rule to enumerate the productive moods of categorical syllogisms across the four figures. The author begins by instructing the reader to apply the rule to known moods: the second figure produces only negative conclusions because it requires a negative premise; the third figure produces only particular conclusions because the minor term is not distributed. The commentator then expands: when both premises are universal affirmative, the conclusion is universal affirmative—this occurs only in the first mood of the first figure, making the first figure the noblest. When only the condition of universality is present but one premise is negative, the conclusion is universal negative—this occurs in the second mood of the first, the first two moods of the second, and the third mood of the fourth. When the condition of universality is absent, the conclusion is particular: if affirmation is present, it is particular affirmative—this occurs in the third mood of the first, the first, third, and fourth moods of the third, and the first two moods of the fourth; if affirmation is absent, it is particular negative—this occurs in the fourth mood of the first. Finally, the author notes that five moods produce universality: two in the first, two in the second, and one in the fourth. This enumeration helps the student identify which moods yield which types of conclusions, which is essential for constructing valid demonstrations.

The Condition of General Placement of the Minor Term (Umum al-Wad')
Definition of umum al-wad' and its effects on figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition of umum al-wad' to determine which syllogistic moods produce universal conclusions and which produce particular or negative conclusions.

Core Idea
Umum al-wad' means the minor term is subject in a universal minor premise, enabling universal conclusions.

The concept of 'general placement' (umum al-wad') is a key condition for determining whether a categorical syllogism yields a universal conclusion. This passage defines the condition and shows how it applies differently across the four figures. Understanding this condition is essential for correctly evaluating the productivity of syllogistic moods in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined term
The passage defines 'عموم وضع الأصغر' (generality of placement of the minor term). Locate this phrase and note that it refers to a condition on the minor premise.
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion.
2
Extract the condition
The condition has two parts: the minor premise must be universal (كُلِّيَّة), and the minor term must be the subject (مَوْضُوع) in it, meaning the middle term is predicated of every individual of the minor.
If the minor is predicate, the condition fails.
3
Apply to the four figures
Check which figures satisfy the condition: In the first and second figures, the minor is subject; in the third and fourth, it is predicate. Thus umum al-wad' holds automatically only in the first and second.
This explains why third and fourth figures cannot produce universal conclusions directly.
4
Consider the converse of the minor premise
In the fourth figure, if the minor premise is a universal negative, its converse is identical (since universal negative converts to itself). After conversion, the minor term becomes subject, so umum al-wad' can be achieved indirectly.
This allows some fourth-figure moods to produce universal conclusions.
5
Derive implications for the figures
The passage concludes: the second figure produces only negative conclusions (because one premise must be negative), and the third figure produces only particular conclusions (because it lacks umum al-wad' for the minor).
These are fundamental rules in Aristotelian syllogistic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَعْنَى عُمُومُ وَضْعِهِ فِيهَا : أَنْ يَكُونَ مَحْكُومَاً عَلَى جَمِيعِ أَفْرَادِهِ بِالْأَوْسَطِ، وَذَلِكَ بِأَنْ تَكُونَ الصُّغْرَى كُلِّيَّةٌ، وَيَكُونَ هُوَ مَوْضُوعاً فِيهَا ؛ كَمَا فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ وَالثَّانِي، لَا مَحْمُولاً كَمَا فِي الثَّالِثِ وَالرَّابِعِ . وَعُمُومُ الوَضْعِ فِي عَكْسِهَا يَكُونُ فِي بَعْضِ ضُرُوبِ الرَّابِعِ، وَذَلِكَ إِذَا كَانَتِ الصُّغْرَى كُلِّيَّةً سَالِبَةٌ ، فَإِنَّهَا تَنْعَكِسُ كَنَفْسِهَا ، فَيَصِيرُ الأَصْغَرُ عَامَّ الوَضْعَ فِي عَكْسِهَا ، فَإِذَا عَرَفْتَ ذَلِكَ فَاخْتَبِرْ بِهِ الضُّرُوبَ المُنْتِجَةَ فِيمَا تَقَدَّمَ، تَعْرِفْ مَا يُنْتِجُةَ كُلُّ ضَرْبٍ مِنْهَا ، وَيَتَبَيَّنُ أَنَّ الشَّكْلَ الثَّانِي لَا يُنْتِجُ إِلَّا السَّلْبَ؛ لِاشْتِرَاطِ السَّلْبِ فِي إِحْدَى مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ، وَأَنَّ الثَّالِثَ لَا يُنْتِجُ إِلَّا الجُزْيَّةَ؛ لِخُلُوِّهِ عَنْ عُمُومِ وَضْعِ الأَصْغَرِ، وَاللهُ تَعَالَى المُوَفِّقُ
And the meaning of the generality of its placement (the minor term) in it (the minor premise) is that it be predicated over all its individuals by the middle term. That is by the minor premise being universal and it (the minor) being subject in it, as in the first and second figures, not predicate as in the third and fourth. And generality of placement in its converse occurs in some moods of the fourth, that is when the minor premise is universal negative, for it converts as itself, so the minor becomes general of placement in its converse. So if you know that, then test by it the productive moods in what preceded, you will know what each mood produces, and it becomes clear that the second figure produces only negative, due to the condition of negation in one of its premises, and that the third produces only particular, due to its lack of generality of placement of the minor. And Allah the Exalted is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 694

Commentary
This passage is the matn defining the concept of 'عموم وضع الأصغر' (generality of placement of the minor term) in a syllogism. It states that for the minor term to be 'general in placement' means that the minor premise predicates something (the middle term) over every individual of the minor term. This is achieved when the minor premise is universal and the minor term is the subject in it. This condition holds in the first and second figures, where the minor is subject; in the third and fourth, the minor is predicate in the minor premise. The passage then discusses the converse of the minor premise: if the minor premise is a universal negative, it converts to itself, so in the fourth figure, after conversion, the minor becomes general in placement. This allows some moods of the fourth to produce universal conclusions. Finally, the passage notes that the second figure produces only negative conclusions because it requires a negative premise, and the third figure produces only particular conclusions because the minor term lacks general placement. This definition is crucial for understanding which moods yield universal vs. particular conclusions.

Key Terms
عموم الوضع
الأصغر
الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which figures is the minor term automatically 'general in placement' (عام الوضع) in the original syllogism?
A) First and second figures
B) First and third figures
C) Second and fourth figures
D) Third and fourth figures

Key Takeaway
The condition of umum al-wad' (minor term being subject in a universal minor premise) is the key to determining which syllogistic moods yield universal conclusions and explains why the third figure only yields particular conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the four figures satisfy umum al-wad' directly and which do not. For each figure, give an example of a valid mood and state whether its conclusion is universal or particular based on the condition.
?
Examine the fourth-figure mood Bramantip (AAI): the minor premise is 'All B are A' (universal affirmative) where B is the minor term. Does this mood have umum al-wad'? Why or why not? What kind of conclusion does it produce?

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Determining Conclusion Type from Premises
Explains how to derive conclusion type from premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the quantity and quality of a categorical syllogism's conclusion based on its premises.

Core Idea
Conclusion mirrors weakest premise: universal only if both universal, affirmative only if both affirmative.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Bannani's matn provides a systematic method to find the conclusion of any categorical syllogism by checking the 'conditions' of universality (ضابط الكل) and affirmation (ضابط الإيجاب). Rather than memorizing all 19 valid moods, you can deduce the conclusion type from the premises in two steps.

Guided Path
1
Identify premise quantity
Look at each premise: is it universal (كلّي) or particular (جزئي)? Both may be universal, one universal one particular, or both particular.
Universal premises include all members of a class (e.g., 'every intoxicant is forbidden').
2
Identify premise quality
Check each premise: is it affirmative (موجبة) or negative (سالبة)? Both may be affirmative, one affirmative one negative, or both negative.
Affirmative premises assert inclusion, negative premises assert exclusion.
3
Apply the dabit al-kull (condition of universality)
If both premises are universal, then the conclusion is universal. Otherwise (if either premise is particular), the conclusion is particular.
This reflects the rule that the conclusion follows the weaker quantity – particular is weaker than universal.
4
Apply the dabit al-ijab (condition of affirmation)
If both premises are affirmative, then the conclusion is affirmative. Otherwise (if at least one premise is negative), the conclusion is negative.
This reflects the rule that a negative premise makes the conclusion negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي : فإن كانتِ المقدِّمتان كلِّيَّتين موجبتين معاً، كانت النتيجة كلية موجبة؛ لوجود شرطي الكلِّ والإيجاب، وذلك مخصوص بالضَّرب الأوَّلِ مِنَ الشَّكل الأوَّل، لذا كان الأوّل أشرف الأشكال لإنتاجه الأشرفين . وإن لم يوجد ضابط الإيجاب لكون إحدى المقدِّمتين سالبة كانت كلِّيَّة سالبة، وذلك في الضَّرب الثَّاني مِنَ الأوَّل، والضَّربين الأولين مِنَ الثَّاني والثَّالث مِنَ الرَّابع. وإن لم يوجد ضابط الكل كانت جزئيَّةٌ، أمَّا مع ضابط الإيجاب فتكون جزئية موجبة كما في الضَّرب الثَّالث مِنَ الأوَّل، والأوّل والثَّالث والرابع مِنَ الثَّالث، والأوَّلين مِنَ الرَّابع . وأما مع غير ضابط الإيجاب، فتكون جزئية سالبة كما في رابع الأوَّل، وبهذا تعلم أنَّ المنتج للكلِّيَّة خمسة أضرب اثنان مِنَ الأوَّل، واثنان مِنَ الثَّاني، وواحدٌ مِنَ الرابع، والمنتج للإيجاب سبعة : اثنان من الأول، وثلاثة من الثالث، واثنان من الرابع، وأنَّ الأول مخصوص بإنتاج الإيجاب الكلي، والثاني مخصوص بأنَّه لا ينتج إلا السلب، والثالث بأنَّه لا ينتج إلا الجزء.
Meaning: If both premises are universal and affirmative together, then the conclusion is universal affirmative, due to the presence of the conditions of universality and affirmation. That is exclusive to the first mood of the first figure, thus the first figure is the noblest of figures because it produces the two noblest (universal affirmative). And if the condition of affirmation is absent because one of the premises is negative, then it is universal negative. That occurs in the second mood of the first, the first two moods of the second, and the third mood of the fourth. And if the condition of universality is absent, then it is particular. In the presence of the condition of affirmation, it is particular affirmative, as in the third mood of the first, the first, third, and fourth of the third, and the first two of the fourth. And in the absence of the condition of affirmation, it is particular negative, as in the fourth of the first. And by this you know that the moods productive of universality are five: two from the first, two from the second, and one from the fourth. And the moods productive of affirmation are seven: two from the first, three from the third, and two from the fourth. And that the first is specified for producing universal affirmative, the second for producing only negative, and the third for producing only particular.
Source pages p. 694

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn's instruction to test the productive moods. It provides a systematic method to determine the conclusion of any mood based on the quantity and quality of its premises. The commentator explains the four possible combinations of universality and affirmation. When both premises are universal affirmative, the conclusion must be universal affirmative; this only occurs in mood 1 of figure 1. When both are universal but one is negative, the conclusion is universal negative; this occurs in mood 2 of figure 1, moods 1 and 2 of figure 2, and mood 3 of figure 4. When the premises are not both universal, the conclusion is particular. If the premises are both affirmative (but not both universal), the conclusion is particular affirmative; otherwise, if there is a negative premise, the conclusion is particular negative. The commentator then counts the total productive moods for universal conclusions (5) and for affirmative conclusions (7), and notes the special characteristics of each figure: first produces universal affirmative, second produces only negative, third produces only particular. This enumeration helps the student memorize the valid moods.

Key Terms
ضابط الكل
ضابط الإيجاب
المنتج للإيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which mood of the first figure produces a universal negative conclusion?
A) First mood (Barbara)
B) Second mood (Celarent)
C) Third mood (Darii)
D) Fourth mood (Ferio)

Key Takeaway
The quantity and quality of the conclusion in a categorical syllogism are determined by the 'dabit al-kull' (both premises universal) and 'dabit al-ijab' (both premises affirmative) – if either condition fails, the conclusion adopts the weaker property.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the premises 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (universal affirmative) and 'Nabidh is an intoxicant' (universal affirmative), use the dabit al-kull and dabit al-ijab to state the conclusion type. Then change one premise to 'Some intoxicants are forbidden' (particular affirmative) and determine the new conclusion type.
?
Identify the figure and mood of the syllogism: 'No forbidden thing is beneficial' (universal negative), 'Some wine is forbidden' (particular affirmative). Apply the rules to determine if the conclusion is universal or particular, affirmative or negative, and whether the syllogism is valid.
